10084{_}893{_}He whined, and Gray Wolf thrust her warm muzzle against his neck.
10084{_}894{_}Something told them both that they were outcasts, that the plains, and the moon, and the stars were against them now, and they slunk into the shelter and the gloom of the forest.
10084{_}895{_}Kazan could not go far.
10084{_}896{_}He could still smell the camp when he lay down.
10084{_}897{_}Gray Wolf snuggled close to him.
10084{_}898{_}Gently she soothed with her soft tongue Kazan's bleeding wounds.
10084{_}899{_}And Kazan, lifting his head, whined softly to the stars.
10084{_}900{_}CHAPTER VI  JOAN   On the edge of the cedar and spruce forest old Pierre Radisson built the fire.
10084{_}901{_}He was bleeding from a dozen wounds, where the fangs of the wolves had reached to his flesh, and he felt in his breast that old and terrible pain, of which no one knew the meaning but himself.
10084{_}902{_}He dragged in log after log, piled them on the fire until the flames leaped tip to the crisping needles of the limbs above, and heaped a supply close at hand for use later in the night.
10084{_}903{_}From the sledge Joan watched him, still wild-eyed and fearful, still trembling.
10084{_}904{_}She was holding her baby close to her breast.
10084{_}905{_}Her long heavy hair smothered her shoulders and arms in a dark lustrous veil that glistened and rippled in the firelight when she moved.
10084{_}906{_}Her young face was scarcely a woman's to-night, though she was a mother.
10084{_}907{_}She looked like a child.
10084{_}908{_}Old Pierre laughed as he threw down the last armful of fuel, and stood breathing hard.
10084{_}909{_}"It was close, _ma cheri_" he panted through his white beard.
10084{_}910{_}"We were nearer to death out there on the plain than we will ever be again, I hope.
10084{_}911{_}But we are comfortable now, and warm.
10084{_}912{_}Eh?
10084{_}913{_}You are no longer afraid?"
10084{_}914{_}He sat down beside his daughter, and gently pulled back the soft fur that enveloped the bundle she held in her arms.
10084{_}915{_}He could see one pink cheek of baby Joan.
10084{_}916{_}The eyes of Joan, the mother, were like stars.
10084{_}917{_}"It was the baby who saved us," she whispered.
10084{_}918{_}"The dogs were being torn to pieces by the wolves, and I saw them leaping upon you, when one of them sprang to the sledge.
10084{_}919{_}At first I thought it was one of the dogs.
10084{_}920{_}But it was a wolf.
10084{_}921{_}He tore once at us, and the bearskin saved us.
10084{_}922{_}He was almost at my throat when baby cried, and then he stood there, his red eyes a foot from us, and I could have sworn again that he was a dog.
10084{_}923{_}In an instant he turned, and was fighting the wolves.
10084{_}924{_}I saw him leap upon one that was almost at your throat."
10084{_}925{_}"He _was_ a dog," said old Pierre, holding out his hands to the warmth.
10084{_}926{_}"They often wander away from the posts, and join the wolves.
10084{_}927{_}I have had dogs do that.
10084{_}928{_}_Ma cheri_, a dog is a dog all his life.
10084{_}929{_}Kicks, abuse, even the wolves can not change him--for long.
10084{_}930{_}He was one of the pack.
10084{_}931{_}He came with them--to kill.
10084{_}932{_}But when he found _us_--"  "He fought for us," breathed the girl.
10084{_}933{_}She gave him the bundle, and stood up, straight and tall and slim in the firelight.
10084{_}934{_}"He fought for us--and he was terribly hurt," she said.
10084{_}935{_}"I saw him drag himself away.
10084{_}936{_}Father, if he is out there--dying--"  Pierre Radisson stood up.
10084{_}937{_}He coughed in a shuddering way, trying to stifle the sound under his beard.
10084{_}938{_}The fleck of crimson that came to his lips with the cough Joan did not see.
10084{_}939{_}She had seen nothing of it during the six days they had been traveling up from the edge of civilization.
10084{_}940{_}Because of that cough, and the stain that came with it, Pierre had made more than ordinary haste.
10084{_}941{_}"I have been thinking of that," he said.
10084{_}942{_}"He was badly hurt, and I do not think he went far.
10084{_}943{_}Here--take little Joan and sit close to the fire until I come back."
10084{_}944{_}The moon and the stars were brilliant in the sky when he went out in the plain.
10084{_}945{_}A short distance from the edge of the timber-line he stood for a moment upon the spot where the wolves had overtaken them an hour before.
10084{_}946{_}Not one of his four dogs had lived.
10084{_}947{_}The snow was red with their blood, and their bodies lay stiff where they had fallen under the pack.
10084{_}948{_}Pierre shuddered as he looked at them.
10084{_}949{_}If the wolves had not turned their first mad attack upon the dogs, what would have become of himself, Joan and the baby?
10084{_}950{_}He turned away, with another of those hollow coughs that brought the blood to his lips.
10084{_}951{_}A few yards to one side he found in the snow the trail of the strange dog that had come with the wolves, and had turned against them in that moment when all seemed lost.
10084{_}952{_}It was not a clean running trail.
10084{_}953{_}It was more of a furrow in the snow, and Pierre Radisson followed it, expecting to find the dog dead at the end of it.
10084{_}954{_}In the sheltered spot to which he had dragged himself in the edge of the forest Kazan lay for a long time after the fight, alert and watchful.
10084{_}955{_}He felt no very great pain.
10084{_}956{_}But he had lost the power to stand upon his legs.
10084{_}957{_}His flanks seemed paralyzed.
10084{_}958{_}Gray Wolf crouched close at his side, sniffing the air.
10084{_}959{_}They could smell the camp, and Kazan could detect the two things that were there--_man_ and _woman_.
10084{_}960{_}He knew that the girl was there, where he could see the glow of the firelight through the spruce and the cedars.
10084{_}961{_}He wanted to go to her.
10084{_}962{_}He wanted to drag himself close in to the fire, and take Gray Wolf with him, and listen to her voice, and feel the touch of her hand.
10084{_}963{_}But the man was there, and to him man had always meant the club, the whip, pain, death.
10084{_}964{_}Gray Wolf crouched close to his side, and whined softly as she urged Kazan to flee deeper with her into the forest.
10084{_}965{_}At last she understood that he could not move, and she ran nervously out into the plain, and back again, until her footprints were thick in the trail she made.
10084{_}966{_}The instincts of matehood were strong in her.
10084{_}967{_}It was she who first saw Pierre Radisson coming over their trail, and she ran swiftly back to Kazan and gave the warning.
10084{_}968{_}Then Kazan caught the scent, and he saw the shadowy figure coming through the starlight.
10084{_}969{_}He tried to drag himself back, but he could move only by inches.
10084{_}970{_}The man came rapidly nearer.
10084{_}971{_}Kazan caught the glisten of the rifle in his hand.
10084{_}972{_}He heard his hollow cough, and the tread of his feet in the snow.
10084{_}973{_}Gray Wolf crouched shoulder to shoulder with him, trembling and showing her teeth.
10084{_}974{_}When Pierre had approached within fifty feet of them she slunk back into the deeper shadows of the spruce.
10084{_}975{_}Kazan's fangs were bared menacingly when Pierre stopped and looked down at him.
10084{_}976{_}With an effort he dragged himself to his feet, but fell back into the snow again.
10084{_}977{_}The man leaned his rifle against a sapling and bent over him fearlessly.
10084{_}978{_}With a fierce growl Kazan snapped at his extended hands.
10084{_}979{_}To his surprise the man did not pick up a stick or a club.
10084{_}980{_}He held out his hand again--cautiously--and spoke in a voice new to Kazan.
10084{_}981{_}The dog snapped again, and growled.
10084{_}982{_}The man persisted, talking to him all the time, and once his mittened hand touched Kazan's head, and escaped before the jaws could reach it.
10084{_}983{_}Again and again the man reached out his hand, and three times Kazan felt the touch of it, and there was neither threat nor hurt in it.
10084{_}984{_}At last Pierre turned away and went back over the trail.
10084{_}985{_}When he was out of sight and hearing, Kazan whined, and the crest along his spine flattened.
10084{_}986{_}He looked wistfully toward the glow of the fire.
10084{_}987{_}The man had not hurt him, and the three-quarters of him that was dog wanted to follow.
10084{_}988{_}Gray Wolf came back, and stood with stiffly planted forefeet at his side.
10084{_}989{_}She had never been this near to man before, except when the pack had overtaken the sledge out on the plain.
10084{_}990{_}She could not understand.
10084{_}991{_}Every instinct that was in her warned her that he was the most dangerous of all things, more to be feared than the strongest beasts, the storms, the floods, cold and starvation.
10084{_}992{_}And yet this man had not harmed her mate.
10084{_}993{_}She sniffed at Kazan's back and head, where the mittened hand had touched.
10084{_}994{_}Then she trotted back into the darkness again, for beyond the edge of the forest she once more saw moving life.
10084{_}995{_}The man was returning, and with him was the girl.
10084{_}996{_}Her voice was soft and sweet, and there was about her the breath and sweetness of woman.
10084{_}997{_}The man stood prepared, but not threatening.
10084{_}998{_}"Be careful, Joan," he warned.
10084{_}999{_}She dropped on her knees in the snow, just out of reach.
10084{_}1000{_}"Come, boy--come!"
10084{_}1001{_}she said gently.
10084{_}1002{_}She held out her hand.
10084{_}1003{_}Kazan's muscles twitched.
10084{_}1004{_}He moved an inch--two inches toward her.
10084{_}1005{_}There was the old light in her eyes and face now, the love and gentleness he had known once before, when another woman with shining hair and eyes had come into his life.
10084{_}1006{_}"Come!"
10084{_}1007{_}she whispered as she saw him move, and she bent a little, reached a little farther with her hand, and at last touched his head.
10084{_}1008{_}Pierre knelt beside her.
10084{_}1009{_}He was proffering something, and Kazan smelled meat.
10084{_}1010{_}But it was the girl's hand that made him tremble and shiver, and when she drew back, urging him to follow her, he dragged himself painfully a foot or two through the snow.
10084{_}1011{_}Not until then did the girl see his mangled leg.
10084{_}1012{_}In an instant she had forgotten all caution, and was down close at his side.
10084{_}1013{_}"He can't walk," she cried, a sudden tremble in her voice.
10084{_}1014{_}"Look, _mon pere!_ Here is a terrible cut.
10084{_}1015{_}We must carry him."
10084{_}1016{_}"I guessed that much," replied Radisson.
10084{_}1017{_}"For that reason I brought the blanket.
10084{_}1018{_}_Mon Dieu_, listen to that!"
10084{_}1019{_}From the darkness of the forest there came a low wailing cry.
10084{_}1020{_}Kazan lifted his head and a trembling whine answered in his throat.
10084{_}1021{_}It was Gray Wolf calling to him.
10084{_}1022{_}It was a miracle that Pierre Radisson should put the blanket about Kazan, and carry him in to the camp, without scratch or bite.
10084{_}1023{_}It was this miracle that he achieved, with Joan's arm resting on Kazan's shaggy neck as she held one end of the blanket.
10084{_}1024{_}They laid him down close to the fire, and after a little it was the man again who brought warm water and washed away the blood from the torn leg, and then put something on it that was soft and warm and soothing, and finally bound a cloth about it.
10084{_}1025{_}All this Was strange and new to Kazan.
10084{_}1026{_}Pierre's hand, as well as the girl's, stroked his head.
10084{_}1027{_}It was the man who brought him a gruel of meal and tallow, and urged him to eat, while Joan sat with her chin in her two hands, looking at the dog, and talking to him.
10084{_}1028{_}After this, when he was quite comfortable, and no longer afraid, he heard a strange small cry from the furry bundle on the sledge that brought his head up with a jerk.
10084{_}1029{_}Joan saw the movement, and heard the low answering whimper in his throat.
10084{_}1030{_}She turned quickly to the bundle, talking and cooing to it as she took it in her arms, and then she pulled back the bearskin so that Kazan could see.
10084{_}1031{_}He had never seen a baby before, and Joan held it out before him, so that he could look straight at it and see what a wonderful creature it was.
10084{_}1032{_}Its little pink face stared steadily at Kazan.
10084{_}1033{_}Its tiny fists reached out, and it made queer little sounds at him, and then suddenly it kicked and screamed with delight and laughed.
10084{_}1034{_}At those sounds Kazan's whole body relaxed, and he dragged himself to the girl's feet.
10084{_}1035{_}"See, he likes the baby!"
10084{_}1036{_}she cried.
10084{_}1037{_}"_Mon pere_, we must give him a name.
10084{_}1038{_}What shall it be?"
10084{_}1039{_}"Wait till morning for that," replied the father.
10084{_}1040{_}"It is late, Joan.
10084{_}1041{_}Go into the tent, and sleep.
10084{_}1042{_}We have no dogs now, and will travel slowly.
10084{_}1043{_}So we must start early."
10084{_}1044{_}With her hand on the tent-flap, Joan, turned.
10084{_}1045{_}"He came with the wolves," she said.
10084{_}1046{_}"Let us call him Wolf."
10084{_}1047{_}With one arm she was holding the little Joan.
10084{_}1048{_}The other she stretched out to Kazan.
10084{_}1049{_}"Wolf!
10084{_}1050{_}Wolf!"
10084{_}1051{_}she called softly.
10084{_}1052{_}Kazan's eyes were on her.
10084{_}1053{_}He knew that she was speaking to him, and he drew himself a foot toward her.
10084{_}1054{_}"He knows it already!"
10084{_}1055{_}she cried.
10084{_}1056{_}"Good night, _mon pere_."
10084{_}1057{_}For a long time after she had gone into the tent, old Pierre Radisson sat on the edge of the sledge, facing the fire, with Kazan at his feet.
10084{_}1058{_}Suddenly the silence was broken again by Gray Wolf's lonely howl deep in the forest.
10084{_}1059{_}Kazan lifted his head and whined.
10084{_}1060{_}"She's calling for you, boy," said Pierre understandingly.
10084{_}1061{_}He coughed, and clutched a hand to his breast, where the pain seemed rending him.
10084{_}1062{_}"Frost-bitten lung," he said, speaking straight at Kazan.
10084{_}1063{_}"Got it early in the winter, up at Fond du Lac.
10084{_}1064{_}Hope we'll get home--in time--with the kids."
10084{_}1065{_}In the loneliness and emptiness of the big northern wilderness one falls into the habit of talking to one's self.
10084{_}1066{_}But Kazan's head was alert, and his eyes watchful, so Pierre spoke to him.
10084{_}1067{_}"We've got to get them home, and there's only you and me to do it," he said, twisting his beard.
10084{_}1068{_}Suddenly he clenched his fists.
10084{_}1069{_}His hollow racking cough convulsed him again.
10084{_}1070{_}"Home!"
10084{_}1071{_}he panted, clutching his chest.
10084{_}1072{_}"It's eighty miles straight north--to the Churchill--and I pray to God we'll get there--with the kids--before my lungs give out."
10084{_}1073{_}He rose to his feet, and staggered a little as he walked.
10084{_}1074{_}There was a collar about Kazan's neck, and he chained him to the sledge.
10084{_}1075{_}After that he dragged three or four small logs upon the fire, and went quietly into the tent where Joan and the baby were already asleep.
10084{_}1076{_}Several times that night Kazan heard the distant voice of Gray Wolf calling for him, but something told him that he must not answer it now.
10084{_}1077{_}Toward dawn Gray Wolf came close in to the camp, and for the first time Kazan replied to her.
10084{_}1078{_}His howl awakened the man.
10084{_}1079{_}He came out of the tent, peered for a few moments up at the sky, built up the fire, and began to prepare breakfast.
10084{_}1080{_}He patted Kazan on the head, and gave him a chunk of meat.
10084{_}1081{_}Joan came out a few moments later, leaving the baby asleep in the tent.
10084{_}1082{_}She ran up and kissed Pierre, and then dropped down on her knees beside Kazan, and talked to him almost as he had heard her talk to the baby.
10084{_}1083{_}When she jumped up to help her father, Kazan followed her, and when Joan saw him standing firmly upon his legs she gave a cry of pleasure.
10084{_}1084{_}It was a strange journey that began into the North that day.
10084{_}1085{_}Pierre Radisson emptied the sledge of everything but the tent, blankets, food and the furry nest for baby Joan.
10084{_}1086{_}Then he harnessed himself in the traces and dragged the sledge over the snow.
10084{_}1087{_}He coughed incessantly.
10084{_}1088{_}"It's a cough I've had half the winter," lied Pierre, careful that Joan saw no sign of blood on his lips or beard.
10084{_}1089{_}"I'll keep in the cabin for a week when we get home."
10084{_}1090{_}Even Kazan, with that strange beast knowledge which man, unable to explain, calls instinct, knew that what he said was not the truth.
10084{_}1091{_}Perhaps it was largely because he had heard other men cough like this, and that for generations his sledge-dog ancestors had heard men cough as Radisson coughed--and had learned what followed it.
10084{_}1092{_}More than once he had scented death in tepees and cabins, which he had not entered, and more than once he had sniffed at the mystery of death that was not quite present, but near--just as he had caught at a distance the subtle warning of storm and of fire.
10084{_}1093{_}And that strange thing seemed to be very near to him now, as he followed at the end of his chain behind the sledge.
10084{_}1094{_}It made him restless, and half a dozen times, when the sledge stopped, he sniffed at the bit of humanity buried in the bearskin.
10084{_}1095{_}Each time that he did this Joan was quickly at his side, and twice she patted his scarred and grizzled head until every drop of blood in his body leaped riotously with a joy which his body did not reveal.
10084{_}1096{_}This day the chief thing that he came to understand was that the little creature on the sledge was very precious to the girl who stroked his head and talked to him, and that it was very helpless.
10084{_}1097{_}He learned, too, that Joan was most delighted, and that her voice was softer and thrilled him more deeply, when he paid attention to that little, warm, living thing in the bearskin.
10084{_}1098{_}For a long time after they made camp Pierre Radisson sat beside the fire.
10084{_}1099{_}To-night he did not smoke.
10084{_}1100{_}He stared straight into the flames.
10084{_}1101{_}When at last he rose to go into the tent with the girl and the baby, he bent over Kazan and examined his hurt.
10084{_}1102{_}"You've got to work in the traces to-morrow, boy," he said.
10084{_}1103{_}"We must make the river by to-morrow night.
10084{_}1104{_}If we don't--"  He did not finish.
10084{_}1105{_}He was choking back one of those tearing coughs when the tent-flap dropped behind him.
10084{_}1106{_}Kazan lay stiff and alert, his eyes filled with a strange anxiety.
10084{_}1107{_}He did not like to see Radisson enter the tent, for stronger than ever there hung that oppressive mystery in the air about him, and it seemed to be a part of Pierre.
10084{_}1108{_}Three times that night he heard faithful Gray Wolf calling for him deep in the forest, and each time he answered her.
10084{_}1109{_}Toward dawn she came in close to camp.
10084{_}1110{_}Once he caught the scent of her when she circled around in the wind, and he tugged and whined at the end of his chain, hoping that she would come in and lie down at his side.
10084{_}1111{_}But no sooner had Radisson moved in the tent than Gray Wolf was gone.
10084{_}1112{_}The man's face was thinner, and his eyes were redder this morning.
10084{_}1113{_}His cough was not so loud or so rending.
10084{_}1114{_}It was like a wheeze, as if something had given way inside, and before the girl came out he clutched his hands often at his throat.
10084{_}1115{_}Joan's face whitened when she saw him.
10084{_}1116{_}Anxiety gave way to fear in her eyes.
10084{_}1117{_}Pierre Radisson laughed when she flung her arms about him, and coughed to prove that what he said was true.
10084{_}1118{_}"You see the cough is not so bad, my Joan," he said.
10084{_}1119{_}"It is breaking up.
10084{_}1120{_}You can not have forgotten, _ma cheri_?
10084{_}1121{_}It always leaves one red-eyed and weak."
10084{_}1122{_}It was a cold bleak dark day that followed, and through it Kazan and the man tugged at the fore of the sledge, with Joan following in the trail behind.
10084{_}1123{_}Kazan's wound no longer hurt him.
10084{_}1124{_}He pulled steadily with all his splendid strength, and the man never lashed him once, but patted him with his mittened hand on head and back.
10084{_}1125{_}The day grew steadily darker and in the tops of the trees there was the low moaning of a storm.
10084{_}1126{_}Darkness and the coming of the storm did not drive Pierre Radisson into camp.
10084{_}1127{_}"We must reach the river," he said to himself over and over again.
10084{_}1128{_}"We must reach the river--we must reach the river--" And he steadily urged Kazan on to greater effort, while his own strength at the end of the traces grew less.
10084{_}1129{_}It had begun to storm when Pierre stopped to build a fire at noon.
10084{_}1130{_}The snow fell straight down in a white deluge so thick that it hid the tree trunks fifty yards away.
10084{_}1131{_}Pierre laughed when Joan shivered and snuggled close up to him with the baby in her arms.
10084{_}1132{_}He waited only an hour, and then fastened Kazan in the traces again, and buckled the straps once more about his own waist.
10084{_}1133{_}In the silent gloom that was almost night Pierre carried his compass in his hand, and at last, late in the afternoon, they came to a break in the timber-line, and ahead of them lay a plain, across which Radisson pointed an exultant hand.
10084{_}1134{_}"There's the river, Joan," he said, his voice faint and husky.
10084{_}1135{_}"We can camp here now and wait for the storm to pass."
10084{_}1136{_}Under a thick clump of spruce he put up the tent, and then began gathering fire-wood.
10084{_}1137{_}Joan helped him.
10084{_}1138{_}As soon as they had boiled coffee and eaten a supper of meat and toasted biscuits, Joan went into the tent and dropped exhausted on her thick bed of balsam boughs, wrapping herself and the baby up close in the skins and blankets.
10084{_}1139{_}To-night she had no word for Kazan.
10084{_}1140{_}And Pierre was glad that she was too tired to sit beside the fire and talk.
10084{_}1141{_}And yet--  Kazan's alert eyes saw Pierre start suddenly.
10084{_}1142{_}He rose from his seat on the sledge and went to the tent.
10084{_}1143{_}He drew back the flap and thrust in his head and shoulders.
10084{_}1144{_}"Asleep, Joan?"
10084{_}1145{_}he asked.
10084{_}1146{_}"Almost, father.
10084{_}1147{_}Won't you please come--soon?"
10084{_}1148{_}"After I smoke," he said.
10084{_}1149{_}"Are you comfortable?"
10084{_}1150{_}"Yes, I'm so tired--and--sleepy--"  Pierre laughed softly.
10084{_}1151{_}In the darkness he was gripping at his throat.
10084{_}1152{_}"We're almost home, Joan.
10084{_}1153{_}That is our river out there--the Little Beaver.
10084{_}1154{_}If I should run away and leave you to-night you could follow it right to our cabin.
10084{_}1155{_}It's only forty miles.
10084{_}1156{_}Do you hear?"
10084{_}1157{_}"Yes--I know--"  "Forty miles--straight down the river.
10084{_}1158{_}You couldn't lose yourself, Joan.
10084{_}1159{_}Only you'd have to be careful of air-holes in the ice."
10084{_}1160{_}"Won't you come to bed, father?
10084{_}1161{_}You're tired--and almost sick."
10084{_}1162{_}"Yes--after I smoke," he repeated.
10084{_}1163{_}"Joan, will you keep reminding me to-morrow of the air-holes?
10084{_}1164{_}I might forget.
10084{_}1165{_}You can always tell them, for the snow and the crust over them are whiter than that on the rest of the ice, and like a sponge.
10084{_}1166{_}Will you remember--the airholes--"  "Yes-s-s-s--"  Pierre dropped the tent-flap and returned to the fire.
10084{_}1167{_}He staggered as he walked.
10084{_}1168{_}"Good night, boy," he said.
10084{_}1169{_}"Guess I'd better go in with the kids.
10084{_}1170{_}Two days more--forty miles--two days--"  Kazan watched him as he entered the tent.
10084{_}1171{_}He laid his weight against the end of his chain until the collar shut off his wind.
10084{_}1172{_}His legs and back twitched.
10084{_}1173{_}In that tent where Radisson had gone were Joan and the baby.
10084{_}1174{_}He knew that Pierre would not hurt them, but he knew also that with Pierre Radisson something terrible and impending was hovering very near to them.
10084{_}1175{_}He wanted the man outside--by the fire--where he could lie still, and watch him.
10084{_}1176{_}In the tent there was silence.
10084{_}1177{_}Nearer to him than before came Gray Wolf's cry.
10084{_}1178{_}Each night she was calling earlier, and coming closer to the camp.
10084{_}1179{_}He wanted her very near to him to-night, but he did not even whine in response.
10084{_}1180{_}He dared not break that strange silence in the tent.
10084{_}1181{_}He lay still for a long time, tired and lame from the day's journey, but sleepless.
10084{_}1182{_}The fire burned lower; the wind in the tree-tops died away; and the thick gray clouds rolled like a massive curtain from under the skies.
10084{_}1183{_}The stars began to glow white and metallic, and from far in the North there came faintly a crisping moaning sound, like steel sleigh-runners running over frosty snow--the mysterious monotone of the Northern Lights.
10084{_}1184{_}After that it grew steadily and swiftly colder.
10084{_}1185{_}To-night Gray Wolf did not compass herself by the direction of the wind.
10084{_}1186{_}She followed like a sneaking shadow over the trail Pierre Radisson had made, and when Kazan heard her again, long after midnight, he lay with, his head erect, and his body rigid, save for a curious twitching of his muscles.
10084{_}1187{_}There was a new note in Gray Wolf's voice, a wailing note in which there was more than the mate-call.
10084{_}1188{_}It was The Message.
10084{_}1189{_}And at the sound of it Kazan rose from out of his silence and his fear, and with his head turned straight up to the sky he howled as the wild dogs of the North howl before the tepees of masters who are newly dead.
10084{_}1190{_}Pierre Radisson was dead.
10084{_}1191{_}CHAPTER VII  OUT OF THE BLIZZARD   It was dawn when the baby snuggled close to Joan's warm breast and awakened her with its cry of hunger.
10084{_}1192{_}She opened her eyes, brushed back the thick hair from her face, and could see where the shadowy form of her father was lying at the other side of the tent.
10084{_}1193{_}He was very quiet, and she was pleased that he was still sleeping.
10084{_}1194{_}She knew that the day before he had been very near to exhaustion, and so for half an hour longer she lay quiet, cooing softly to the baby Joan.
10084{_}1195{_}Then she arose cautiously, tucked the baby in the warm blankets and furs, put on her heavier garments, and went outside.
10084{_}1196{_}By this time it was broad day, and she breathed a sigh of relief when she saw that the storm had passed.
10084{_}1197{_}It was bitterly cold.
10084{_}1198{_}It seemed to her that she had never known it to be so cold in all her life.
10084{_}1199{_}The fire was completely out.
10084{_}1200{_}Kazan was huddled in a round ball, his nose tucked under his body.
10084{_}1201{_}He raised his head, shivering, as Joan came out.
10084{_}1202{_}With her heavily moccasined foot Joan scattered the ashes and charred sticks where the fire had been.
10084{_}1203{_}There was not a spark left.
10084{_}1204{_}In returning to the tent she stopped for a moment beside Kazan, and patted his shaggy head.
10084{_}1205{_}"Poor Wolf!"
10084{_}1206{_}she said.
10084{_}1207{_}"I wish I had given you one of the bearskins!"
10084{_}1208{_}She threw back the tent-flap and entered.
10084{_}1209{_}For the first time she saw her father's face in the light--and outside, Kazan heard the terrible moaning cry that broke from her lips.
10084{_}1210{_}No one could have looked at Pierre Radisson's face once--and not have understood.
10084{_}1211{_}After that one agonizing cry, Joan flung herself upon her father's breast, sobbing so softly that even Kazan's sharp ears heard no sound.
10084{_}1212{_}She remained there in her grief until every vital energy of womanhood and motherhood in her girlish body was roused to action by the wailing cry of baby Joan.
10084{_}1213{_}Then she sprang to her feet and ran out through the tent opening.
10084{_}1214{_}Kazan tugged at the end of his chain to meet her, but she saw nothing of him now.
10084{_}1215{_}The terror of the wilderness is greater than that of death, and in an instant it had fallen upon Joan.
10084{_}1216{_}It was not because of fear for herself.
10084{_}1217{_}It was the baby.
10084{_}1218{_}The wailing cries from the tent pierced her like knife-thrusts.
10084{_}1219{_}And then, all at once, there came to her what old Pierre had said the night before--his words about the river, the air-holes, the home forty miles away.
10084{_}1220{_}"_You couldn't lose yourself, Joan_" He had guessed what might happen.
10084{_}1221{_}She bundled the baby deep in the furs and returned to the fire-bed.
10084{_}1222{_}Her one thought now was that they must have fire.
10084{_}1223{_}She made a little pile of birch-bark, covered it with half-burned bits of wood, and went into the tent for the matches.
10084{_}1224{_}Pierre Radisson carried them in a water-proof box in a pocket of his bearskin coat.
10084{_}1225{_}She sobbed as she kneeled beside him again, and obtained the box.
10084{_}1226{_}As the fire flared up she added other bits of wood, and then some of the larger pieces that Pierre had dragged into camp.
10084{_}1227{_}The fire gave her courage.
10084{_}1228{_}Forty miles--and the river led to their home!
10084{_}1229{_}She must make that, with the baby and Wolf.
10084{_}1230{_}For the first time she turned to him, and spoke his name as she put her hand on his head.
10084{_}1231{_}After that she gave him a chunk of meat which she thawed out over the fire, and melted the snow for tea.
10084{_}1232{_}She was not hungry, but she recalled how her father had made her eat four or five times a day, so she forced herself to make a breakfast of a biscuit, a shred of meat and as much hot tea as she could drink.
10084{_}1233{_}The terrible hour she dreaded followed that.
10084{_}1234{_}She wrapped blankets closely about her father's body, and tied them with babiche cord.
10084{_}1235{_}After that she piled all the furs and blankets that remained on the sledge close to the fire, and snuggled baby Joan deep down in them.
10084{_}1236{_}Pulling down the tent was a task.
10084{_}1237{_}The ropes were stiff and frozen, and when she had finished, one of her hands was bleeding.
10084{_}1238{_}She piled the tent on the sledge, and then, half, covering her face, turned and looked back.
10084{_}1239{_}Pierre Radisson lay on his balsam bed, with nothing over him now but the gray sky and the spruce-tops.
10084{_}1240{_}Kazan stood stiff-legged and sniffed the air.
10084{_}1241{_}His spine bristled when Joan went back slowly and kneeled beside the blanket-wrapped object.
10084{_}1242{_}When she returned to him her face was white and tense, and now there was a strange and terrible look in her eyes as she stared out across the barren.
10084{_}1243{_}She put him in the traces, and fastened about her slender waist the strap that Pierre had used.
10084{_}1244{_}Thus they struck out for the river, floundering knee-deep in the freshly fallen and drifted snow.
10084{_}1245{_}Half-way Joan stumbled in a drift and fell, her loose hair flying in a shimmering veil over the snow.
10084{_}1246{_}With a mighty pull Kazan was at her side, and his cold muzzle touched her face as she drew herself to her feet.
10084{_}1247{_}For a moment Joan took his shaggy head between her two hands.
10084{_}1248{_}"Wolf!"
10084{_}1249{_}she moaned.
10084{_}1250{_}"Oh, Wolf!"
10084{_}1251{_}She went on, her breath coming pantingly now, even from her brief exertion.
10084{_}1252{_}The snow was not so deep on the ice of the river.
10084{_}1253{_}But a wind was rising.
10084{_}1254{_}It came from the north and east, straight in her face, and Joan bowed her head as she pulled with Kazan.
10084{_}1255{_}Half a mile down the river she stopped, and no longer could she repress the hopelessness that rose to her lips in a sobbing choking cry.
10084{_}1256{_}Forty miles!
10084{_}1257{_}She clutched her hands at her breast, and stood breathing like one who had been beaten, her back to the wind.
10084{_}1258{_}The baby was quiet.
10084{_}1259{_}Joan went back and peered down under the furs, and what she saw there spurred her on again almost fiercely.
10084{_}1260{_}Twice she stumbled to her knees in the drifts during the next quarter of a mile.
10084{_}1261{_}After that there was a stretch of wind-swept ice, and Kazan pulled the sledge alone.
10084{_}1262{_}Joan walked at his side.
10084{_}1263{_}There was a pain in her chest.
10084{_}1264{_}A thousand needles seemed pricking her face, and suddenly she remembered the thermometer.
10084{_}1265{_}She exposed it for a time on the top of the tent.
10084{_}1266{_}When she looked at it a few minutes later it was thirty degrees below zero.
10084{_}1267{_}Forty miles!
10084{_}1268{_}And her father had told her that she could make it--and could not lose herself!
10084{_}1269{_}But she did not know that even her father would have been afraid to face the north that day, with the temperature at thirty below, and a moaning wind bringing the first warning of a blizzard.
10084{_}1270{_}The timber was far behind her now.
10084{_}1271{_}Ahead there was nothing but the pitiless barren, and the timber beyond that was hidden by the gray gloom of the day.
10084{_}1272{_}If there had been trees, Joan's heart would not have choked so with terror.
10084{_}1273{_}But there was nothing--nothing but that gray ghostly gloom, with the rim of the sky touching the earth a mile away.
10084{_}1274{_}The snow grew heavy under her feet again.
10084{_}1275{_}Always she was watching for those treacherous, frost-coated traps in the ice her father had spoken of.
10084{_}1276{_}But she found now that all the ice and snow looked alike to her, and that there was a growing pain back of her eyes.
10084{_}1277{_}It was the intense cold.
10084{_}1278{_}The river widened into a small lake, and here the wind struck her in the face with such force that her weight was taken from the strap, and Kazan dragged the sledge alone.
10084{_}1279{_}A few inches of snow impeded her as much as a foot had done before.
10084{_}1280{_}Little by little she dropped back.
10084{_}1281{_}Kazan forged to her side, every ounce of his magnificent strength in the traces.
10084{_}1282{_}By the time they were on the river channel again, Joan was at the back of the sledge, following in the trail made by Kazan.
10084{_}1283{_}She was powerless to help him.
10084{_}1284{_}She felt more and more the leaden weight of her legs.
10084{_}1285{_}There was but one hope--and that was the forest.
10084{_}1286{_}If they did not reach it soon, within half an hour, she would be able to go no farther.
10084{_}1287{_}Over and over again she moaned a prayer for her baby as she struggled on.
10084{_}1288{_}She fell in the snow-drifts.
10084{_}1289{_}Kazan and the sledge became only a dark blotch to her.
10084{_}1290{_}And then, all at once, she saw that they were leaving her.
10084{_}1291{_}They were not more than twenty feet ahead of her--but the blotch seemed to be a vast distance away.
10084{_}1292{_}Every bit of life and strength in her body was now bent upon reaching the sledge--and baby Joan.
10084{_}1293{_}It seemed an interminable time before she gained.
10084{_}1294{_}With the sledge only six feet ahead of her she struggled for what seemed to her to be an hour before she could reach out and touch it.
10084{_}1295{_}With a moan she flung herself forward, and fell upon it.
10084{_}1296{_}She no longer heard the wailing of the storm.
10084{_}1297{_}She no longer felt discomfort.
10084{_}1298{_}With her face in the furs under which baby Joan was buried, there came to her with swiftness and joy a vision of warmth and home.
10084{_}1299{_}And then the vision faded away, and was followed by deep night.
10084{_}1300{_}Kazan stopped in the trail.
10084{_}1301{_}He came back then and sat down upon his haunches beside her, waiting for her to move and speak.
10084{_}1302{_}But she was very still.
10084{_}1303{_}He thrust his nose into her loose hair.
10084{_}1304{_}A whine rose in his throat, and suddenly he raised his head and sniffed in the face of the wind.
10084{_}1305{_}Something came to him with that wind.
10084{_}1306{_}He muzzled Joan again, hut she did not stir.
10084{_}1307{_}Then he went forward, and stood in his traces, ready for the pull, and looked hack at her.
10084{_}1308{_}Still she did not move or speak, and Kazan's whine gave place to a sharp excited bark.
10084{_}1309{_}The strange thing in the wind came to him stronger for a moment.
10084{_}1310{_}He began to pull.
10084{_}1311{_}The sledge-runners had frozen to the snow, and it took every ounce of his strength to free them.
10084{_}1312{_}Twice during the next five minutes he stopped and sniffed the air.
10084{_}1313{_}The third time that he halted, in a drift of snow, he returned to Joan's side again, and whined to awaken her.
10084{_}1314{_}Then he tugged again at the end of his traces, and foot by foot he dragged the sledge through the drift.
10084{_}1315{_}Beyond the drift there was a stretch of clear ice, and here Kazan rested.
10084{_}1316{_}During a lull in the wind the scent came to him stronger than before.
10084{_}1317{_}At the end of the clear ice was a narrow break in the shore, where a creek ran into the main stream.
10084{_}1318{_}If Joan had been conscious she would have urged him straight ahead.
10084{_}1319{_}But Kazan turned into the break, and for ten minutes he struggled through the snow without a rest, whining more and more frequently, until at last the whine broke into a joyous bark.
10084{_}1320{_}Ahead of him, close to the creek, was a small cabin.
10084{_}1321{_}Smoke was rising out of the chimney.
10084{_}1322{_}It was the scent of smoke that had come to him in the wind.
10084{_}1323{_}A hard level slope reached to the cabin door, and with the last strength that was in him Kazan dragged his burden up that.
10084{_}1324{_}Then he settled himself back beside Joan, lifted his shaggy head to the dark sky and howled.
10084{_}1325{_}A moment later the door opened.
10084{_}1326{_}A man came out.
10084{_}1327{_}Kazan's reddened, snow-shot eyes followed him watchfully as he ran to the sledge.
10084{_}1328{_}He heard his startled exclamation as he bent over Joan.
10084{_}1329{_}In another lull of the wind there came from out of the mass of furs on the sledge the wailing, half-smothered voice of baby Joan.
10084{_}1330{_}A deep sigh of relief heaved up from Kazan's chest.
10084{_}1331{_}He was exhausted.
10084{_}1332{_}His strength was gone.
10084{_}1333{_}His feet were torn and bleeding.
10084{_}1334{_}But the voice of baby Joan filled him with a strange happiness, and he lay down in his traces, while the man carried Joan and the baby into the life and warmth of the cabin.
10084{_}1335{_}A few minutes later the man reappeared.
10084{_}1336{_}He was not old, like Pierre Radisson.
10084{_}1337{_}He came close to Kazan, and looked down at him.
10084{_}1338{_}"My God," he said.
10084{_}1339{_}"And you did that--_alone!_"  He bent down fearlessly, unfastened him from the traces, and led him toward the cabin door.
10084{_}1340{_}Kazan hesitated but once--almost on the threshold.
10084{_}1341{_}He turned his head, swift and alert.
10084{_}1342{_}From out of the moaning and wailing of the storm it seemed to him that for a moment he had heard the voice of Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1343{_}Then the cabin door closed behind him.
10084{_}1344{_}Back in a shadowy corner of the cabin he lay, while the man prepared something over a hot stove for Joan.
10084{_}1345{_}It was a long time before Joan rose from the cot on which the man had placed her.
10084{_}1346{_}After that Kazan heard her sobbing; and then the man made her eat, and for a time they talked.
10084{_}1347{_}Then the stranger hung up a big blanket in front of the bunk, and sat down close to the stove.
10084{_}1348{_}Quietly Kazan slipped along the wall, and crept under the bunk.
10084{_}1349{_}For a long time he could hear the sobbing breath of the girl.
10084{_}1350{_}Then all was still.
10084{_}1351{_}The next morning he slipped out through the door when the man opened it, and sped swiftly into the forest.
10084{_}1352{_}Half a mile away he found the trail of Gray Wolf, and called to her.
10084{_}1353{_}From the frozen river came her reply, and he went to her.
10084{_}1354{_}Vainly Gray Wolf tried to lure him back into their old haunts--away from the cabin and the scent of man.
10084{_}1355{_}Late that morning the man harnessed his dogs, and from the fringe of the forest Kazan saw him tuck Joan and the baby among the furs on the sledge, as old Pierre had done.
10084{_}1356{_}All that day he followed in the trail of the team, with Gray Wolf slinking behind him.
10084{_}1357{_}They traveled until dark; and then, under the stars and the moon that had followed the storm, the man still urged on his team.
10084{_}1358{_}It was deep in the night when they came to another cabin, and the man beat upon the door.
10084{_}1359{_}A light, the opening of the door, the joyous welcome of a man's voice, Joan's sobbing cry--Kazan heard these from the shadows in which he was hidden, and then slipped back to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1360{_}In the days and weeks that followed Joan's home-coming the lure of the cabin and of the woman's hand held Kazan.
10084{_}1361{_}As he had tolerated Pierre, so now he tolerated the younger man who lived with Joan and the baby.
10084{_}1362{_}He knew that the man was very dear to Joan, and that the baby was very dear to him, as it was to the girl.
10084{_}1363{_}It was not until the third day that Joan succeeded in coaxing him into the cabin--and that was the day on which the man returned with the dead and frozen body of Pierre.
10084{_}1364{_}It was Joan's husband who first found the name on the collar he wore, and they began calling him Kazan.
10084{_}1365{_}Half a mile away, at the summit of a huge mass of rock which the Indians called the Sun Rock, he and Gray Wolf had found a home; and from here they went down to their hunts on the plain, and often the girl's voice reached up to them, calling, "_Kazan!
10084{_}1366{_}Kazan!
10084{_}1367{_}Kazan_!"
10084{_}1368{_}Through all the long winter Kazan hovered thus between the lure of Joan and the cabin--and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1369{_}Then came Spring--and the Great Change.
10084{_}1370{_}CHAPTER VIII  THE GREAT CHANGE   The rocks, the ridges and the valleys were taking on a warmer glow.
10084{_}1371{_}The poplar buds were ready to burst.
10084{_}1372{_}The scent of balsam and of spruce grew heavier in the air each day, and all through the wilderness, in plain and forest, there was the rippling murmur of the spring floods finding their way to Hudson's Bay.
10084{_}1373{_}In that great bay there was the rumble and crash of the ice fields thundering down in the early break-up through the Roes Welcome--the doorway to the Arctic, and for that reason there still came with the April wind an occasional sharp breath of winter.
10084{_}1374{_}Kazan had sheltered himself against that wind.
10084{_}1375{_}Not a breath of air stirred in the sunny spot the wolf-dog had chosen for himself.
10084{_}1376{_}He was more comfortable than he had been at any time during the six months of terrible winter--and as he slept he dreamed.
10084{_}1377{_}Gray Wolf, his wild mate, lay near him, flat on her belly, her forepaws reaching out, her eyes and nostrils as keen and alert as the smell of man could make them.
10084{_}1378{_}For there was that smell of man, as well as of balsam and spruce, in the warm spring air.
10084{_}1379{_}She gazed anxiously and sometimes steadily, at Kazan as he slept.
10084{_}1380{_}Her own gray spine stiffened when she saw the tawny hair along Kazan's back bristle at some dream vision.
10084{_}1381{_}She whined softly as his upper lip snarled back, showing his long white fangs.
10084{_}1382{_}But for the most part Kazan lay quiet, save for the muscular twitchings of legs, shoulders and muzzle, which always tell when a dog is dreaming; and as he dreamed there came to the door of the cabin out on the plain a blue-eyed girl-woman, with a big brown braid over her shoulder, who called through the cup of her hands, "Kazan, Kazan, Kazan!"
10084{_}1383{_}The voice reached faintly to the top of the Sun Rock, and Gray Wolf flattened her ears.
10084{_}1384{_}Kazan stirred, and in another instant he was awake and on his feet.
10084{_}1385{_}He leaped to an outcropping ledge, sniffing the air and looking far out over the plain that lay below them.
10084{_}1386{_}Over the plain the woman's voice came to them again, and Kazan ran to the edge of the rock and whined.
10084{_}1387{_}Gray Wolf stepped softly to his side and laid her muzzle on his shoulder.
10084{_}1388{_}She had grown to know what the Voice meant.
10084{_}1389{_}Day and night she feared it, more than she feared the scent or sound of man.
10084{_}1390{_}Since she had given up the pack and her old life for Kazan, the Voice had become Gray Wolf's greatest enemy, and she hated it.
10084{_}1391{_}It took Kazan from her.
10084{_}1392{_}And wherever it went, Kazan followed.
10084{_}1393{_}Night after night it robbed her of her mate, and left her to wander alone under the stars and the moon, keeping faithfully to her loneliness, and never once responding with her own tongue to the hunt-calls of her wild brothers and sisters in the forests and out on the plains.
10084{_}1394{_}Usually she would snarl at the Voice, and sometimes nip Kazan lightly to show her displeasure.
10084{_}1395{_}But to-day, as the Voice came a third time, she slunk back into the darkness of a fissure between two rocks, and Kazan saw only the fiery glow of her eyes.
10084{_}1396{_}Kazan ran nervously to the trail their feet had worn up to the top of the Sun Rock, and stood undecided.
10084{_}1397{_}All day, and yesterday, he had been uneasy and disturbed.
10084{_}1398{_}Whatever it was that stirred him seemed to be in the air, for he could not see it or hear it or scent it.
10084{_}1399{_}But he could _feel_ it.
10084{_}1400{_}He went to the fissure and sniffed at Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1401{_}Usually she whined coaxingly.
10084{_}1402{_}But her response to-day was to draw back her lips until he could see her white fangs.
10084{_}1403{_}A fourth tune the Voice came to them faintly, and she snapped fiercely at some unseen thing in the darkness between the two rocks.
10084{_}1404{_}Kazan went again to the trail, still hesitating.
10084{_}1405{_}Then he began to go down.
10084{_}1406{_}It was a narrow winding trail, worn only by the pads and claws of animals, for the Sun Rock was a huge crag that rose almost sheer up for a hundred feet above the tops of the spruce and balsam, its bald crest catching the first gleams of the sun in the morning and the last glow of it in the evening.
10084{_}1407{_}Gray Wolf had first led Kazan to the security of the retreat at the top of the rock.
10084{_}1408{_}When he reached the bottom he no longer hesitated, but darted swiftly in the direction of the cabin.
10084{_}1409{_}Because of that instinct of the wild that was still in him, he always approached the cabin with caution.
10084{_}1410{_}He never gave warning, and for a moment Joan was startled when she looked up from her baby and saw Kazan's shaggy head and shoulders in the open door.
10084{_}1411{_}The baby struggled and kicked in her delight, and held out her two hands with cooing cries to Kazan.
10084{_}1412{_}Joan, too, held out a hand.
10084{_}1413{_}"Kazan!"
10084{_}1414{_}she cried softly.
10084{_}1415{_}"Come in, Kazan!"
10084{_}1416{_}Slowly the wild red light in Kazan's eyes softened.
10084{_}1417{_}He put a forefoot on the sill, and stood there, while the girl urged him again.
10084{_}1418{_}Suddenly his legs seemed to sink a little under him, his tail drooped and he slunk in with that doggish air of having committed a crime.
10084{_}1419{_}The creatures he loved were in the cabin, but the cabin itself he hated.
10084{_}1420{_}He hated all cabins, for they all breathed of the club and the whip and bondage.
10084{_}1421{_}Like all sledge-dogs he preferred the open snow for a bed, and the spruce-tops for shelter.
10084{_}1422{_}Joan dropped her hand to his head, and at its touch there thrilled through him that strange joy that was his reward for leaving Gray Wolf and the wild.
10084{_}1423{_}Slowly he raised his head until his black muzzle rested on her lap, and he closed his eyes while that wonderful little creature that mystified him so--the baby--prodded him with her tiny feet, and pulled his tawny hair.
10084{_}1424{_}He loved these baby-maulings even more than the touch of Joan's hand.
10084{_}1425{_}Motionless, sphinx-like, undemonstrative in every muscle of his body, Kazan stood, scarcely breathing.
10084{_}1426{_}More than once this lack of demonstration had urged Joan's husband to warn her.
10084{_}1427{_}But the wolf that was in Kazan, his wild aloofness, even his mating with Gray Wolf had made her love him more.
10084{_}1428{_}She understood, and had faith in him.
10084{_}1429{_}In the days of the last snow Kazan had proved himself.
10084{_}1430{_}A neighboring trapper had run over with his team, and the baby Joan had toddled up to one of the big huskies.
10084{_}1431{_}There was a fierce snap of jaws, a scream of horror from Joan, a shout from the men as they leaped toward the pack.
10084{_}1432{_}But Kazan was ahead of them all.
10084{_}1433{_}In a gray streak that traveled with the speed of a bullet he was at the big husky's throat.
10084{_}1434{_}When they pulled him off, the husky was dead.
10084{_}1435{_}Joan thought of that now, as the baby kicked and tousled Kazan's head.
10084{_}1436{_}"Good old Kazan," she cried softly, putting her face down close to him.
10084{_}1437{_}"We're glad you came, Kazan, for we're going to be alone to-night--baby and I. Daddy's gone to the post, and you must care for us while he's away."
10084{_}1438{_}She tickled his nose with the end of her long shining braid.
10084{_}1439{_}This always delighted the baby, for in spite of his stoicism Kazan had to sniff and sometimes to sneeze, and twig his ears.
10084{_}1440{_}And it pleased him, too.
10084{_}1441{_}He loved the sweet scent of Joan's hair.
10084{_}1442{_}"And you'd fight for us, if you had to, wouldn't you?"
10084{_}1443{_}she went on.
10084{_}1444{_}Then she rose quietly.
10084{_}1445{_}"I must close the door," she said.
10084{_}1446{_}"I don't want you to go away again to-day, Kazan.
10084{_}1447{_}You must stay with us."
10084{_}1448{_}Kazan went off to his corner, and lay down.
10084{_}1449{_}Just as there had been some strange thing at the top of the Sun Rock to disturb him that day, so now there was a mystery that disturbed him in the cabin.
10084{_}1450{_}He sniffed the air, trying to fathom its secret.
10084{_}1451{_}Whatever it was, it seemed to make his mistress different, too.
10084{_}1452{_}And she was digging out all sorts of odds and ends of things about the cabin, and doing them up in packages.
10084{_}1453{_}Late that night, before she went to bed, Joan came and snuggled her hand close down beside him for a few moments.
10084{_}1454{_}"We're going away," she whispered, and there was a curious tremble that was almost a sob in her voice.
10084{_}1455{_}"We're going home, Kazan.
10084{_}1456{_}We're going away down where his people live--where they have churches, and cities, and music, and all the beautiful things in the world.
10084{_}1457{_}And we're going to take _you_, Kazan!"
10084{_}1458{_}Kazan didn't understand.
10084{_}1459{_}But he was happy at having the woman so near to him, and talking to him.
10084{_}1460{_}At these times he forgot Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1461{_}The dog that was in him surged over his quarter-strain of wildness, and the woman and the baby alone filled his world.
10084{_}1462{_}But after Joan had gone to her bed, and all was quiet in the cabin, his old uneasiness returned.
10084{_}1463{_}He rose to his feet and moved stealthily about the cabin, sniffing at the walls, the door and the things his mistress had done into packages.
10084{_}1464{_}A low whine rose in his throat.
10084{_}1465{_}Joan, half asleep, heard it, and murmured: "Be quiet, Kazan.
10084{_}1466{_}Go to sleep--go to sleep--"  Long after that, Kazan stood rigid in the center of the room, listening, trembling.
10084{_}1467{_}And faintly he heard, far away, the wailing cry of, Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1468{_}But to-night it was not the cry of loneliness.
10084{_}1469{_}It sent a thrill through him.
10084{_}1470{_}He ran to the door, and whined, but Joan was deep in slumber and did not hear him.
10084{_}1471{_}Once more he heard the cry, and only once.
10084{_}1472{_}Then the night grew still.
10084{_}1473{_}He crouched down near the door.
10084{_}1474{_}Joan found him there, still watchful, still listening, when she awoke in the early morning.
10084{_}1475{_}She came to open the door for him, and in a moment he was gone.
10084{_}1476{_}His feet seemed scarcely to touch the earth as he sped in the direction of the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1477{_}Across the plain he could see the cap of it already painted with a golden glow.
10084{_}1478{_}He came to the narrow winding trail, and wormed his way up it swiftly.
10084{_}1479{_}Gray Wolf was not at the top to greet him.
10084{_}1480{_}But he could smell her, and the scent of that other thing was strong in the air.
10084{_}1481{_}His muscles tightened; his legs grew tense.
10084{_}1482{_}Deep down in his chest there began the low rumble of a growl.
10084{_}1483{_}He knew now what that strange thing was that had haunted him, and made him uneasy.
10084{_}1484{_}It was _life_.
10084{_}1485{_}Something that lived and breathed had invaded the home which he and Gray Wolf had chosen.
10084{_}1486{_}He bared his long fangs, and a snarl of defiance drew back his lips.
10084{_}1487{_}Stiff-legged, prepared to spring, his neck and head reaching out, he approached the two rocks between which Gray Wolf had crept the night before.
10084{_}1488{_}She was still there.
10084{_}1489{_}And with her was _something else_.
10084{_}1490{_}After a moment the tenseness left Kazan's body.
10084{_}1491{_}His bristling crest drooped until it lay flat.
10084{_}1492{_}His ears shot forward, and he put his head and shoulders between the two rocks, and whined softly.
10084{_}1493{_}And Gray Wolf whined.
10084{_}1494{_}Slowly Kazan backed out, and faced the rising sun.
10084{_}1495{_}Then he lay down, so that his body shielded I the entrance to the chamber between the rocks.
10084{_}1496{_}Gray Wolf was a mother.
10084{_}1497{_}CHAPTER IX  THE TRAGEDY ON SUN ROCK   All that day Kazan guarded the top of the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1498{_}Fate, and the fear and brutality of masters, had heretofore kept him from fatherhood, and he was puzzled.
10084{_}1499{_}Something told him now that he belonged to the Sun Rock, and not to the cabin.
10084{_}1500{_}The call that came to him from over the plain was not so strong.
10084{_}1501{_}At dusk Gray Wolf came out from her retreat, and slunk to his side, whimpering, and nipped gently at his shaggy neck.
10084{_}1502{_}It was the old instinct of his fathers that made him respond by caressing Gray Wolf's face with his tongue.
10084{_}1503{_}Then Gray Wolf's jaws opened, and she laughed in short panting breaths, as if she had been hard run.
10084{_}1504{_}She was happy, and as they heard a little snuffling sound from between the rocks, Kazan wagged his tail, and Gray Wolf darted back to her young.
10084{_}1505{_}The babyish cry and its effect upon Gray Wolf taught Kazan his first lesson in fatherhood.
10084{_}1506{_}Instinct again told him that Gray Wolf could not go down to the hunt with him now--that she must stay at the top of the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1507{_}So when the moon rose he went down alone, and toward dawn returned with a big white rabbit between his jaws.
10084{_}1508{_}It was the wild in him that made him do this, and Gray Wolf ate ravenously.
10084{_}1509{_}Then he knew that each night hereafter he must hunt for Gray Wolf--and the little whimpering creatures hidden between the two rocks.
10084{_}1510{_}The next day, and still the next, he did not go to the cabin, though he heard the voices of both the man and the woman calling him.
10084{_}1511{_}On the fifth he went down, and Joan and the baby were so glad that the woman hugged him, and the baby kicked and laughed and screamed at him, while the man stood by cautiously, watching their demonstrations with a gleam of disapprobation in his eyes.
10084{_}1512{_}"I'm afraid of him," he told Joan for the hundredth time.
10084{_}1513{_}"That's the wolf-gleam in his eyes.
10084{_}1514{_}He's of a treacherous breed.
10084{_}1515{_}Sometimes I wish we'd never brought him home."
10084{_}1516{_}"If we hadn't--where would the baby--have gone?"
10084{_}1517{_}Joan reminded him, a little catch in her voice.
10084{_}1518{_}"I had almost forgotten that," said her husband.
10084{_}1519{_}"Kazan, you old devil, I guess I love you, too."
10084{_}1520{_}He laid his hand caressingly on Kazan's head.
10084{_}1521{_}"Wonder how he'll take to life down there?"
10084{_}1522{_}he asked.
10084{_}1523{_}"He has always been used to the forests.
10084{_}1524{_}It'll seem mighty strange."
10084{_}1525{_}"And so--have I--always been used to the forests," whispered Joan.
10084{_}1526{_}"I guess that's why I love Kazan--next to you and the baby.
10084{_}1527{_}Kazan--dear old Kazan!"
10084{_}1528{_}This time Kazan felt and scented more of that mysterious change in the cabin.
10084{_}1529{_}Joan and her husband talked incessantly of their plans when they were together; and when the man was away Joan talked to the baby, and to him.
10084{_}1530{_}And each time that he came down to the cabin during the week that followed, he grew more and more restless, until at last the man noticed the change in him.
10084{_}1531{_}"I believe he knows," he said to Joan one evening.
10084{_}1532{_}"I believe he knows we're preparing to leave."
10084{_}1533{_}Then he added: "The river was rising again to-day.
10084{_}1534{_}It will be another week before we can start, perhaps longer."
10084{_}1535{_}That same night the moon flooded the top of the Sun Rock with a golden light, and out into the glow of it came Gray Wolf, with her three little whelps toddling behind her.
10084{_}1536{_}There was much about these soft little balls that tumbled about him and snuggled in his tawny coat that reminded Kazan of the baby.
10084{_}1537{_}At times they made the same queer, soft little sounds, and they staggered about on their four little legs just as helplessly as baby Joan made her way about on two.
10084{_}1538{_}He did not fondle them, as Gray Wolf did, but the touch of them, and their babyish whimperings, filled him with a kind of pleasure that he had never experienced before.
10084{_}1539{_}The moon was straight above them, and the night was almost as bright as day, when he went down again to hunt for Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1540{_}At the foot of the rock a big white rabbit popped up ahead of him, and he gave chase.
10084{_}1541{_}For half a mile he pursued, until the wolf instinct in him rose over the dog, and he gave up the futile race.
10084{_}1542{_}A deer he might have overtaken, but small game the wolf must hunt as the fox hunts it, and he began to slip through the thickets slowly and as quietly as a shadow.
10084{_}1543{_}He was a mile from the Sun Rock when two quick leaps put Gray Wolf's supper between his jaws.
10084{_}1544{_}He trotted back slowly, dropping the big seven-pound snow-shoe hare now and then to rest.
10084{_}1545{_}When he came to the narrow trail that led to the top of the Sun Rock he stopped.
10084{_}1546{_}In that trail was the warm scent of strange feet.
10084{_}1547{_}The rabbit fell from his jaws.
10084{_}1548{_}Every hair in his body was suddenly electrified into life.
10084{_}1549{_}What he scented was not the scent of a rabbit, a marten or a porcupine.
10084{_}1550{_}Fang and claw had climbed the path ahead of him.
10084{_}1551{_}And then, coming faintly to him from the top of the rock, he heard sounds which sent him up with a terrible whining cry.
10084{_}1552{_}When he reached the summit he saw in the white moonlight a scene that stopped him for a single moment.
10084{_}1553{_}Close to the edge of the sheer fall to the rocks, fifty feet below, Gray Wolf was engaged in a death-struggle with a huge gray lynx.
10084{_}1554{_}She was down--and under, and from her there came a sudden sharp terrible cry of pain.
10084{_}1555{_}Kazan flew across the rock.
10084{_}1556{_}His attack was the swift silent assault of the wolf, combined with the greater courage, the fury and the strategy of the husky.
10084{_}1557{_}Another husky would have died in that first attack.
10084{_}1558{_}But the lynx was not a dog or a wolf.
10084{_}1559{_}It was "Mow-lee, the swift," as the Sarcees had named it--the quickest creature in the wilderness.
10084{_}1560{_}Kazan's inch-long fangs should have sunk deep in its jugular.
10084{_}1561{_}But in a fractional part of a second the lynx had thrown itself back like a huge soft ball, and Kazan's teeth buried themselves in the flesh of its neck instead of the jugular.
10084{_}1562{_}And Kazan was not now fighting the fangs of a wolf in the pack, or of another husky.
10084{_}1563{_}He was fighting claws--claws that ripped like twenty razor-edged knives, and which even a jugular hold could not stop.
10084{_}1564{_}Once he had fought a lynx in a trap, and he had not forgotten the lesson the battle had taught him.
10084{_}1565{_}He fought to pull the lynx _down_, instead of forcing it on its back, as he would have done with another dog or a wolf.
10084{_}1566{_}He knew that when on its back the fierce cat was most dangerous.
10084{_}1567{_}One rip of its powerful hindfeet could disembowel him.
10084{_}1568{_}Behind him he heard Gray Wolf sobbing and crying, and he knew that she was terribly hurt.
10084{_}1569{_}He was filled with the rage and strength of two dogs, and his teeth met through the flesh and hide of the cat's throat.
10084{_}1570{_}But the big lynx escaped death by half an inch.
10084{_}1571{_}It would take a fresh grip to reach the jugular, and suddenly Kazan made the deadly lunge.
10084{_}1572{_}There was an instant's freedom for the lynx, and in that moment it flung itself back, and Kazan gripped at its throat--_on top_.
10084{_}1573{_}The cat's claws ripped through his flesh, cutting open his side--a little too high to kill.
10084{_}1574{_}Another stroke and they would have cut to his vitals.
10084{_}1575{_}But they had struggled close to the edge of the rock wall, and suddenly, without a snarl or a cry, they rolled over.
10084{_}1576{_}It was fifty or sixty feet to the rocks of the ledge below, and even as they pitched over and over in the fall, Kazan's teeth sank deeper.
10084{_}1577{_}They struck with terrific force, Kazan uppermost.
10084{_}1578{_}The shock sent him half a dozen feet from his enemy.
10084{_}1579{_}He was up like a flash, dizzy, snarling, on the defensive.
10084{_}1580{_}The lynx lay limp and motionless where it had fallen.
10084{_}1581{_}Kazan came nearer, still prepared, and sniffed cautiously.
10084{_}1582{_}Something told him that the fight was over.
10084{_}1583{_}He turned and dragged himself slowly along the ledge to the trail, and returned to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1584{_}Gray Wolf was no longer in the moonlight.
10084{_}1585{_}Close to the two rocks lay the limp and lifeless little bodies of the three pups.
10084{_}1586{_}The lynx had torn them to pieces.
10084{_}1587{_}With a whine of grief Kazan approached the two boulders and thrust his head between them.
10084{_}1588{_}Gray Wolf was there, crying to herself in that terrible sobbing way.
10084{_}1589{_}He went in, and began to lick her bleeding shoulders and head.
10084{_}1590{_}All the rest of that night she whimpered with pain.
10084{_}1591{_}With dawn she dragged herself out to the lifeless little bodies on the rock.
10084{_}1592{_}And then Kazan saw the terrible work of the lynx.
10084{_}1593{_}For Gray Wolf was blind--not for a day or a night, but blind for all time.
10084{_}1594{_}A gloom that no sun could break had become her shroud.
10084{_}1595{_}And perhaps again it was that instinct of animal creation, which often is more wonderful than man's reason, that told Kazan what had happened.
10084{_}1596{_}For he knew now that she was helpless--more helpless than the little creatures that had gamboled in the moonlight a few hours before.
10084{_}1597{_}He remained close beside her all that day.
10084{_}1598{_}[Illustration: Kazan gripped at its throat]  Vainly that day did Joan call for Kazan.
10084{_}1599{_}Her voice rose to the Sun Rock, and Gray Wolf's head snuggled closer to Kazan, and Kazan's ears dropped back, and he licked her wounds.
10084{_}1600{_}Late in the afternoon Kazan left Gray Wolf long enough to run to the bottom of the trail and bring up the snow-shoe rabbit.
10084{_}1601{_}Gray Wolf muzzled the fur and flesh, but would not eat.
10084{_}1602{_}Still a little later Kazan urged her to follow him to the trail.
10084{_}1603{_}He no longer wanted to stay at the top of the Sun Rock, and he no longer wanted Gray Wolf to stay there.
10084{_}1604{_}Step by step he drew her down the winding path away from her dead puppies.
10084{_}1605{_}She would move only when he was very near her--so near that she could touch his scarred flank with her nose.
10084{_}1606{_}They came at last to the point in the trail where they had to leap down a distance of three or four feet from the edge of a rock, and here Kazan saw how utterly helpless Gray Wolf had become.
10084{_}1607{_}She whined, and crouched twenty times before she dared make the spring, and then she jumped stiff-legged, and fell in a heap at Kazan's feet.
10084{_}1608{_}After this Kazan did not have to urge her so hard, for the fall impinged on her the fact that she was safe only when her muzzle touched her mate's flank.
10084{_}1609{_}She followed him obediently when they reached the plain, trotting with her foreshoulder to his hip.
10084{_}1610{_}Kazan was heading for a thicket in the creek bottom half a mile away, and a dozen times in that short distance Gray Wolf stumbled and fell.
10084{_}1611{_}And each time that she fell Kazan learned a little more of the limitations of blindness.
10084{_}1612{_}Once he sprang off in pursuit of a rabbit, but he had not taken twenty leaps when he stopped and looked back.
10084{_}1613{_}Gray Wolf had not moved an inch.
10084{_}1614{_}She stood motionless, sniffing the air--waiting for him!
10084{_}1615{_}For a full minute Kazan stood, also waiting.
10084{_}1616{_}Then he returned to her.
10084{_}1617{_}Ever after this he returned to the point where he had left Gray Wolf, knowing that he would find her there.
10084{_}1618{_}All that day they remained in the thicket.
10084{_}1619{_}In the afternoon he visited the cabin.
10084{_}1620{_}Joan and her husband were there, and both saw at once Kazan's torn side and his lacerated head and shoulders.
10084{_}1621{_}"Pretty near a finish fight for him," said the man, after he had examined him.
10084{_}1622{_}"It was either a lynx or a bear.
10084{_}1623{_}Another wolf could not do that."
10084{_}1624{_}For half an hour Joan worked over him, talking to him all the time, and fondling him with her soft hands.
10084{_}1625{_}She bathed his wounds in warm water, and then covered them with a healing salve, and Kazan was filled again with that old restful desire to remain with her always, and never to go back into the forests.
10084{_}1626{_}For an hour she let him lie on the edge of her dress, with his nose touching her foot, while she worked on baby things.
10084{_}1627{_}Then she rose to prepare supper, and Kazan got up--a little wearily--and went to the door.
10084{_}1628{_}Gray Wolf and the gloom of the night were calling him, and he answered that call with a slouch of his shoulders and a drooping head.
10084{_}1629{_}Its old thrill was gone.
10084{_}1630{_}He watched his chance, and went out through the door.
10084{_}1631{_}The moon had risen when he rejoined Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1632{_}She greeted his return with a low whine of joy, and muzzled him with her blind face.
10084{_}1633{_}In her helplessness she looked happier than Kazan in all his strength.
10084{_}1634{_}From now on, during the days that followed, it was a last great fight between blind and faithful Gray Wolf and the woman.
10084{_}1635{_}If Joan had known of what lay in the thicket, if she could once have seen the poor creature to whom Kazan was now all life--the sun, the stars, the moon, and food--she would have helped Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1636{_}But as it was she tried to lure Kazan more and more to the cabin, and slowly she won.
10084{_}1637{_}At last the great day came, eight days after the fight on the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1638{_}Kazan had taken Gray Wolf to a wooded point on the river two days before, and there he had left her the preceding night when he went to the cabin.
10084{_}1639{_}This time a stout babiche thong was tied to the collar round his neck, and he was fastened to a staple in the log wall.
10084{_}1640{_}Joan and her husband were up before it was light next day.
10084{_}1641{_}The sun was just rising when they all went out, the man carrying the baby, and Joan leading him.
10084{_}1642{_}Joan turned and locked the cabin door, and Kazan heard a sob in her throat as they followed the man down to the river.
10084{_}1643{_}The big canoe was packed and waiting.
10084{_}1644{_}Joan got in first, with the baby.
10084{_}1645{_}Then, still holding the babiche thong, she drew Kazan up close to her, so that he lay with his weight against her.
10084{_}1646{_}The sun fell warmly on Kazan's back as they shoved off, and he closed his eyes, and rested his head on Joan's lap.
10084{_}1647{_}Her hand fell softly on his shoulder.
10084{_}1648{_}He heard again that sound which the man could not hear, the broken sob in her throat, as the canoe moved slowly down to the wooded point.
10084{_}1649{_}Joan waved her hand back at the cabin, just disappearing behind the trees.
10084{_}1650{_}"Good-by!"
10084{_}1651{_}she cried sadly.
10084{_}1652{_}"Good-by--" And then she buried her face close down to Kazan and the baby, and sobbed.
10084{_}1653{_}The man stopped paddling.
10084{_}1654{_}"You're not sorry--Joan?"
10084{_}1655{_}he asked.
10084{_}1656{_}They were drifting past the point now, and the scent of Gray Wolf came to Kazan's nostrils, rousing him, and bringing a low whine from his throat.
10084{_}1657{_}"You're not sorry--we're going?"
10084{_}1658{_}Joan shook her head.
10084{_}1659{_}"No," she replied.
10084{_}1660{_}"Only I've--always lived here--in the forests--and they're--home!"
10084{_}1661{_}The point with its white finger of sand, was behind them now.
10084{_}1662{_}And Kazan was standing rigid, facing it.
10084{_}1663{_}The man called to him, and Joan lifted her head.
10084{_}1664{_}She, too, saw the point, and suddenly the babiche leash slipped from her fingers, and a strange light leaped into her blue eyes as she saw what stood at the end of that white tip of sand.
10084{_}1665{_}It was Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1666{_}Her blind eyes were turned toward Kazan.
10084{_}1667{_}At last Gray Wolf, the faithful, understood.
10084{_}1668{_}Scent told her what her eyes could not see.
10084{_}1669{_}Kazan and the man-smell were together.
10084{_}1670{_}And they were going--going--going--  "Look!"
10084{_}1671{_}whispered Joan.
10084{_}1672{_}The man turned.
10084{_}1673{_}Gray Wolf's forefeet were in the water.
10084{_}1674{_}And now, as the canoe drifted farther and farther away, she settled back on her haunches, raised her head to the sun which she could not see and gave her last long wailing cry for Kazan.
10084{_}1675{_}The canoe lurched.
10084{_}1676{_}A tawny body shot through the air--and Kazan was gone.
10084{_}1677{_}The man reached forward for his rifle.
10084{_}1678{_}Joan's hand stopped him.
10084{_}1679{_}Her face was white.
10084{_}1680{_}"Let him go back to her!
10084{_}1681{_}Let him go--let him go!"
10084{_}1682{_}she cried.
10084{_}1683{_}"It is his place--with her."
10084{_}1684{_}And Kazan reaching the shore, shook the water from his shaggy hair, and looked for the last time toward the woman.
10084{_}1685{_}The canoe was drifting slowly around the first bend.
10084{_}1686{_}A moment more and it had disappeared.
10084{_}1687{_}Gray Wolf had won.
10084{_}1688{_}CHAPTER X  THE DAYS OF FIRE   From the night of the terrible fight with the big gray lynx on the top of the Sun Rock, Kazan remembered less and less vividly the old days when he had been a sledge-dog, and the leader of a pack.
10084{_}1689{_}He would never quite forget them, and always there would stand out certain memories from among the rest, like fires cutting the blackness of night.
10084{_}1690{_}But as man dates events from his birth, his marriage, his freedom from a bondage, or some foundation-step in his career, so all things seemed to Kazan to begin with two tragedies which had followed one fast upon the other after the birth of Gray Wolf's pups.
10084{_}1691{_}The first was the fight on the Sun Rock, when the big gray lynx had blinded his beautiful wolf mate for all time, and had torn her pups into pieces.
10084{_}1692{_}He in turn had killed the lynx.
10084{_}1693{_}But Gray Wolf was still blind.
10084{_}1694{_}Vengeance had not been able to give her sight.
10084{_}1695{_}She could no longer hunt with him, as they had hunted with the wild wolf-packs out on the plain, and in the dark forests.
10084{_}1696{_}So at thought of that night he always snarled, and his lips curled back to reveal his inch-long fangs.
10084{_}1697{_}The other tragedy was the going of Joan, her baby and her husband.
10084{_}1698{_}Something more infallible than reason told Kazan that they would not come back.
10084{_}1699{_}Brightest of all the pictures that remained with him was that of the sunny morning when the woman and the baby he loved, and the man he endured because of them, had gone away in the canoe, and often he would go to the point, and gaze longingly down-stream, where he had leaped from the canoe to return to his blind mate.
10084{_}1700{_}So Kazan's life seemed now to be made up chiefly of three things: his hatred of everything that bore the scent or mark of the lynx, his grieving for Joan and the baby, and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1701{_}It was natural that the strongest passion in him should be his hatred of the lynx, for not only Gray Wolf's blindness and the death of the pups, but even the loss of the woman and the baby he laid to that fatal struggle on the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1702{_}From that hour he became the deadliest enemy of the lynx tribe.
10084{_}1703{_}Wherever he struck the scent of the big gray cat he was turned into a snarling demon, and his hatred grew day by day, as he became more completely a part of the wild.
10084{_}1704{_}He found that Gray Wolf was more necessary to him now than she had ever been since the day she had left the wolf-pack for him.
10084{_}1705{_}He was three-quarters dog, and the dog-part of him demanded companionship.
10084{_}1706{_}There was only Gray Wolf to give him that now.
10084{_}1707{_}They were alone.
10084{_}1708{_}Civilization was four hundred miles south of them.
10084{_}1709{_}The nearest Hudson's Bay post was sixty miles to the west.
10084{_}1710{_}Often, in the days of the woman and the baby, Gray Wolf had spent her nights alone out in the forest, waiting and calling for Kazan.
10084{_}1711{_}Now it was Kazan who was lonely and uneasy when he was away from her side.
10084{_}1712{_}In her blindness Gray Wolf could no longer hunt with her mate.
10084{_}1713{_}But gradually a new code of understanding grew up between them, and through her blindness they learned many things that they had not known before.
10084{_}1714{_}By early summer Gray Wolf could travel with Kazan, if he did not move too swiftly.
10084{_}1715{_}She ran at his flank, with her shoulder or muzzle touching him, and Kazan learned not to leap, but to trot.
10084{_}1716{_}Very quickly he found that he must choose the easiest trails for Gray Wolf's feet.
10084{_}1717{_}When they came to a space to be bridged by a leap, he would muzzle Gray Wolf and whine, and she would stand with ears alert--listening.
10084{_}1718{_}Then Kazan would take the leap, and she understood the distance she had to cover.
10084{_}1719{_}She always over-leaped, which was a good fault.
10084{_}1720{_}In another way, and one that was destined to serve them many times in the future, she became of greater help than ever to Kazan.
10084{_}1721{_}Scent and hearing entirely took the place of sight.
10084{_}1722{_}Each day developed these senses more and more, and at the same time there developed between them the dumb language whereby she could impress upon Kazan what she had discovered by scent or sound.
10084{_}1723{_}It became a curious habit of Kazan's always to look at Gray Wolf when they stopped to listen, or to scent the air.
10084{_}1724{_}After the fight on the Sun Rock, Kazan had taken his blind mate to a thick clump of spruce and balsam in the river-bottom, where they remained until early summer.
10084{_}1725{_}Every day for weeks Kazan went to the cabin where Joan and the baby--and the man--had been.
10084{_}1726{_}For a long time he went hopefully, looking each day or night to see some sign of life there.
10084{_}1727{_}But the door was never open.
10084{_}1728{_}The boards and saplings at the windows always remained.
10084{_}1729{_}Never a spiral of smoke rose from the clay chimney.
10084{_}1730{_}Grass and vines began to grow in the path.
10084{_}1731{_}And fainter and fainter grew that scent which Kazan could still find about it--the scent of man, of the woman, the baby.
10084{_}1732{_}One day he found a little baby moccasin under one of the closed windows.
10084{_}1733{_}It was old, and worn out, and blackened by snow and rain, but he lay down beside it, and remained there for a long time, while the baby Joan--a thousand miles away--was playing with the strange toys of civilization.
10084{_}1734{_}Then he returned to Gray Wolf among the spruce and balsam.
10084{_}1735{_}The cabin was the one place to which Gray Wolf would not follow him.
10084{_}1736{_}At all other times she was at his side.
10084{_}1737{_}Now that she had become accustomed to blindness, she even accompanied him on his hunts, until he struck game, and began the chase.
10084{_}1738{_}Then she would wait for him.
10084{_}1739{_}Kazan usually hunted the big snow-shoe rabbits.
10084{_}1740{_}But one night he ran down and killed a young doe.
10084{_}1741{_}The kill was too heavy to drag to Gray Wolf, so he returned to where she was waiting for him and guided her to the feast.
