10084{_}1742{_}In many ways they became more and more inseparable as the summer lengthened, until at last, through all the wilderness, their footprints were always two by two and never one by one.
10084{_}1743{_}Then came the great fire.
10084{_}1744{_}Gray Wolf caught the scent of it when it was still two days to the west.
10084{_}1745{_}The sun that night went down in a lurid cloud.
10084{_}1746{_}The moon, drifting into the west, became blood red.
10084{_}1747{_}When it dropped behind the wilderness in this manner, the Indians called it the Bleeding Moon, and the air was filled with omens.
10084{_}1748{_}All the next day Gray Wolf was nervous, and toward noon Kazan caught in the air the warning that she had sensed many hours ahead of him.
10084{_}1749{_}Steadily the scent grew stronger, and by the middle of the afternoon the sun was veiled by a film of smoke.
10084{_}1750{_}The flight of the wild things from the triangle of forest between the junctions of the Pipestone and Cree Rivers would have begun then, but the wind shifted.
10084{_}1751{_}It was a fatal shift.
10084{_}1752{_}The fire was raging from the west and south.
10084{_}1753{_}Then the wind swept straight eastward, carrying the smoke with it, and during this breathing spell all the wild creatures in the triangle between the two rivers waited.
10084{_}1754{_}This gave the fire time to sweep completely, across the base of the forest triangle, cutting off the last trails of escape.
10084{_}1755{_}Then the wind shifted again, and the fire swept north.
10084{_}1756{_}The head of the triangle became a death-trap.
10084{_}1757{_}All through the night the southern sky was filled with a lurid glow, and by morning the heat and smoke and ash were suffocating.
10084{_}1758{_}Panic-striken, Kazan searched vainly for a means of escape.
10084{_}1759{_}Not for an instant did he leave Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1760{_}It would have been easy for him to swim across either of the two streams, for he was three-quarters dog.
10084{_}1761{_}But at the first touch of water on her paws, Gray Wolf drew back, shrinking.
10084{_}1762{_}Like all her breed, she would face fire and death before water.
10084{_}1763{_}Kazan urged.
10084{_}1764{_}A dozen times he leaped in, and swam out into the stream.
10084{_}1765{_}But Gray Wolf would come no farther than she could wade.
10084{_}1766{_}They could hear the distant murmuring roar of the fire now.
10084{_}1767{_}Ahead of it came the wild things.
10084{_}1768{_}Moose, caribou and deer plunged into the water of the streams and swam to the safety of the opposite side.
10084{_}1769{_}Out upon a white finger of sand lumbered a big black bear with two cubs, and even the cubs took to the water, and swam across easily.
10084{_}1770{_}Kazan watched them, and whined to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1771{_}And then out upon that white finger of sand came other things that dreaded the water as Gray Wolf dreaded it: a big fat porcupine, a sleek little marten, a fisher-cat that sniffed the air and wailed like a child.
10084{_}1772{_}Those things that could not or would not swim outnumbered the others three to one.
10084{_}1773{_}Hundreds of little ermine scurried along the shore like rats, their squeaking little voices sounding incessantly; foxes ran swiftly along the banks, seeking a tree or a windfall that might bridge the water for them; the lynx snarled and faced the fire; and Gray Wolf's own tribe--the wolves--dared take no deeper step than she.
10084{_}1774{_}Dripping and panting, and half choked by heat and smoke, Kazan came to Gray Wolf's side.
10084{_}1775{_}There was but one refuge left near them, and that was the sand-bar.
10084{_}1776{_}It reached out for fifty feet into the stream.
10084{_}1777{_}Quickly he led his blind mate toward it.
10084{_}1778{_}As they came through the low bush to the river-bed, something stopped them both.
10084{_}1779{_}To their nostrils had come the scent of a deadlier enemy than fire.
10084{_}1780{_}A lynx had taken possession of the sand-bar, and was crouching at the end of it.
10084{_}1781{_}Three porcupines had dragged themselves into the edge of the water, and lay there like balls, their quills alert and quivering.
10084{_}1782{_}A fisher-cat was snarling at the lynx.
10084{_}1783{_}And the lynx, with ears laid back, watched Kazan and Gray Wolf as they began the invasion of the sand-bar.
10084{_}1784{_}Faithful Gray Wolf was full of fight, and she sprang shoulder to shoulder with Kazan, her fangs bared.
10084{_}1785{_}With an angry snap, Kazan drove her back, and she stood quivering and whining while he advanced.
10084{_}1786{_}Light-footed, his pointed ears forward, no menace or threat in his attitude, he advanced.
10084{_}1787{_}It was the deadly advance of the husky trained in battle, skilled in the art of killing.
10084{_}1788{_}A man from civilization would have said that the dog was approaching the lynx with friendly intentions.
10084{_}1789{_}But the lynx understood.
10084{_}1790{_}It was the old feud of many generations--made deadlier now by Kazan's memory of that night at the top of the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1791{_}Instinct told the fisher-cat what was coming, and it crouched low and flat; the porcupines, scolding like little children at the presence of enemies and the thickening clouds of smoke, thrust their quills still more erect.
10084{_}1792{_}The lynx lay on its belly, like a cat, its hindquarters twitching, and gathered for the spring.
10084{_}1793{_}Kazan's feet seemed scarcely to touch the sand as he circled lightly around it.
10084{_}1794{_}The lynx pivoted as he circled, and then it shot in a round snarling ball over the eight feet of space that separated them.
10084{_}1795{_}Kazan did not leap aside.
10084{_}1796{_}He made no effort to escape the attack, but met it fairly with the full force of his shoulders, as sledge-dog meets sledge-dog.
10084{_}1797{_}He was ten pounds heavier than the lynx, and for a moment the big loose-jointed cat with its twenty knife-like claws was thrown on its side.
10084{_}1798{_}Like a flash Kazan took advantage of the moment, and drove for the back of the cat's neck.
10084{_}1799{_}In that same moment blind Gray Wolf leaped in with a snarling cry, and fighting under Kazan's belly, she fastened her jaws in one of the cat's hindlegs.
10084{_}1800{_}The bone snapped.
10084{_}1801{_}The lynx, twice outweighed, leaped backward, dragging both Kazan and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1802{_}It fell back down on one of the porcupines, and a hundred quills drove into its body.
10084{_}1803{_}Another leap and it was free--fleeing into the face of the smoke.
10084{_}1804{_}Kazan did not pursue.
10084{_}1805{_}Gray Wolf came to his side and licked his neck, where fresh blood was crimsoning his tawny hide.
10084{_}1806{_}The fisher-cat lay as if dead, watching them with fierce little black eyes.
10084{_}1807{_}The porcupines continued to chatter, as if begging for mercy.
10084{_}1808{_}And then a thick black suffocating pall of smoke drove low over the sand-bar and with it came air that was furnace-hot.
10084{_}1809{_}At the uttermost end of the sand-bar Kazan and Gray Wolf rolled themselves into balls and thrust their heads under their bodies.
10084{_}1810{_}The fire was very near now.
10084{_}1811{_}The roar of it was like that of a great cataract, with now and then a louder crash of falling trees.
10084{_}1812{_}The air was filled with ash and burning sparks, and twice Kazan drew forth his head to snap at blazing embers that fell upon and seared him like hot irons.
10084{_}1813{_}Close along the edge of the stream grew thick green bush, and when the fire reached this, it burned more slowly, and the heat grew less.
10084{_}1814{_}Still, it was a long time before Kazan and Gray Wolf could draw forth their heads and breathe more freely.
10084{_}1815{_}Then they found that the finger of sand reaching out into the river had saved them.
10084{_}1816{_}Everywhere in that triangle between the two rivers the world had turned black, and was hot underfoot.
10084{_}1817{_}The smoke cleared away.
10084{_}1818{_}The wind changed again, and swung down cool and fresh from the west and north.
10084{_}1819{_}The fisher-cat was the first to move cautiously back to the forests that had been, but the porcupines were still rolled into balls when Gray Wolf and Kazan left the sand-bar.
10084{_}1820{_}They began to travel up-stream, and before night came, their feet were sore from hot ash and burning embers.
10084{_}1821{_}The moon was strange and foreboding that night, like a spatter of blood in the sky, and through the long silent hours there was not even the hoot of an owl to give a sign that life still existed where yesterday had been a paradise of wild things.
10084{_}1822{_}Kazan knew that there was nothing to hunt, and they continued to travel all that night.
10084{_}1823{_}With dawn they struck a narrow swamp along the edge of the stream.
10084{_}1824{_}Here beavers had built a dam, and they were able to cross over into the green country on the opposite side.
10084{_}1825{_}For another day and another night they traveled westward, and this brought them into the thick country of swamp and timber along the Waterfound.
10084{_}1826{_}And as Kazan and Gray Wolf came from the west, there came from the Hudson's Bay post to the east a slim dark-faced French half-breed by the name of Henri Loti, the most famous lynx hunter in all the Hudson's Bay country.
10084{_}1827{_}He was prospecting for "signs," and he found them in abundance along the Waterfound.
10084{_}1828{_}It was a game paradise, and the snow-shoe rabbit abounded in thousands.
10084{_}1829{_}As a consequence, the lynxes were thick, and Henri built his trapping shack, and then returned to the post to wait until the first snows fell, when he would come back with his team, supplies and traps.
10084{_}1830{_}And up from the south, at this same time, there was slowly working his way by canoe and trail a young university zoologist who was gathering material for a book on _The Reasoning of the Wild_.
10084{_}1831{_}His name was Paul Weyman, and he had made arrangements to spend a part of the winter with Henri Loti, the half-breed.
10084{_}1832{_}He brought with him plenty of paper, a camera and the photograph of a girl.
10084{_}1833{_}His only weapon was a pocket-knife.
10084{_}1834{_}And meanwhile Kazan and Gray Wolf found the home they were seeking in a thick swamp five or six miles from the cabin that Henri Loti had built.
10084{_}1835{_}CHAPTER XI  ALWAYS TWO BY TWO   It was January when a guide from the post brought Paul Weyman to Henri Loti's cabin on the Waterfound.
10084{_}1836{_}He was a man of thirty-two or three, full of the red-blooded life that made Henri like him at once.
10084{_}1837{_}If this had not been the case, the first few days in the cabin might have been unpleasant, for Henri was in bad humor.
10084{_}1838{_}He told Weyman about it their first night, as they were smoking pipes alongside the redly glowing box stove.
10084{_}1839{_}"It is damn strange," said Henri.
10084{_}1840{_}"I have lost seven lynx in the traps, torn to pieces like they were no more than rabbits that the foxes had killed.
10084{_}1841{_}No thing--not even bear--have ever tackled lynx in a trap before.
10084{_}1842{_}It is the first time I ever see it.
10084{_}1843{_}And they are torn up so bad they are not worth one half dollar at the post.
10084{_}1844{_}Seven!--that is over two hundred dollar I have lost!
10084{_}1845{_}There are two wolves who do it.
10084{_}1846{_}Two--I know it by the tracks--always two--an'--never one.
10084{_}1847{_}They follow my trap-line an' eat the rabbits I catch.
10084{_}1848{_}They leave the fisher-cat, an' the mink, an' the ermine, an' the marten; but the lynx--_sacre_ an' damn!--they jump on him an' pull the fur from him like you pull the wild cotton balls from the burn-bush!
10084{_}1849{_}I have tried strychnine in deer fat, an' I have set traps and deadfalls, but I can not catch them.
10084{_}1850{_}They will drive me out unless I get them, for I have taken only five good lynx, an' they have destroyed seven."
10084{_}1851{_}This roused Weyman.
10084{_}1852{_}He was one of that growing number of thoughtful men who believe that man's egoism, as a race, blinds him to many of the more wonderful facts of creation.
10084{_}1853{_}He had thrown down the gantlet, and with a logic that had gained him a nation-wide hearing, to those who believed that man was the only living creature who could reason, and that common sense and cleverness when displayed by any other breathing thing were merely instinct.
10084{_}1854{_}The facts behind Henri's tale of woe struck him as important, and until midnight they talked about the two strange wolves.
10084{_}1855{_}"There is one big wolf an' one smaller," said Henri.
10084{_}1856{_}"An' it is always the big wolf who goes in an' fights the lynx.
10084{_}1857{_}I see that by the snow.
10084{_}1858{_}While he's fighting, the smaller wolf makes many tracks in the snow just out of reach, an' then when the lynx is down, or dead, it jumps in an' helps tear it into pieces.
10084{_}1859{_}All that I know by the snow.
10084{_}1860{_}Only once have I seen where the smaller one went in an' fought with the other, an' then there was blood all about that was not lynx blood; I trailed the devils a mile by the dripping."
10084{_}1861{_}During the two weeks that followed, Weyman found much to add to the material of his book.
10084{_}1862{_}Not a day passed that somewhere along Henri's trap-line they did not see the trails of the two wolves, and Weyman observed that--as Henri had told him--the footprints were always two by two, and never one by one.
10084{_}1863{_}On the third day they came to a trap that had held a lynx, and at sight of what remained Henri cursed in both French and English until he was purple in the face.
10084{_}1864{_}The lynx had been torn until its pelt was practically worthless.
10084{_}1865{_}Weyman saw where the smaller wolf had waited on its haunches, while its companion had killed the lynx.
10084{_}1866{_}He did not tell Henri all he thought.
10084{_}1867{_}But the days that followed convinced him more and more that he had found the most dramatic exemplification of his theory.
10084{_}1868{_}Back of this mysterious tragedy of the trap-line there was a _reason_.
10084{_}1869{_}Why did the two wolves not destroy the fisher-cat, the ermine and the marten?
10084{_}1870{_}Why was their feud with the lynx alone?
10084{_}1871{_}Weyman was strangely thrilled.
10084{_}1872{_}He was a lover of wild things, and for that reason he never carried a gun.
10084{_}1873{_}And when he saw Henri placing poison-baits for the two marauders, he shuddered, and when, day after day, he saw that these poison-baits were untouched, he rejoiced.
10084{_}1874{_}Something in his own nature went out in sympathy to the heroic outlaw of the trap-line who never failed to give battle to the lynx.
10084{_}1875{_}Nights in the cabin he wrote down his thoughts and discoveries of the day.
10084{_}1876{_}One night he turned suddenly on Henri.
10084{_}1877{_}"Henri, doesn't it ever make you sorry to kill so many wild things?"
10084{_}1878{_}he asked.
10084{_}1879{_}Henri stared and shook his head.
10084{_}1880{_}"I kill t'ousand an' t'ousand," he said.
10084{_}1881{_}"I kill t'ousand more."
10084{_}1882{_}"And there are twenty thousand others just like you in this northern quarter of the continent--all killing, killing for hundreds of years back, and yet you can't kill out wild life.
10084{_}1883{_}The war of Man and the Beast, you might call it.
10084{_}1884{_}And, if you could return five hundred years from now, Henri, you'd still find wild life here.
10084{_}1885{_}Nearly all the rest of the world is changing, but you can't change these almost impenetrable thousands of square miles of ridges and swamps and forests.
10084{_}1886{_}The railroads won't come here, and I, for one, thank God for that.
10084{_}1887{_}Take all the great prairies to the west, for instance.
10084{_}1888{_}Why, the old buffalo trails are still there, plain as day--and yet, towns and cities are growing up everywhere.
10084{_}1889{_}Did you ever hear of North Battleford?"
10084{_}1890{_}"Is she near Montreal or Quebec?"
10084{_}1891{_}Henri asked.
10084{_}1892{_}Weyman smiled, and drew a photograph from his pocket.
10084{_}1893{_}It was the picture of a girl.
10084{_}1894{_}"No.
10084{_}1895{_}It's far to the west, in Saskatchewan.
10084{_}1896{_}Seven years ago I used to go up there every year, to shoot prairie chickens, coyotes and elk.
10084{_}1897{_}There wasn't any North Battleford then--just the glorious prairie, hundreds and hundreds of square miles of it.
10084{_}1898{_}There was a single shack on the Saskatchewan River, where North Battleford now stands, and I used to stay there.
10084{_}1899{_}In that shack there was a little girl, twelve years old.
10084{_}1900{_}We used to go out hunting together--for I used to kill things in those days.
10084{_}1901{_}And the little girl would cry sometimes when I killed, and I'd laugh at her.
10084{_}1902{_}"Then a railroad came, and then another, and they joined near the shack, and all at once a town sprang up.
10084{_}1903{_}Seven years ago there was only the shack there, Henri.
10084{_}1904{_}Two years ago there were eighteen hundred people.
10084{_}1905{_}This year, when I came through, there were five thousand, and two years from now there'll be ten thousand.
10084{_}1906{_}"On the ground where that shack stood are three banks, with a capital of forty million dollars; you can see the glow of the electric lights of the city twenty miles away.
10084{_}1907{_}It has a hundred-thousand dollar college, a high school, the provincial asylum, a fire department, two clubs, a board of trade, and it's going to have a street-car line within two years.
10084{_}1908{_}Think of that--all where the coyotes howled a few years ago!
10084{_}1909{_}"People are coming in so fast that they can't keep a census.
10084{_}1910{_}Five years from now there'll be a city of twenty thousand where the old shack stood.
10084{_}1911{_}And the little girl in that shack, Henri--she's a young lady now, and her people are--well, rich.
10084{_}1912{_}I don't care about that.
10084{_}1913{_}The chief thing is that she is going to marry me in the spring.
10084{_}1914{_}Because of her I stopped killing things when she was only sixteen.
10084{_}1915{_}The last thing I killed was a prairie wolf, and it had young.
10084{_}1916{_}Eileen kept the little puppy.
10084{_}1917{_}She's got it now--tamed.
10084{_}1918{_}That's why above all other wild things I love the wolves.
10084{_}1919{_}And I hope these two leave your trap-line safe."
10084{_}1920{_}Henri was staring at him.
10084{_}1921{_}Weyman gave him the picture.
10084{_}1922{_}It was of a sweet-faced girl, with deep pure eyes, and there came a twitch at the corners of Henri's mouth as he looked at it.
10084{_}1923{_}"My Iowaka died t'ree year ago," he said.
10084{_}1924{_}"She too loved the wild thing.
10084{_}1925{_}But them wolf--damn!
10084{_}1926{_}They drive me out if I can not kill them!"
10084{_}1927{_}He put fresh fuel into the stove, and prepared for bed.
10084{_}1928{_}One day the big idea came to Henri.
10084{_}1929{_}Weyman was with him when they struck fresh signs of lynx.
10084{_}1930{_}There was a great windfall ten or fifteen feet high, and in one place the logs had formed a sort of cavern, with almost solid walls on three sides.
10084{_}1931{_}The snow was beaten down by tracks, and the fur of rabbit was scattered about.
10084{_}1932{_}Henri was jubilant.
10084{_}1933{_}"We got heem--sure!"
10084{_}1934{_}he said.
10084{_}1935{_}He built the bait-house, set a trap and looked about him shrewdly.
10084{_}1936{_}Then he explained his scheme to Weyman.
10084{_}1937{_}If the lynx was caught, and the two wolves came to destroy it, the fight would take place in that shelter under the windfall, and the marauders would have to pass through the opening.
10084{_}1938{_}So Henri set five smaller traps, concealing them skilfully under leaves and moss and snow, and all were far enough away from the bait-house so that the trapped lynx could not spring them in his struggles.
10084{_}1939{_}"When they fight, wolf jump this way an' that--an' sure get in," said Henri.
10084{_}1940{_}"He miss one, two, t'ree--but he sure get in trap somewhere."
10084{_}1941{_}That same morning a light snow fell, making the work more complete, for it covered up all footprints and buried the telltale scent of man.
10084{_}1942{_}That night Kazan and Gray Wolf passed within a hundred feet of the windfall, and Gray Wolf's keen scent detected something strange and disquieting in the air.
10084{_}1943{_}She informed Kazan by pressing her shoulder against his, and they swung off at right angles, keeping to windward of the trap-line.
10084{_}1944{_}For two days and three cold starlit nights nothing happened at the windfall.
10084{_}1945{_}Henri understood, and explained to Weyman.
10084{_}1946{_}The lynx was a hunter, like himself, and also had its hunt-line, which it covered about once a week.
10084{_}1947{_}On the fifth night the lynx returned, went to the windfall, was lured straight to the bait, and the sharp-toothed steel trap closed relentlessly over its right hindfoot.
10084{_}1948{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf were traveling a quarter of a mile deeper in the forest when they heard the clanking of the steel chain as the lynx fought; to free itself.
10084{_}1949{_}Ten minutes later they stood in the door of the windfall cavern.
10084{_}1950{_}It was a white clear night, so filled with brilliant stars that Henri himself could have hunted by the light of them.
10084{_}1951{_}The lynx had exhausted itself, and lay crouching on its belly as Kazan and Gray Wolf appeared.
10084{_}1952{_}As usual, Gray Wolf held back while Kazan began the battle.
10084{_}1953{_}In the first or second of these fights on the trap-line, Kazan would probably have been disemboweled or had his jugular vein cut open, had the fierce cats been free.
10084{_}1954{_}They were more than his match in open fight, though the biggest of them fell ten pounds under his weight.
10084{_}1955{_}Chance had saved him on the Sun Rock.
10084{_}1956{_}Gray Wolf and the porcupine had both added to the defeat of the lynx on the sand-bar.
10084{_}1957{_}And along Henri's hunting line it was the trap that was his ally.
10084{_}1958{_}Even with his enemy thus shackled he took big chances.
10084{_}1959{_}And he took bigger chances than ever with the lynx under the windfall.
10084{_}1960{_}The cat was an old warrior, six or seven years old.
10084{_}1961{_}His claws were an inch and a quarter long, and curved like simitars.
10084{_}1962{_}His forefeet and his left hindfoot were free, and as Kazan advanced, he drew back, so that the trap-chain was slack under his body.
10084{_}1963{_}Here Kazan could not follow his old tactics of circling about his trapped foe, until it had become tangled in the chain, or had so shortened and twisted it that there was no chance for a leap.
10084{_}1964{_}He had to attack face to face, and suddenly he lunged in.
10084{_}1965{_}They met shoulder to shoulder.
10084{_}1966{_}Kazan's fangs snapped at the other's throat, and missed.
10084{_}1967{_}Before he could strike again, the lynx flung out its free hindfoot, and even Gray Wolf heard the ripping sound that it made.
10084{_}1968{_}With a snarl Kazan was flung back, his shoulder torn to the bone.
10084{_}1969{_}Then it was that one of Henri's hidden traps saved him from a second attack--and death.
10084{_}1970{_}Steel jaws snapped over one of his forefeet, and when he leaped, the chain stopped him.
10084{_}1971{_}Once or twice before, blind Gray Wolf had leaped in, when she knew that Kazan was in great danger.
10084{_}1972{_}For an instant she forgot her caution now, and as she heard Kazan's snarl of pain, she sprang in under the windfall.
10084{_}1973{_}Five traps Henri had hidden in the space in front of the bait-house, and Gray Wolf's feet found two of these.
10084{_}1974{_}She fell on her side, snapping and snarling.
10084{_}1975{_}In his struggles Kazan sprung the remaining two traps.
10084{_}1976{_}One of them missed.
10084{_}1977{_}The fifth, and last, caught him by a hindfoot.
10084{_}1978{_}This was a little past midnight.
10084{_}1979{_}From then until morning the earth and snow under the windfall were torn up by the struggles of the wolf, the dog and the lynx to regain their freedom.
10084{_}1980{_}And when morning came, all three were exhausted, and lay on their sides, panting and with bleeding jaws, waiting for the coming of man--and death.
10084{_}1981{_}Henri and Weyman were out early.
10084{_}1982{_}When they struck off the main line toward the windfall, Henri pointed to the tracks of Kazan and Gray Wolf, and his dark face lighted up with pleasure and excitement.
10084{_}1983{_}When they reached the shelter under the mass of fallen timber, both stood speechless for a moment, astounded by what they saw.
10084{_}1984{_}Even Henri had seen nothing like this before--two wolves and a lynx, all in traps, and almost within reach of one another's fangs.
10084{_}1985{_}But surprise could not long delay the business of Henri's hunter's instinct.
10084{_}1986{_}The wolves lay first in his path, and he was raising his rifle to put a steel-capped bullet through the base of Kazan's brain, when Weyman caught him eagerly by the arm.
10084{_}1987{_}Weyman was staring.
10084{_}1988{_}His fingers dug into Henri's flesh.
10084{_}1989{_}His eyes had caught a glimpse of the steel-studded collar about Kazan's neck.
10084{_}1990{_}"Wait!"
10084{_}1991{_}he cried.
10084{_}1992{_}"It's not a wolf.
10084{_}1993{_}It's a dog!"
10084{_}1994{_}Henri lowered his rifle, staring at the collar.
10084{_}1995{_}Weyman's eyes shot to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}1996{_}She was facing them, snarling, her white fangs bared to the foes she could not see.
10084{_}1997{_}Her blind eyes were closed.
10084{_}1998{_}Where there should have been eyes there was only hair, and an exclamation broke from Weyman's lips.
10084{_}1999{_}"Look!"
10084{_}2000{_}he commanded of Henri.
10084{_}2001{_}"What in the name of heaven--"  "One is dog--wild dog that has run to the wolves," said Henri.
10084{_}2002{_}"And the other is--wolf."
10084{_}2003{_}"And _blind_!"
10084{_}2004{_}gasped Weyman.
10084{_}2005{_}"_Oui_, blind, m'sieur," added Henri, falling partly into French in his amazement.
10084{_}2006{_}He was raising his rifle again.
10084{_}2007{_}Weyman seized it firmly.
10084{_}2008{_}[Illustration: "Wait!
10084{_}2009{_}it's not a wolf!"]
10084{_}2010{_}"Don't kill them, Henri," he said.
10084{_}2011{_}"Give them to me--alive.
10084{_}2012{_}Figure up the value of the lynx they have destroyed, and add to that the wolf bounty, and I will pay.
10084{_}2013{_}Alive, they are worth to me a great deal.
10084{_}2014{_}My God, a dog--and a blind wolf--_mates_!"
10084{_}2015{_}He still held Henri's rifle, and Henri was staring at him, as if he did not yet quite understand.
10084{_}2016{_}Weyman continued speaking, his eyes and face blazing.
10084{_}2017{_}"A dog--and a blind wolf--_mates_!"
10084{_}2018{_}he repeated.
10084{_}2019{_}"It is wonderful, Henri.
10084{_}2020{_}Down there, they will say I have gone beyond _reason_, when my book comes out.
10084{_}2021{_}But I shall have proof.
10084{_}2022{_}I shall take twenty photographs here, before you kill the lynx.
10084{_}2023{_}I shall keep the dog and the wolf alive.
10084{_}2024{_}And I shall pay you, Henri, a hundred dollars apiece for the two.
10084{_}2025{_}May I have them?"
10084{_}2026{_}Henri nodded.
10084{_}2027{_}He held his rifle in readiness, while Weyman unpacked his camera and got to work.
10084{_}2028{_}Snarling fangs greeted the click of the camera-shutter--the fangs of wolf and lynx.
10084{_}2029{_}But Kazan lay cringing, not through fear, but because he still recognized the mastery of man.
10084{_}2030{_}And when he had finished with his pictures, Weyman approached almost within reach of him, and spoke even more kindly to him than the man who had lived back in the deserted cabin.
10084{_}2031{_}Henri shot the lynx, and when Kazan understood this, he tore at the end of his trap-chains and snarled at the writhing body of his forest enemy.
10084{_}2032{_}By means of a pole and a babiche noose, Kazan was brought out from under the windfall and taken to Henri's cabin.
10084{_}2033{_}The two men then returned with a thick sack and more babiche, and blind Gray Wolf, still fettered by the traps, was made prisoner.
10084{_}2034{_}All the rest of that day Weyman and Henri worked to build a stout cage of saplings, and when it was finished, the two prisoners were placed in it.
10084{_}2035{_}Before the dog was put in with Gray Wolf, Weyman closely examined the worn and tooth-marked collar about his neck.
10084{_}2036{_}On the brass plate he found engraved the one word, "Kazan," and with a strange thrill made note of it in his diary.
10084{_}2037{_}After this Weyman often remained at the cabin when Henri went out on the trap-line.
10084{_}2038{_}After the second day he dared to put his hand between the sapling bars and touch Kazan, and the next day Kazan accepted a piece of raw moose meat from his hand.
10084{_}2039{_}But at his approach, Gray Wolf would always hide under the pile of balsam in the corner of their prison.
10084{_}2040{_}The instinct of generations and perhaps of centuries had taught her that man was her deadliest enemy.
10084{_}2041{_}And yet, this man did not hurt her, and Kazan was not afraid of him.
10084{_}2042{_}She was frightened at first; then puzzled, and a growing curiosity followed that.
10084{_}2043{_}Occasionally, after the third day, she would thrust her blind face out of the balsam and sniff the air when Weyman was at the cage, making friends with Kazan.
10084{_}2044{_}But she would not eat.
10084{_}2045{_}Weyman noted that, and each day he tempted her with the choicest morsels of deer and moose fat.
10084{_}2046{_}Five days--six--seven passed, and she had not taken a mouthful.
10084{_}2047{_}Weyman could count her ribs.
10084{_}2048{_}"She die," Henri told him on the seventh night.
10084{_}2049{_}"She starve before she eat in that cage.
10084{_}2050{_}She want the forest, the wild kill, the fresh blood.
10084{_}2051{_}She two--t'ree year old--too old to make civilize."
10084{_}2052{_}Henri went to bed at the usual hour, but Weyman was troubled, and sat up late.
10084{_}2053{_}He wrote a long letter to the sweet-faced girl at North Battleford, and then he turned out the light, and painted visions of her in the red glow of the fire.
10084{_}2054{_}He saw her again for that first time when he camped in the little shack where the fifth city of Saskatchewan now stood--with her blue eyes, the big shining braid, and the fresh glow of the prairies in her cheeks.
10084{_}2055{_}She had hated him--yes, actually hated him, because he loved to kill.
10084{_}2056{_}He laughed softly as he thought of that.
10084{_}2057{_}She had changed him--wonderfully.
10084{_}2058{_}He rose, opened the door, softly, and went out.
10084{_}2059{_}Instinctively his eyes turned westward.
10084{_}2060{_}The sky was a blaze of stars.
10084{_}2061{_}In their light he could see the cage, and he stood, watching and listening.
10084{_}2062{_}A sound came to him.
10084{_}2063{_}It was Gray Wolf gnawing at the sapling bars of her prison.
10084{_}2064{_}A moment later there came a low sobbing whine, and he knew that it was Kazan crying for his freedom.
10084{_}2065{_}Leaning against the side of the cabin was an ax.
10084{_}2066{_}Weyman seized it, and his lips smiled silently.
10084{_}2067{_}He was thrilled by a strange happiness, and a thousand miles away in that city on the Saskatchewan he could feel another spirit rejoicing with him.
10084{_}2068{_}He moved toward the cage.
10084{_}2069{_}A dozen blows, and two of the sapling bars were knocked out.
10084{_}2070{_}Then Weyman drew back.
10084{_}2071{_}Gray Wolf found the opening first, and she slipped out into the starlight like a shadow.
10084{_}2072{_}But she did not flee.
10084{_}2073{_}Out in the open space she waited for Kazan, and for a moment the two stood there, looking at the cabin.
10084{_}2074{_}Then they set off into freedom, Gray Wolf's shoulder at Kazan's flank.
10084{_}2075{_}Weyman breathed deeply.
10084{_}2076{_}"Two by two--always two by two, until death finds one of them," he whispered.
10084{_}2077{_}CHAPTER XII  THE RED DEATH   Kazan and Gray Wolf wandered northward into the Fond du Lac country, and were there when Jacques, a Hudson Bay Company's runner, came up to the post from the south with the first authentic news of the dread plague--the smallpox.
10084{_}2078{_}For weeks there had been rumors on all sides.
10084{_}2079{_}And rumor grew into rumor.
10084{_}2080{_}From the east, the south and the west they multiplied, until on all sides the Paul Reveres of the wilderness were carrying word that _La Mort Rouge_--the Red Death--was at their heels, and the chill of a great fear swept like a shivering wind from the edge of civilization to the bay.
10084{_}2081{_}Nineteen years before these same rumors had come up from the south, and the Red Terror had followed.
10084{_}2082{_}The horror of it still remained with the forest people, for a thousand unmarked graves, shunned like a pestilence, and scattered from the lower waters of James Bay to the lake country of the Athabasca, gave evidence of the toll it demanded.
10084{_}2083{_}Now and then in their wanderings Kazan and Gray Wolf had come upon the little mounds that covered the dead.
10084{_}2084{_}Instinct--something that was infinitely beyond the comprehension of man--made them _feel_ the presence of death about them, perhaps smell it in the air.
10084{_}2085{_}Gray Wolf's wild blood and her blindness gave her an immense advantage over Kazan when it came to detecting those mysteries of the air and the earth which the eyes were not made to see.
10084{_}2086{_}Each day that had followed that terrible moonlit night on the Sun Rock, when the lynx had blinded her, had added to the infallibility of her two chief senses--hearing and scent.
10084{_}2087{_}And it was she who discovered the presence of the plague first, just as she had scented the great forest fire hours before Kazan had found it in the air.
10084{_}2088{_}Kazan had lured her back to a trap-line.
10084{_}2089{_}The trail they found was old.
10084{_}2090{_}It had not been traveled for many days.
10084{_}2091{_}In a trap they found a rabbit, but it had been dead a long time.
10084{_}2092{_}In another there was the carcass of a fox, torn into bits by the owls.
10084{_}2093{_}Most of the traps were sprung.
10084{_}2094{_}Others were covered with snow.
10084{_}2095{_}Kazan, with his three-quarters strain of dog, ran over the trail from trap to trap, intent only on something alive--meat to devour.
10084{_}2096{_}Gray Wolf, in her blindness, scented _death_.
10084{_}2097{_}It shivered in the tree-tops above her.
10084{_}2098{_}She found it in every trap-house they came to--death--_man death_.
10084{_}2099{_}It grew stronger and stronger, and she whined, and nipped Kazan's flank.
10084{_}2100{_}And Kazan went on.
10084{_}2101{_}Gray Wolf followed him to the edge of the clearing in which Loti's cabin stood, and then she sat back on her haunches, raised her blind face to the gray sky, and gave a long and wailing cry.
10084{_}2102{_}In that moment the bristles began to stand up along Kazan's spine.
10084{_}2103{_}Once, long ago, he had howled before the tepee of a master who was newly dead, and he settled back on his haunches, and gave the death-cry with Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2104{_}He, too, scented it now.
10084{_}2105{_}Death was in the cabin, and over the cabin there stood a sapling pole, and at the end of the pole there fluttered a strip of red cotton rag--the warning flag of the plague from Athabasca to the bay.
10084{_}2106{_}This man, like a hundred other heroes of the North, had run up the warning before he laid himself down to die.
10084{_}2107{_}And that same night, in the cold light of the moon, Kazan and Gray Wolf swung northward into the country of the Fond du Lac.
10084{_}2108{_}There preceded them a messenger from the post on Reindeer Lake, who was passing up the warning that had come from Nelson House and the country to the southeast.
10084{_}2109{_}"There's smallpox on the Nelson," the messenger informed Williams, at Fond du Lac, "and it has struck the Crees on Wollaston Lake.
10084{_}2110{_}God only knows what it is doing to the Bay Indians, but we hear it is wiping out the Chippewas between the Albany and the Churchill."
10084{_}2111{_}He left the same day with his winded dogs.
10084{_}2112{_}"I'm off to carry word to the Reveillon people to the west," he explained.
10084{_}2113{_}Three days later, word came from Churchill that all of the company's servants and his majesty's subjects west of the bay should prepare themselves for the coming of the Red Terror.
10084{_}2114{_}Williams' thin face turned as white as the paper he held, as he read the words of the Churchill factor.
10084{_}2115{_}"It means dig graves," he said.
10084{_}2116{_}"That's the only preparation we can make."
10084{_}2117{_}He read the paper aloud to the men at Fond du Lac, and every available man was detailed to spread the warning throughout the post's territory.
10084{_}2118{_}There was a quick harnessing of dogs, and on each sledge that went out was a roll of red cotton cloth--rolls that were ominous of death, lurid signals of pestilence and horror, whose touch sent shuddering chills through the men who were about to scatter them among the forest people.
10084{_}2119{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf struck the trail of one of these sledges on the Gray Beaver, and followed it for half a mile.
10084{_}2120{_}The next day, farther to the west, they struck another, and on the fourth day still a third.
10084{_}2121{_}The last trail was fresh, and Gray Wolf drew back from it as if stung, her fangs snarling.
10084{_}2122{_}On the wind there came to them the pungent odor of smoke.
10084{_}2123{_}They cut at right angles to the trail, Gray Wolf leaping clear of the marks in the snow, and climbed to the cap of a ridge.
10084{_}2124{_}To windward of them, and down in the plain, a cabin was burning.
10084{_}2125{_}A team of huskies and a man were disappearing in the spruce forest.
10084{_}2126{_}Deep down in his throat Kazan gave a rumbling whine.
10084{_}2127{_}Gray Wolf stood as rigid as a rock.
10084{_}2128{_}In the cabin a plague-dead man was burning.
10084{_}2129{_}It was the law of the North.
10084{_}2130{_}And the mystery of the funeral pyre came again to Kazan and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2131{_}This time they did not howl, but slunk down into the farther plain, and did not stop that day until they had buried themselves deep in a dry and sheltered swamp ten miles to the north.
10084{_}2132{_}After this they followed the days and weeks which marked the winter of nineteen hundred and ten as one of the most terrible in all the history of the Northland--a single month in which wild life as well as human hung in the balance, and when cold, starvation and plague wrote a chapter in the lives of the forest people which will not be forgotten for generations to come.
10084{_}2133{_}In the swamp Kazan and Gray Wolf found a home under a windfall.
10084{_}2134{_}It was a small comfortable nest, shut in entirely from the snow and wind.
10084{_}2135{_}Gray Wolf took possession of it immediately.
10084{_}2136{_}She flattened herself out on her belly, and panted to show Kazan her contentment and satisfaction.
10084{_}2137{_}Nature again kept Kazan close at her side.
10084{_}2138{_}A vision came to him, unreal and dream-like, of that wonderful night under the stars--ages and ages ago, it seemed--when he had fought the leader of the wolf-pack, and young Gray Wolf had crept to his side after his victory and had given herself to him for mate.
10084{_}2139{_}But this mating season there was no running after the doe or the caribou, or mingling with the wild pack.
10084{_}2140{_}They lived chiefly on rabbit and spruce partridge, because of Gray Wolf's blindness.
10084{_}2141{_}Kazan could hunt those alone.
10084{_}2142{_}The hair had now grown over Gray Wolf's sightless eyes.
10084{_}2143{_}She had ceased to grieve, to rub her eyes with her paws, to whine for the sunlight, the golden moon and the stars.
10084{_}2144{_}Slowly she began to forget that she had ever seen those things.
10084{_}2145{_}She could now run more swiftly at Kazan's flank.
10084{_}2146{_}Scent and hearing had become wonderfully keen.
10084{_}2147{_}She could wind a caribou two miles distant, and the presence of man she could pick up at an even greater distance.
10084{_}2148{_}On a still night she had heard the splash of a trout half a mile away.
10084{_}2149{_}And as these two things--scent and hearing--became more and more developed in her, those same senses became less active in Kazan.
10084{_}2150{_}He began to depend upon Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2151{_}She would point out the hiding-place of a partridge fifty yards from their trail.
10084{_}2152{_}In their hunts she became the leader--until game was found.
10084{_}2153{_}And as Kazan learned to trust to her in the hunt, so he began just as instinctively to heed her warnings.
10084{_}2154{_}If Gray Wolf reasoned, it was to the effect that without Kazan she would die.
10084{_}2155{_}She had tried hard now and then to catch a partridge, or a rabbit, but she had always failed.
10084{_}2156{_}Kazan meant life to her.
10084{_}2157{_}And--if she reasoned--it was to make herself indispensable to her mate.
10084{_}2158{_}Blindness had made her different than she would otherwise have been.
10084{_}2159{_}Again nature promised motherhood to her.
10084{_}2160{_}But she did not--as she would have done in the open, and with sight--hold more and more aloof from Kazan as the days passed.
10084{_}2161{_}It was her habit, spring, summer and winter, to snuggle close to Kazan and lie with her beautiful head resting on his neck or back.
10084{_}2162{_}If Kazan snarled at her she did not snap back, but slunk down as though struck a blow.
10084{_}2163{_}With her warm tongue she would lick away the ice that froze to the long hair between Kazan's toes.
10084{_}2164{_}For days after he had run a sliver in his paw she nursed his foot.
10084{_}2165{_}Blindness had made Kazan absolutely necessary to her existence--and now, in a different way, she became more and more necessary to Kazan.
10084{_}2166{_}They were happy in their swamp home.
10084{_}2167{_}There was plenty of small game about them, and it was warm under the windfall.
10084{_}2168{_}Rarely did they go beyond the limits of the swamp to hunt.
10084{_}2169{_}Out on the more distant plains and the barren ridges they occasionally heard the cry of the wolf-pack on the trail of meat, but it no longer thrilled them with a desire to join in the chase.
10084{_}2170{_}One day they struck farther than usual to the west.
10084{_}2171{_}They left the swamp, crossed a plain over which a fire had swept the preceding year, climbed a ridge, and descended into a second plain.
10084{_}2172{_}At the bottom Gray Wolf stopped and sniffed the air.
10084{_}2173{_}At these times Kazan always watched her, waiting eagerly and nervously if the scent was too faint for him to catch.
10084{_}2174{_}But to-day he caught the edge of it, and he knew why Gray Wolf's ears flattened, and her hindquarters drooped.
10084{_}2175{_}The scent of game would have made her rigid and alert.
10084{_}2176{_}But it was not the game smell.
10084{_}2177{_}It was human, and Gray Wolf slunk behind Kazan and whined.
10084{_}2178{_}For several minutes they stood without moving or making a sound, and then Kazan led the way on.
10084{_}2179{_}Less than three hundred yards away they came to a thick clump of scrub spruce, and almost ran into a snow-smothered tepee.
10084{_}2180{_}It was abandoned.
10084{_}2181{_}Life and fire had not been there for a long time.
10084{_}2182{_}But from the tepee had come the man-smell.
10084{_}2183{_}With legs rigid and his spine quivering Kazan approached the opening to the tepee.
10084{_}2184{_}He looked in.
10084{_}2185{_}In the middle of the tepee, lying on the charred embers of a fire, lay a ragged blanket--and in the blanket was wrapped the body of a little Indian child.
10084{_}2186{_}Kazan could see the tiny moccasined feet.
10084{_}2187{_}But so long had death been there that he could scarcely smell the presence of it.
10084{_}2188{_}He drew back, and saw Gray Wolf cautiously nosing about a long and peculiarly shaped hummock in the snow.
10084{_}2189{_}She had traveled about it three times, but never approaching nearer than a man could have reached with a rifle barrel.
10084{_}2190{_}At the end of her third circle she sat down on her haunches, and Kazan went close to the hummock and sniffed.
10084{_}2191{_}Under that bulge in the snow, as well as in the tepee, there was death.
10084{_}2192{_}They slunk away, their ears flattened and their tails drooping until they trailed the snow, and did not stop until they reached their swamp home.
10084{_}2193{_}Even there Gray Wolf still sniffed the horror of the plague, and her muscles twitched and shivered as she lay close at Kazan's side.
10084{_}2194{_}That night the big white moon had around its edge a crimson rim.
10084{_}2195{_}It meant cold--intense cold.
10084{_}2196{_}Always the plague came in the days of greatest cold--the lower the temperature the more terrible its havoc.
10084{_}2197{_}It grew steadily colder that night, and the increased chill penetrated to the heart of the windfall, and drew Kazan and Gray Wolf closer together.
10084{_}2198{_}With dawn, which came at about eight o'clock, Kazan and his blind mate sallied forth into the day.
10084{_}2199{_}It was fifty degrees below zero.
10084{_}2200{_}About them the trees cracked with reports like pistol-shots.
10084{_}2201{_}In the thickest spruce the partridges were humped into round balls of feathers.
10084{_}2202{_}The snow-shoe rabbits had burrowed deep under the snow or to the heart of the heaviest windfalls.
10084{_}2203{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf found few fresh trails, and after an hour of fruitless hunting they returned to their lair.
10084{_}2204{_}Kazan, dog-like, had buried the half of a rabbit two or three days before, and they dug this out of the snow and ate the frozen flesh.
10084{_}2205{_}All that day it grew colder--steadily colder.
10084{_}2206{_}The night that followed was cloudless, with a white moon and brilliant stars.
10084{_}2207{_}The temperature had fallen another ten degrees, and nothing was moving.
10084{_}2208{_}Traps were never sprung on such nights, for even the furred things--the mink, and the ermine, and the lynx--lay snug in the holes and the nests they had found for themselves.
10084{_}2209{_}An increasing hunger was not strong enough to drive Kazan and Gray Wolf from their windfall.
10084{_}2210{_}The next day there was no break in the terrible cold, and toward noon Kazan set out on a hunt for meat, leaving Gray Wolf in the windfall.
10084{_}2211{_}Being three-quarters dog, food was more necessary to Kazan than to his mate.
10084{_}2212{_}Nature has fitted the wolf-breed for famine, and in ordinary temperature Gray Wolf could have lived for a fortnight without food.
10084{_}2213{_}At sixty degrees below zero she could exist a week, perhaps ten days.
10084{_}2214{_}Only thirty hours had passed sinee they had devoured the last of the frozen rabbit, and she was quite satisfied to remain in their snug retreat.
10084{_}2215{_}But Kazan was hungry.
10084{_}2216{_}He began to hunt in the face of the wind, traveling toward the burned plain.
10084{_}2217{_}He nosed about every windfall that he came to, and investigated the thickets.
10084{_}2218{_}A thin shot-like snow had fallen, and in this--from the windfall to the burn--he found but a single trail, and that was the trail of an ermine.
10084{_}2219{_}Under a windfall he caught the warm scent of a rabbit, but the rabbit was as safe from him there as were the partridges in the trees, and after an hour of futile digging and gnawing he gave up his effort to reach it.
10084{_}2220{_}For three hours he had hunted when he returned to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2221{_}He was exhausted.
10084{_}2222{_}While Gray Wolf, with the instinct of the wild, had saved her own strength and energy, Kazan had been burning up his reserve forces, and was hungrier than ever.
10084{_}2223{_}The moon rose clear and brilliant in the sky again that night, and Kazan set out once more on the hunt.
10084{_}2224{_}He urged Gray Wolf to accompany him, whining for her outside the windfall--returning for her twice--but Gray Wolf laid her ears aslant and refused to move.
10084{_}2225{_}The temperature had now fallen to sixty-five or seventy degrees below zero, and with it there came from the north an increasing wind, making the night one in which human life could not have existed for an hour.
10084{_}2226{_}By midnight Kazan was back under the windfall.
10084{_}2227{_}The wind grew stronger.
10084{_}2228{_}It began to wail in mournful dirges over the swamp, and then it burst in fierce shrieking volleys, with intervals of quiet between.
10084{_}2229{_}These were the first warnings from the great barrens that lay between the last lines of timber and the Arctic.
10084{_}2230{_}With morning the storm burst in all its fury from out of the north, and Gray Wolf and Kazan lay close together and shivered as they listened to the roar of it over the windfall.
10084{_}2231{_}Once Kazan thrust his head and shoulders out from the shelter of the fallen trees, but the storm drove him back.
10084{_}2232{_}Everything that possessed life had sought shelter, according to its way and instinct.
10084{_}2233{_}The furred creatures like the mink and the ermine were safest, for during the warmer hunting days they were of the kind that cached meat.
10084{_}2234{_}The wolves and the foxes had sought out the windfalls, and the rocks.
10084{_}2235{_}Winged things, with the exception of the owls, who were a tenth part body and nine-tenths feathers, burrowed under snow-drifts or found shelter in thick spruce.
10084{_}2236{_}To the hoofed and horned animals the storm meant greatest havoc.
10084{_}2237{_}The deer, the caribou and the moose could not crawl under windfalls or creep between rocks.
10084{_}2238{_}The best they could do was to lie down in the lee of a drift, and allow themselves to be covered deep with the protecting snow.
10084{_}2239{_}Even then they could not keep their shelter long, for they had to _eat_.
10084{_}2240{_}For eighteen hours out of the twenty-four the moose had to feed to keep himself alive during the winter.
10084{_}2241{_}His big stomach demanded quantity, and it took him most of his time to nibble from the tops of bushes the two or three bushels he needed a day.
10084{_}2242{_}The caribou required almost as much--the deer least of the three.
10084{_}2243{_}And the storm kept up that day, and the next, and still a third--three days and three nights--and the third day and night there came with it a stinging, shot-like snow that fell two feet deep on the level, and in drifts of eight and ten.
10084{_}2244{_}It was the "heavy snow" of the Indians--the snow that lay like lead on the earth, and under which partridges and rabbits were smothered in thousands.
10084{_}2245{_}On the fourth day after the beginning of the storm Kazan and Gray Wolf issued forth from the windfall.
10084{_}2246{_}There was no longer a wind--no more falling snow.
10084{_}2247{_}The whole world lay under a blanket of unbroken white, and it was intensely cold.
10084{_}2248{_}The plague had worked its havoc with men.
10084{_}2249{_}Now had come the days of famine and death for the wild things.
10084{_}2250{_}CHAPTER XIII  THE TRAIL OF HUNGER   Kazan and Gray Wolf had been a hundred and forty hours without food.
10084{_}2251{_}To Gray Wolf this meant acute discomfort, a growing weakness.
10084{_}2252{_}To Kazan it was starvation.
10084{_}2253{_}Six days and six nights of fasting had drawn in their ribs and put deep hollows in front of their hindquarters.
10084{_}2254{_}Kazan's eyes were red, and they narrowed to slits as he looked forth into the day.
10084{_}2255{_}Gray Wolf followed him this time when he went out on the hard snow.
10084{_}2256{_}Eagerly and hopefully they began the hunt in the bitter cold.
10084{_}2257{_}They swung around the edge of the windfall, where there had always been rabbits.
10084{_}2258{_}There were no tracks now, and no scent.
10084{_}2259{_}They continued in a horseshoe circle through the swamp, and the only scent they caught was that of a snow-owl perched up in a spruce.
10084{_}2260{_}They came to the burn and turned back, hunting the opposite side of the swamp.
10084{_}2261{_}On this side there was a ridge.
10084{_}2262{_}They climbed the ridge, and from the cap of it looked out over a world that was barren of life.
10084{_}2263{_}Ceaselessly Gray Wolf sniffed the air, but she gave no signal to Kazan.
10084{_}2264{_}On the top of the ridge Kazan stood panting.
10084{_}2265{_}His endurance was gone.
10084{_}2266{_}On their return through the swamp he stumbled over an obstacle which he tried to clear with a jump.
10084{_}2267{_}Hungrier and weaker, they returned to the windfall.
10084{_}2268{_}The night that followed was clear, and brilliant with stars.
10084{_}2269{_}They hunted the swamp again.
10084{_}2270{_}Nothing was moving--save one other creature, and that was a fox.
10084{_}2271{_}Instinct told them that it was futile to follow him.
10084{_}2272{_}It was then that the old thought of the cabin returned to Kazan.
10084{_}2273{_}Two things the cabin had always meant to him--warmth and food.
10084{_}2274{_}And far beyond the ridge was the cabin, where he and Gray Wolf had howled at the scent of death.
10084{_}2275{_}He did not think of man--or of that mystery which he had howled at.
10084{_}2276{_}He thought only of the cabin, and the cabin had always meant food.
10084{_}2277{_}He set off in a straight line for the ridge, and Gray Wolf followed.
10084{_}2278{_}They crossed the ridge and the burn beyond, and entered the edge of a second swamp.
10084{_}2279{_}Kazan was hunting listlessly now.
10084{_}2280{_}His head hung low.
10084{_}2281{_}His bushy tail dragged in the snow.
10084{_}2282{_}He was intent on the cabin--only the cabin.
10084{_}2283{_}It was his last hope.
10084{_}2284{_}But Gray Wolf was still alert, taking in the wind, and lifting her head whenever Kazan stopped to snuffle his chilled nose in the snow.
10084{_}2285{_}At last it came--the scent!
10084{_}2286{_}Kazan had moved on, but he stopped when he found that Gray Wolf was not following.
10084{_}2287{_}All the strength that was in his starved body revealed itself in a sudden rigid tenseness as he looked at his mate.
10084{_}2288{_}Her forefeet were planted firmly to the east; her slim gray head was reaching out for the scent; her body trembled.
10084{_}2289{_}Then--suddenly--they heard a sound, and with a whining cry Kazan set out in its direction, with Gray Wolf at his flank.
10084{_}2290{_}The scent grew stronger and stronger in Gray Wolf's nostrils, and soon it came to Kazan.
10084{_}2291{_}It was not the scent of a rabbit or a partridge.
10084{_}2292{_}It was big game.
10084{_}2293{_}They approached cautiously, keeping full in the wind.
10084{_}2294{_}The swamp grew thicker, the spruce more dense, and now--from a hundred yards ahead of them--there came a crashing of locked and battling horns.
10084{_}2295{_}Ten seconds more they climbed over a snowdrift, and Kazan stopped and dropped flat on his belly.
10084{_}2296{_}Gray Wolf crouched close at his side, her blind eyes turned to what she could smell but could not see.
10084{_}2297{_}Fifty yards from them a number of moose had gathered for shelter in the thick spruce.
10084{_}2298{_}They had eaten clear a space an acre in extent.
10084{_}2299{_}The trees were cropped bare as high as they could reach, and the snow was beaten hard under their feet.
10084{_}2300{_}There were six animals in the acre, two of them bulls--and these bulls were fighting, while three cows and a yearling were huddled in a group watching the mighty duel.
10084{_}2301{_}Just before the storm a young bull, sleek, three-quarters grown, and with the small compact antlers of a four-year-old, had led the three cows and the yearling to this sheltered spot among the spruce.
10084{_}2302{_}Until last night he had been master of the herd.
10084{_}2303{_}During the night the older bull had invaded his dominion.
10084{_}2304{_}The invader was four times as old as the young bull.
10084{_}2305{_}He was half again as heavy.
10084{_}2306{_}His huge palmate horns, knotted and irregular--but massive--spoke of age.
10084{_}2307{_}A warrior of a hundred fights, he had not hesitated to give battle in his effort to rob the younger bull of his home and family.
10084{_}2308{_}Three times they had fought since dawn, and the hard-trodden snow was red with blood.
10084{_}2309{_}The smell of it came to Kazan's and Gray Wolf's nostrils.
10084{_}2310{_}Kazan sniffed hungrily.
10084{_}2311{_}Queer sounds rolled up and down in Gray Wolf's throat, and she licked her jaws.
10084{_}2312{_}For a moment the two fighters drew a few yards apart, and stood with lowered heads.
10084{_}2313{_}The old bull had not yet won victory.
10084{_}2314{_}The younger bull represented youth and endurance; in the older bull those things were pitted against craft, greater weight, maturer strength--and a head and horns that were like a battering ram.
10084{_}2315{_}But in that great hulk of the older bull there was one other thing--age.
10084{_}2316{_}His huge sides were panting.
10084{_}2317{_}His nostrils were as wide as bells.
10084{_}2318{_}Then, as if some invisible spirit of the arena had given the signal, the animals came together again.
10084{_}2319{_}The crash of their horns could have been heard half a mile away, and under twelve hundred pounds of flesh and bone the younger hull went plunging back upon his haunches.
10084{_}2320{_}Then was when youth displayed itself.
10084{_}2321{_}In an instant he was up, and locking horns with his adversary.
10084{_}2322{_}Twenty times he had done this, and each attack had seemed filled with increasing strength.
10084{_}2323{_}And now, as if realizing that the last moments of the last fight had come, he twisted the old bull's neck and fought as he had never fought before.
10084{_}2324{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf both heard the sharp crack that followed--as if a dry stick had been stepped upon and broken.
10084{_}2325{_}It was February, and the hoofed animals were already beginning to shed their horns--especially the older bulls, whose palmate growths drop first.
10084{_}2326{_}This fact gave victory to the younger bull in the blood-stained arena a few yards from Gray Wolf and Kazan.
10084{_}2327{_}From its socket in the old bull's skull one of his huge antlers broke with that sharp snapping sound, and in another moment four inches of stiletto-like horn buried itself back of his foreleg.
10084{_}2328{_}In an instant all hope and courage left him, and he swung backward yard by yard, with the younger bull prodding his neck and shoulders until blood dripped from him in little streams.
10084{_}2329{_}At the edge of the clearing he flung himself free and crashed off into the forest.
10084{_}2330{_}The younger bull did not pursue.
10084{_}2331{_}He tossed his head, and stood for a few moments with heaving sides and dilated nostrils, facing in the direction his vanquished foe had taken.
10084{_}2332{_}Then he turned, and trotted back to the still motionless cows and yearling.
10084{_}2333{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf were quivering.
10084{_}2334{_}Gray Wolf slunk back from the edge of the clearing, and Kazan followed.
10084{_}2335{_}No longer were they interested in the cows and the young bull.
10084{_}2336{_}From that clearing they had seen meat driven forth--meat that was beaten in fight, and bleeding.
10084{_}2337{_}Every instinct of the wild pack returned to Gray Wolf now--and in Kazan the mad desire to taste the blood he smelled.
10084{_}2338{_}Swiftly they turned toward the blood-stained trail of the old bull, and when they came to it they found it spattered red.
10084{_}2339{_}Kazan's jaws dripped as the hot scent drove the blood like veins of fire through his weakened body.
10084{_}2340{_}His eyes were reddened by starvation, and in them there was a light now that they had never known even in the days of the wolf-pack.
10084{_}2341{_}He set off swiftly, almost forgetful of Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2342{_}But his mate no longer required his flank for guidance.
10084{_}2343{_}With her nose close to the trail she ran--ran as she had run in the long and thrilling hunts before blindness came.
10084{_}2344{_}Half a mile from the spruce thicket they came upon the old bull.
10084{_}2345{_}He had sought shelter behind a clump of balsam, and he stood over a growing pool of blood in the snow.
10084{_}2346{_}He was still breathing hard.
10084{_}2347{_}His massive head, grotesque now with its one antler, was drooping.
10084{_}2348{_}Flecks of blood dropped from his distended nostrils.
10084{_}2349{_}Even then, with the old bull weakened by starvation, exhaustion and loss of blood, a wolf-pack would have hung back before attacking.
10084{_}2350{_}Where they would have hesitated, Kazan leaped in with a snarling cry.
10084{_}2351{_}For an instant his fangs sunk into the thick hide of the bull's throat.
10084{_}2352{_}Then he was flung back--twenty feet.
10084{_}2353{_}Hunger gnawing at his vitals robbed him of all caution, and he sprang to the attack again--full at the bull's front--while Gray Wolf crept up unseen behind, seeking in her blindness the vulnerable part which nature had not taught Kazan to find.
10084{_}2354{_}This time Kazan was caught fairly on the broad palmate leaf of the bull's antler, and he was flung back again, half stunned.
10084{_}2355{_}In that same moment Gray Wolf's long white teeth cut like knives through one of the bull's rope-like hamstrings.
10084{_}2356{_}For thirty seconds she kept the hold, while the bull plunged wildly in his efforts to trample her underfoot.
10084{_}2357{_}Kazan was quick to learn, still quicker to be guided by Gray Wolf, and he leaped in again, snapping for a hold on the bulging cord just above the knee.
10084{_}2358{_}He missed, and as he lunged forward on his shoulders Gray Wolf was flung off.
10084{_}2359{_}But she had accomplished her purpose.
10084{_}2360{_}Beaten in open battle with one of his kind, and now attacked by a still deadlier foe, the old bull began to retreat.
10084{_}2361{_}As he went, one hip sank under him at every step.
10084{_}2362{_}The tendon of his left leg was bitten half through.
10084{_}2363{_}Without being able to see, Gray Wolf seemed to realize what had happened.
10084{_}2364{_}Again she was the pack-wolf--with all the old wolf strategy.
10084{_}2365{_}Twice flung back by the old bull's horn, Kazan knew better than to attack openly again.
10084{_}2366{_}Gray Wolf trotted after the bull, but he remained behind for a moment to lick up hungrily mouthfuls of the blood-soaked snow.
10084{_}2367{_}Then he followed, and ran close against Gray Wolf's side, fifty yards behind the bull.
10084{_}2368{_}There was more blood in the trail now--a thin red ribbon of it.
10084{_}2369{_}Fifteen minutes later the bull stopped again, and faced about, his great head lowered.
10084{_}2370{_}His eyes were red.
10084{_}2371{_}There was a droop to his neck and shoulders that spoke no longer of the unconquerable fighting spirit that had been a part of him for nearly a score of years.
10084{_}2372{_}No longer was he lord of the wilderness about him; no longer was there defiance in the poise of his splendid head, or the flash of eager fire in his bloodshot eyes.
10084{_}2373{_}His breath came with a gasping sound that was growing more and more distinct.
10084{_}2374{_}A hunter would have known what it meant.
10084{_}2375{_}The stiletto-point of the younger bull's antler had gone home, and the old bull's lungs were failing him.
10084{_}2376{_}More than once Gray Wolf had heard that sound in the early days of her hunting with the pack, and she understood.
10084{_}2377{_}Slowly she began to circle about the wounded monarch at a distance of about twenty yards.
10084{_}2378{_}Kazan kept at her side.
10084{_}2379{_}Once--twice--twenty times they made that slow circle, and with each turn they made the old bull turned, and his breath grew heavier and his head drooped lower.
10084{_}2380{_}Noon came, and was followed by the more intense cold of the last half of the day.
10084{_}2381{_}Twenty circles became a hundred--two hundred--and more.
10084{_}2382{_}Under Gray Wolf's and Kazan's feet the snow grew hard in the path they made.
10084{_}2383{_}Under the old bull's widespread hoofs the snow was no longer white--but red.
10084{_}2384{_}A thousand times before this unseen tragedy of the wilderness had been enacted.
10084{_}2385{_}It was an epoch of that life where life itself means the survival of the fittest, where to live means to kill, and to die means to perpetuate life.
10084{_}2386{_}At last, in that steady and deadly circling of Gray Wolf and Kazan, there came a time when the old bull did not turn--then a second, a third and a fourth time, and Gray Wolf seemed to know.
10084{_}2387{_}With Kazan she drew back from the hard-beaten trail, and they flattened themselves on their bellies under a dwarf spruce--and waited.
10084{_}2388{_}For many minutes the bull stood motionless, his hamstrung quarter sinking lower and lower.
10084{_}2389{_}And then with a deep blood-choked gasp he sank down.
10084{_}2390{_}For a long time Kazan and Gray Wolf did not move, and when at last they returned to the beaten trail the bull's heavy head was resting on the snow.
10084{_}2391{_}Again they began to circle, and now the circle narrowed foot by foot, until only ten yards--then nine--then eight--separated them from their prey.
10084{_}2392{_}The bull attempted to rise, and failed.
10084{_}2393{_}Gray Wolf heard the effort.
10084{_}2394{_}She heard him sink back and suddenly she leaped in swiftly and silently from behind.
10084{_}2395{_}Her sharp fangs buried themselves in the bull's nostrils, and with the first instinct of the husky, Kazan sprang for a throat hold.
10084{_}2396{_}This time he was not flung off.
10084{_}2397{_}It was Gray Wolf's terrible hold that gave him time to tear through the half-inch hide, and to bury his teeth deeper and deeper, until at last they reached the jugular.
10084{_}2398{_}A gush of warm blood spurted into his face.
10084{_}2399{_}But he did not let go.
10084{_}2400{_}Just as he had held to the jugular of his first buck on that moonlight night a long time ago, so he held to the old bull now.
10084{_}2401{_}It was Gray Wolf who unclamped his jaws.
10084{_}2402{_}She drew back, sniffing the air, listening.
10084{_}2403{_}Then, slowly, she raised her head, and through the frozen and starving wilderness there went her wailing triumphant cry--the call to meat.
10084{_}2404{_}For them the days of famine had passed.
10084{_}2405{_}CHAPTER XIV  THE RIGHT OF FANG   After the fight Kazan lay down exhausted in the blood-stained snow, while faithful Gray Wolf, still filled with the endurance of her wild wolf breed, tore fiercely at the thick skin on the bull's neck to lay open the red flesh.
10084{_}2406{_}When she had done this she did not eat, but ran to Kazan's side and whined softly as she muzzled him with her nose.
10084{_}2407{_}After that they feasted, crouching side by side at the bull's neck and tearing at the warm sweet flesh.
10084{_}2408{_}The last pale light of the northern day was fading swiftly into night when they drew back, gorged until there were no longer hollows in their sides.
10084{_}2409{_}The faint wind died away.
10084{_}2410{_}The clouds that had hung in the sky during the day drifted eastward, and the moon shone brilliant and clear.
10084{_}2411{_}For an hour the night continued to grow lighter.
10084{_}2412{_}To the brilliance of the moon and the stars there was added now the pale fires of the aurora borealis, shivering and flashing over the Pole.
10084{_}2413{_}Its hissing crackling monotone, like the creaking of steel sledge-runners on frost-filled snow, came faintly to the ears of Kazan and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2414{_}As yet they had not gone a hundred yards from the dead bull, and at the first sound of that strange mystery in the northern skies they stopped and listened to it, alert and suspicious.
10084{_}2415{_}Then they laid their ears aslant and trotted slowly back to the meat they had killed.
10084{_}2416{_}Instinct told them that it was theirs only by right of fang.
10084{_}2417{_}They had fought to kill it.
10084{_}2418{_}And it was in the law of the wild that they would have to fight to keep it.
10084{_}2419{_}In good hunting days they would have gone on and wandered under the moon and the stars.
10084{_}2420{_}But long days and nights of starvation had taught them something different now.
10084{_}2421{_}On that clear and stormless night following the days of plague and famine, a hundred thousand hungry creatures came out from their retreats to hunt for food.
10084{_}2422{_}For eighteen hundred miles east and west and a thousand miles north and south, slim gaunt-bellied creatures hunted under the moon and the stars.
10084{_}2423{_}Something told Kazan and Gray Wolf that this hunt was on, and never for an instant did they cease their vigilance.
10084{_}2424{_}At last they lay down at the edge of the spruce thicket, and waited.
10084{_}2425{_}Gray Wolf muzzled Kazan gently with her blind face.
10084{_}2426{_}The uneasy whine in her throat was a warning to him.
10084{_}2427{_}Then she sniffed the air, and listened--sniffed and listened.
10084{_}2428{_}Suddenly every muscle in their bodies grew rigid.
10084{_}2429{_}Something living had passed near them, something that they could not see or hear, and scarcely scent.
10084{_}2430{_}It came again, as mysterious as a shadow, and then out of the air there floated down as silently as a huge snowflake a great white owl.
10084{_}2431{_}Kazan saw the hungry winged creature settle on the bull's shoulder.
10084{_}2432{_}Like a flash he was out from his cover, Gray Wolf a yard behind him.
10084{_}2433{_}With an angry snarl he lunged at the white robber, and his jaws snapped on empty air.
10084{_}2434{_}His leap carried him clean over the bull.
10084{_}2435{_}He turned, but the owl was gone.
10084{_}2436{_}Nearly all of his old strength had returned to him now.
10084{_}2437{_}He trotted about the bull, the hair along his spine bristling like a brush, his eyes wide and menacing.
10084{_}2438{_}He snarled at the still air.
10084{_}2439{_}His jaws clicked, and he sat back on his haunches and faced the blood-stained trail that the moose had left before he died.
10084{_}2440{_}Again that instinct as infallible as reason told him that danger would come from there.
10084{_}2441{_}Like a red ribbon the trail ran back through the wilderness.
10084{_}2442{_}The little swift-moving ermine were everywhere this night, looking like white rats as they dodged about in the moonlight.
10084{_}2443{_}They were first to find the trail, and with all the ferocity of their blood-eating nature followed it with quick exciting leaps.
10084{_}2444{_}A fox caught the scent of it a quarter of a mile to windward, and came nearer.
10084{_}2445{_}From out of a deep windfall a beady-eyed, thin-bellied fisher-cat came forth, and stopped with his feet in the crimson ribbon.
10084{_}2446{_}It was the fisher-cat that brought Kazan out; from under his cover of spruce again.
10084{_}2447{_}In the moonlight there was a sharp quick fight, a snarling and scratching, a cat-like yowl of pain, and the fisher forgot his hunger in flight.
10084{_}2448{_}Kazan returned to Gray Wolf with a lacerated and bleeding nose.
10084{_}2449{_}Gray Wolf licked it sympathetically, while Kazan stood rigid and listening.
10084{_}2450{_}The fox swung swiftly away with the wind, warned by the sounds of conflict.
10084{_}2451{_}He was not a fighter, but a murderer who killed from behind, and a little later he leaped upon an owl and tore it into bits for the half-pound of flesh within the mass of feathers.
10084{_}2452{_}But nothing could drive back those little white outlaws of the wilderness--the ermine.
10084{_}2453{_}They would have stolen between the feet of man to get at the warm flesh and blood of the freshly killed bull.
10084{_}2454{_}Kazan hunted them savagely.
10084{_}2455{_}They were too quick for him, more like elusive flashes in the moonlight than things of life.
10084{_}2456{_}They burrowed under the old bull's body and fed while he raved and filled his mouth with snow.
10084{_}2457{_}Gray Wolf sat placidly on her haunches.
10084{_}2458{_}The little ermine did not trouble her, and after a time Kazan realized this, and flung himself down beside her, panting and exhausted.
10084{_}2459{_}For a long time after that the night was almost unbroken by sound.
10084{_}2460{_}Once in the far distance there came the cry of a wolf, and now and then, to punctuate the deathly silence, the snow owl hooted in blood-curdling protest from his home in the spruce-tops.
10084{_}2461{_}The moon was straight above the old bull when Gray Wolf scented the first real danger.
10084{_}2462{_}Instantly she gave the warning to Kazan and faced the bloody trail, her lithe body quivering, her fangs gleaming in the starlight, a snarling whine in her throat.
10084{_}2463{_}Only in the face of their deadliest enemy, the lynx--the terrible fighter who had blinded her long ago in that battle on the Sun Rock!--did she give such warning as this to Kazan.
10084{_}2464{_}He sprang ahead of her, ready for battle even before he caught the scent of the gray beautiful creature of death stealing over the trail.
10084{_}2465{_}Then came the interruption.
10084{_}2466{_}From a mile away there burst forth a single fierce long-drawn howl.
10084{_}2467{_}After all, that was the cry of the true master of the wilderness--the wolf.
10084{_}2468{_}It was the cry of hunger.
10084{_}2469{_}It was the cry that sent men's blood running more swiftly through their veins, that brought the moose and the deer to their feet shivering in every limb--the cry that wailed like a note of death through swamp and forest and over the snow-smothered ridges until its faintest echoes reached for miles into the starlit night.
10084{_}2470{_}There was silence, and in that awesome stillness Kazan and Gray Wolf stood shoulder to shoulder facing the cry, and in response to that cry there worked within them a strange and mystic change, for what they had heard was not a warning or a menace but the call of Brotherhood.
10084{_}2471{_}Away off there--beyond the lynx and the fox and the fisher-cat, were the creatures of their kind, the wild-wolf pack, to which the right to all flesh and blood was common--in which existed that savage socialism of the wilderness, the Brotherhood of the Wolf.
10084{_}2472{_}And Gray Wolf, setting back on her haunches, sent forth the response to that cry--a wailing triumphant note that told her hungry brethren there was feasting at the end of the trail.
10084{_}2473{_}And the lynx, between those two cries, sneaked off into the wide and moonlit spaces of the forest.
10084{_}2474{_}CHAPTER XV  A FIGHT UNDER THE STARS   On their haunches Kazan and Gray Wolf waited.
10084{_}2475{_}Five minutes passed, ten--fifteen--and Gray Wolf became uneasy.
10084{_}2476{_}No response had followed her call.
10084{_}2477{_}Again she howled, with Kazan quivering and listening beside her, and again there followed that dead stillness of the night.
10084{_}2478{_}This was not the way of the pack.
10084{_}2479{_}She knew that it had not gone beyond the reach of her voice and its silence puzzled her.
10084{_}2480{_}And then in a flash it came to them both that the pack, or the single wolf whose cry they had heard, was very near them.
10084{_}2481{_}The scent was warm.
10084{_}2482{_}A few moments later Kazan saw a moving object in the moonlight.
10084{_}2483{_}It was followed by another, and still another, until there were five slouching in a half-circle about them, seventy yards away.
10084{_}2484{_}Then they laid themselves flat in the snow and were motionless.
10084{_}2485{_}A snarl turned Kazan's eyes to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}2486{_}His blind mate had drawn back.
10084{_}2487{_}Her white fangs gleamed menacingly in the starlight.
10084{_}2488{_}Her ears were flat.
10084{_}2489{_}Kazan was puzzled.
10084{_}2490{_}Why was she signaling danger to him when it was the wolf, and not the lynx, out there in the snow?
10084{_}2491{_}And why did the wolves not come in and feast?
10084{_}2492{_}Slowly he moved toward them, and Gray Wolf called to him with her whine.
10084{_}2493{_}He paid no attention to her, but went on, stepping lightly, his head high in the air, his spine bristling.
10084{_}2494{_}In the scent of the strangers, Kazan was catching something now that was strangely familiar.
10084{_}2495{_}It drew him toward them more swiftly and when at last he stopped twenty yards from where the little group lay flattened in the snow, his thick brush waved slightly.
10084{_}2496{_}One of the animals sprang up and approached.
10084{_}2497{_}The others followed and in another moment Kazan was in the midst of them, smelling and smelled, and wagging his tail.
10084{_}2498{_}They were dogs, and not wolves.
10084{_}2499{_}In some lonely cabin in the wilderness their master had died, and they had taken to the forests.
10084{_}2500{_}They still bore signs of the sledge-traces.
10084{_}2501{_}About their necks were moose-hide collars.
10084{_}2502{_}The hair was worn short at their flanks, and one still dragged after him three feet of corded babiche trace.
10084{_}2503{_}Their eyes gleamed red and hungry in the glow of the moon and the stars.
10084{_}2504{_}They were thin, and gaunt and starved, and Kazan suddenly turned and trotted ahead of them to the side of the dead bull.
10084{_}2505{_}Then he fell back and sat proudly on his haunches beside Gray Wolf, listening to the snapping of jaws and the rending of flesh as the starved pack feasted.
10084{_}2506{_}Gray Wolf slunk closer to Kazan.
10084{_}2507{_}She muzzled his neck and Kazan gave her a swift dog-like caress of his tongue, assuring her that all was well.
10084{_}2508{_}She flattened herself in the snow when the dogs had finished and came up in their dog way to sniff at her, and make closer acquaintance with Kazan.
10084{_}2509{_}Kazan towered over her, guarding her.
10084{_}2510{_}One huge red-eyed dog who still dragged the bit of babiche trace muzzled Gray Wolf's soft neck for a fraction of a second too long, and Kazan uttered a savage snarl of warning.
10084{_}2511{_}The dog drew back, and for a moment their fangs gleamed over Gray Wolf's blind face.
10084{_}2512{_}It was the Challenge of the Breed.
10084{_}2513{_}The big husky was the leader of the pack, and if one of the other dogs had snarled at him, as Kazan snarled he would have leaped at his throat.
10084{_}2514{_}But in Kazan, standing fierce and half wild over Gray Wolf, he recognized none of the serfdom of the sledge-dogs.
10084{_}2515{_}It was master facing master; in Kazan it was more than that for he was Gray Wolf's mate.
10084{_}2516{_}In an instant more he would have leaped over her body to have fought for her, more than for the right of leadership.
