10084{_}3247{_}Thus clinched, with Kazan's long teeth buried almost to the beaver's jugular, they plunged down into the deep water of the pond.
10084{_}3248{_}Broken Tooth weighed sixty pounds.
10084{_}3249{_}The instant he struck the water he was in his element, and holding tenaciously to the grip he had obtained on Kazan's neck he sank like a chunk of iron.
10084{_}3250{_}Kazan was pulled completely under.
10084{_}3251{_}The water rushed into his mouth, his ears, eyes and nose.
10084{_}3252{_}He was blinded, and his senses were a roaring tumult.
10084{_}3253{_}But instead of struggling to free himself he held his breath and buried his teeth deeper.
10084{_}3254{_}They touched the soft bottom and for a moment floundered in the mud.
10084{_}3255{_}Then Kazan loosened his hold.
10084{_}3256{_}He was fighting for his own life now--and not for Broken Tooth's.
10084{_}3257{_}With all of the strength of his powerful limbs he struggled to break loose--to rise to the surface, to fresh air, to life.
10084{_}3258{_}He clamped his jaws shut, knowing that to breathe was to die.
10084{_}3259{_}On land he could have freed himself from Broken Tooth's hold without an effort.
10084{_}3260{_}But under water the old beaver's grip was more deadly than would have been the fangs of a lynx ashore.
10084{_}3261{_}There was a sudden swirl of water as a second beaver circled close about the struggling pair.
10084{_}3262{_}Had he closed in with Broken Tooth, Kazan's struggles would quickly have ceased.
10084{_}3263{_}But nature had not foreseen the day when Broken Tooth would be fighting with fang.
10084{_}3264{_}The old patriarch had no particular reason now for holding Kazan down.
10084{_}3265{_}He was not vengeful.
10084{_}3266{_}He did not thirst for blood or death.
10084{_}3267{_}Finding that he was free, and that this strange enemy that had twice leaped upon him could do him no harm, he loosed his hold.
10084{_}3268{_}It was not a moment too soon for Kazan.
10084{_}3269{_}He was struggling weakly when he rose to the surface of the water.
10084{_}3270{_}Three-quarters drowned, he succeeded in raising his forepaws over a slender branch that projected from the dam.
10084{_}3271{_}This gave him time to fill his lungs with air, and to cough forth the water that had almost ended his existence.
10084{_}3272{_}For ten minutes he clung to the branch before he dared attempt the short swim ashore.
10084{_}3273{_}When he reached the bank he dragged himself up weakly.
10084{_}3274{_}All the strength was gone from his body.
10084{_}3275{_}His limbs shook.
10084{_}3276{_}His jaws hung loose.
10084{_}3277{_}He was beaten--completely beaten.
10084{_}3278{_}And a creature without a fang had worsted him.
10084{_}3279{_}He felt the abasement of it.
10084{_}3280{_}Drenched and slinking, he went to the windfall, lay down in the sun, and waited for Gray Wolf.
10084{_}3281{_}Days followed in which Kazan's desire to destroy his beaver enemies became the consuming passion of his life.
10084{_}3282{_}Each day the dam became more formidable.
10084{_}3283{_}Cement work in the water was carried on by the beavers swiftly and safely.
10084{_}3284{_}The water in the pond rose higher each twenty-four hours, and the pond grew steadily wider.
10084{_}3285{_}The water had now been turned into the depression that encircled the windfall, and in another week or two, if the beavers continued their work, Kazan's and Gray Wolf's home would be nothing more than a small island in the center of a wide area of submerged swamp.
10084{_}3286{_}Kazan hunted only for food now, and not for pleasure.
10084{_}3287{_}Ceaselessly he watched his opportunity to leap upon incautious members of Broken Tooth's tribe.
10084{_}3288{_}The third day after the struggle under the water he killed a big beaver that approached too close to the willow thicket.
10084{_}3289{_}The fifth day two of the young beavers wandered into the flooded depression back of the windfall and Kazan caught them in shallow water and tore them into pieces.
10084{_}3290{_}After these successful assaults the beavers began to work mostly at night.
10084{_}3291{_}This was to Kazan's advantage, for he was a night-hunter.
10084{_}3292{_}On each of two consecutive nights he killed a beaver.
10084{_}3293{_}Counting the young, he had killed seven when the otter came.
10084{_}3294{_}Never had Broken Tooth been placed between two deadlier or more ferocious enemies than the two that now assailed him.
10084{_}3295{_}On shore Kazan was his master because of his swiftness, keener scent, and fighting trickery.
10084{_}3296{_}In the water the otter was a still greater menace.
10084{_}3297{_}He was swifter than the fish that he caught for food.
10084{_}3298{_}His teeth were like steel needles.
10084{_}3299{_}He was so sleek and slippery that it would have been impossible for them to hold him with their chisel-like teeth could they have caught him.
10084{_}3300{_}The otter, like the beaver, possessed no hunger for blood.
10084{_}3301{_}Yet in all the Northland he was the greatest destroyer of their kind--an even greater destroyer than man.
10084{_}3302{_}He came and passed like a plague, and it was in the coldest days of winter that greatest destruction came with him.
10084{_}3303{_}In those days he did not assault the beavers in their snug houses.
10084{_}3304{_}He did what man could do only with dynamite--made an embrasure through their dam.
10084{_}3305{_}Swiftly the water would fall, the surface ice would crash down, and the beaver houses would be left out of water.
10084{_}3306{_}Then followed death for the beavers--starvation and cold.
10084{_}3307{_}With the protecting water gone from about their houses, the drained pond a chaotic mass of broken ice, and the temperature forty or fifty degrees below zero, they would die within a few hours.
10084{_}3308{_}For the beaver, with his thick coat of fur, can stand less cold than man.
10084{_}3309{_}Through all the long winter the water about his home is as necessary to him as fire to a child.
10084{_}3310{_}But it was summer now and Broken Tooth and his colony had no very great fear of the otter.
10084{_}3311{_}It would cost them some labor to repair the damage he did, but there was plenty of food and it was warm.
10084{_}3312{_}For two days the otter frisked about the dam and the deep water of the pond.
10084{_}3313{_}Kazan took him for a beaver, and tried vainly to stalk him.
10084{_}3314{_}The otter regarded Kazan suspiciously and kept well out of his way.
10084{_}3315{_}Neither knew that the other was an ally.
10084{_}3316{_}Meanwhile the beavers continued their work with greater caution.
10084{_}3317{_}The water in the pond had now risen to a point where the engineers had begun the construction of three lodges.
10084{_}3318{_}On the third day the destructive instinct of the otter began its work.
10084{_}3319{_}He began to examine the dam, close down to the foundation.
10084{_}3320{_}It was not long before he found a weak spot to begin work on, and with his sharp teeth and small bullet-like head he commenced his drilling operations.
10084{_}3321{_}Inch by inch he worked his way through the dam, burrowing and gnawing over and under the timbers, and always through the cement.
10084{_}3322{_}The round hole he made was fully seven inches in diameter.
10084{_}3323{_}In six hours he had cut it through the five-foot base of the dam.
10084{_}3324{_}A torrent of water began to rush from the pond as if forced out by a hydraulic pump.
10084{_}3325{_}Kazan and Gray Wolf were hiding in the willows on the south side of the pond when this happened.
10084{_}3326{_}They heard the roar of the stream tearing through the embrasure and Kazan saw the otter crawl up to the top of the dam and shake himself like a huge water-rat.
10084{_}3327{_}Within thirty minutes the water in the pond had fallen perceptibly, and the force of the water pouring through the hole was constantly increasing the outlet.
10084{_}3328{_}In another half hour the foundations of the three lodges, which had been laid in about ten inches of water, stood on mud.
10084{_}3329{_}Not until Broken Tooth discovered that the water was receding from the houses did he take alarm.
10084{_}3330{_}He was thrown into a panic, and very soon every beaver in the colony tearing excitedly about the pond.
10084{_}3331{_}They swam swiftly from shore to shore, paying no attention to the dead-line now.
10084{_}3332{_}Broken Tooth and the older workmen made for the dam, and with a snarling cry the otter plunged down among them and out like a flash for the creek above the pond.
10084{_}3333{_}Swiftly the water continued to fall and as it fell the excitement of the beavers increased.
10084{_}3334{_}They forgot Kazan and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}3335{_}Several of the younger members of the colony drew themselves ashore on the windfall side of the pond, and whining softly Kazan was about to slip back through the willows when one of the older beavers waddled up through the deepening mud close on his ambush.
10084{_}3336{_}In two leaps Kazan was upon him, with Gray Wolf a leap behind him.
10084{_}3337{_}The short fierce struggle in the mud was seen by the other beavers and they crossed swiftly to the opposite side of the pond.
10084{_}3338{_}The water had receded to a half of its greatest width before Broken Tooth and his workmen discovered the breach in the wall of the dam.
10084{_}3339{_}The work of repair was begun at once.
10084{_}3340{_}For this work sticks and brush of considerable size were necessary, and to reach this material the beavers were compelled to drag their heavy bodies through the ten or fifteen yards of soft mud left by the falling water.
10084{_}3341{_}Peril of fang no longer kept them back.
10084{_}3342{_}Instinct told them that they were fighting for their existence--that if the embrasure were not filled up and the water kept in the pond they would very soon be completely exposed to their enemies.
10084{_}3343{_}It was a day of slaughter for Gray Wolf and Kazan.
10084{_}3344{_}They killed two more beavers in the mud close to the willows.
10084{_}3345{_}Then they crossed the creek below the dam and cut off three beavers in the depression behind the windfall.
10084{_}3346{_}There was no escape for these three.
10084{_}3347{_}They were torn into pieces.
10084{_}3348{_}Farther up the creek Kazan caught a young beaver and killed it.
10084{_}3349{_}Late in the afternoon the slaughter ended.
10084{_}3350{_}Broken Tooth and his courageous engineers had at last repaired the breach, and the water in the pond began to rise.
10084{_}3351{_}Half a mile up the creek the big otter was squatted on a log basking in the last glow of the setting sun.
10084{_}3352{_}To-morrow he would go and do over again his work of destruction.
10084{_}3353{_}That was his method.
10084{_}3354{_}For him it was play.
10084{_}3355{_}But that strange and unseen arbiter of the forests called O-ee-ki, "the Spirit," by those who speak the wild tongue, looked down at last with mercy upon Broken Tooth and his death-stricken tribe.
10084{_}3356{_}For in that last glow of sunset Kazan and Gray Wolf slipped stealthily up the creek--to find the otter basking half asleep on the log.
10084{_}3357{_}The day's work, a full stomach, and the pool of warm sunlight in which he lay had all combined to make the otter sleepy.
10084{_}3358{_}He was as motionless as the log on which he had stretched himself.
10084{_}3359{_}He was big and gray and old.
10084{_}3360{_}For ten years he had lived to prove his cunning superior to that of man.
10084{_}3361{_}Vainly traps had been set for him.
10084{_}3362{_}Wily trappers had built narrow sluice-ways of rock and tree in small streams for him, but the old otter had foiled their cunning and escaped the steel jaws waiting at the lower end of each sluice.
10084{_}3363{_}The trail he left in soft mud told of his size.
10084{_}3364{_}A few trappers had seen him.
10084{_}3365{_}His soft pelt would long ago have found its way to London, Paris or Berlin had it not been for his cunning.
10084{_}3366{_}He was fit for a princess, a duke or an emperor.
10084{_}3367{_}For ten years he had lived and escaped the demands of the rich.
10084{_}3368{_}But this was summer.
10084{_}3369{_}No trapper would have killed him now, for his pelt was worthless.
10084{_}3370{_}Nature and instinct both told him this.
10084{_}3371{_}At this season he did not dread man, for there was no man to dread.
10084{_}3372{_}So he lay asleep on the log, oblivious to everything but the comfort of sleep and the warmth of the sun.
10084{_}3373{_}Soft-footed, searching still for signs of the furry enemies who had invaded their domain, Kazan slipped along the creek.
10084{_}3374{_}Gray Wolf ran close at his shoulder.
10084{_}3375{_}They made no sound, and the wind was in their favor--bringing scents toward them.
10084{_}3376{_}It brought the otter smell.
10084{_}3377{_}To Kazan and Gray Wolf it was the scent of a water animal, rank and fishy, and they took it for the beaver.
10084{_}3378{_}They advanced still more cautiously.
10084{_}3379{_}Then Kazan saw the big otter asleep on the log and he gave the warning to Gray Wolf.
10084{_}3380{_}She stopped, standing with her head thrown up, while Kazan made his stealthy advance.
10084{_}3381{_}The otter stirred uneasily.
10084{_}3382{_}It was growing dusk.
10084{_}3383{_}The golden pool of sunlight had faded away.
10084{_}3384{_}Back in the darkening timber an owl greeted night with its first-low call.
10084{_}3385{_}The otter breathed deeply.
10084{_}3386{_}His whiskered muzzle twitched.
10084{_}3387{_}He was awakening--stirring--when Kazan leaped upon him.
10084{_}3388{_}Face to face, in fair fight, the old otter could have given a good account of himself.
10084{_}3389{_}But there was no chance now.
10084{_}3390{_}The wild itself had for the first time in his life become his deadliest enemy.
10084{_}3391{_}It was not man now--but O-ee-ki, "the Spirit," that had laid its hand upon him.
10084{_}3392{_}And from the Spirit there was no escape.
10084{_}3393{_}Kazan's fangs sank into his soft jugular.
10084{_}3394{_}Perhaps he died without knowing what it was that had leaped upon him.
10084{_}3395{_}For he died--quickly, and Kazan and Gray Wolf went on their way, hunting still for enemies to slaughter, and not knowing that in the otter they had killed the one ally who would have driven the beavers from their swamp home.
10084{_}3396{_}The days that followed grew more and more hopeless for Kazan and Gray Wolf.
10084{_}3397{_}With the otter gone Broken Tooth and his tribe held the winning hand.
10084{_}3398{_}Each day the water backed a little farther into the depression surrounding the windfall.
10084{_}3399{_}By the middle of July only a narrow strip of land connected the windfall hummock with the dry land of the swamp.
10084{_}3400{_}In deep water the beavers now worked unmolested.
10084{_}3401{_}Inch by inch the water rose, until there came the day when it began to overflow the connecting strip.
10084{_}3402{_}For the last time Kazan and Gray Wolf passed from their windfall home and traveled up the stream between the two ridges.
10084{_}3403{_}The creek held a new meaning for them now and as they traveled they sniffed its odors and listened to its sounds with an interest they had never known before.
10084{_}3404{_}It was an interest mingled a little with fear, for something in the manner in which the beavers had beaten them reminded Kazan and Gray Wolf of _man_.
10084{_}3405{_}And that night, when in the radiance of the big white moon they came within scent of the beaver colony that Broken Tooth had left, they turned quickly northward into the plains.
10084{_}3406{_}Thus had brave old Broken Tooth taught them to respect the flesh and blood and handiwork of his tribe.
10084{_}3407{_}CHAPTER XXI  A SHOT ON THE SAND-BAR   July and August of 1911 were months of great fires in the Northland.
10084{_}3408{_}The swamp home of Kazan and Gray Wolf, and the green valley between the two ridges, had escaped the seas of devastating flame; but now, as they set forth on their wandering adventures again, it was not long before their padded feet came in contact with the seared and blackened desolation that had followed so closely after the plague and starvation of the preceding winter.
10084{_}3409{_}In his humiliation and defeat, after being driven from his swamp home by the beavers, Kazan led his blind mate first into the south.
10084{_}3410{_}Twenty miles beyond the ridge they struck the fire-killed forests.
10084{_}3411{_}Winds from Hudson's Bay had driven the flames in an unbroken sea into the west, and they had left not a vestige of life or a patch of green.
10084{_}3412{_}Blind Gray Wolf could not see the blackened world, but she _sensed_ it.
10084{_}3413{_}It recalled to her memory of that other fire, after the battle on the Sun Rock; and all of her wonderful instincts, sharpened and developed by her blindness, told her that to the north--and not south--lay the hunting-grounds they were seeking.
10084{_}3414{_}The strain of dog that was in Kazan still pulled him south.
10084{_}3415{_}It was not because he sought man, for to man he had now become as deadly an enemy as Gray Wolf herself.
10084{_}3416{_}It was simply dog instinct to travel southward; in the face of fire it was wolf instinct to travel northward.
10084{_}3417{_}At the end of the third day Gray Wolf won.
10084{_}3418{_}They recrossed the little valley between the two ridges, and swung north and west into the Athabasca country, striking a course that would ultimately bring them to the headwaters of the McFarlane River.
10084{_}3419{_}Late in the preceding autumn a prospector had come up to Fort Smith, on the Slave River, with a pickle bottle filled with gold dust and nuggets.
10084{_}3420{_}He had made the find on the McFarlane.
10084{_}3421{_}The first mails had taken the news to the outside world, and by midwinter the earliest members of a treasure-hunting horde were rushing into the country by snow-shoe and dog-sledge.
10084{_}3422{_}Other finds came thick and fast.
10084{_}3423{_}The McFarlane was rich in free gold, and miners by the score staked out their claims along it and began work.
10084{_}3424{_}Latecomers swung to new fields farther north and east, and to Fort Smith came rumors of "finds" richer than those of the Yukon.
10084{_}3425{_}A score of men at first--then a hundred, five hundred, a thousand--rushed into the new country.
10084{_}3426{_}Most of these were from the prairie countries to the south, and from the placer beds of the Saskatchewan and the Frazer.
10084{_}3427{_}From the far North, traveling by way of the Mackenzie and the Liard, came a smaller number of seasoned prospectors and adventurers from the Yukon--men who knew what it meant to starve and freeze and die by inches.
10084{_}3428{_}One of these late comers was Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}3429{_}There were several reasons why Sandy had left the Yukon.
10084{_}3430{_}He was "in bad" with the police who patrolled the country west of Dawson, and he was "broke."
10084{_}3431{_}In spite of these facts he was one of the best prospectors that had ever followed the shores of the Klondike.
10084{_}3432{_}He had made discoveries running up to a million or two, and had promptly lost them through gambling and drink.
10084{_}3433{_}He had no conscience, and little fear.
10084{_}3434{_}Brutality was the chief thing written in his face.
10084{_}3435{_}His undershot jaw, his wide eyes, low forehead and grizzly mop of red hair proclaimed him at once as a man not to be trusted beyond one's own vision or the reach of a bullet.
10084{_}3436{_}It was suspected that he had killed a couple of men, and robbed others, but as yet the police had failed to get anything "on" him.
10084{_}3437{_}But along with this bad side of him, Sandy McTrigger possessed a coolness and a courage which even his worst enemies could not but admire, and also certain mental depths which his unpleasant features did not proclaim.
10084{_}3438{_}Inside of six months Red Gold City had sprung up on the McFarlane, a hundred and fifty miles from Fort Smith, and Fort Smith was five hundred miles from civilization.
10084{_}3439{_}When Sandy came he looked over the crude collection of shacks, gambling houses and saloons in the new town, and made up his mind that the time was not ripe for any of his "inside" schemes just yet.
10084{_}3440{_}He gambled a little, and won sufficient to buy himself grub and half an outfit.
10084{_}3441{_}A feature of this outfit was an old muzzle-loading rifle.
10084{_}3442{_}Sandy, who always carried the latest Savage on the market, laughed at it.
10084{_}3443{_}But it was the best his finances would allow of.
10084{_}3444{_}He started south--up the McFarlane.
10084{_}3445{_}Beyond a certain point on the river prospectors had found no gold.
10084{_}3446{_}Sandy pushed confidently _beyond_ this point.
10084{_}3447{_}Not until he was in new country did he begin his search.
10084{_}3448{_}Slowly he worked his way up a small tributary whose headwaters were fifty or sixty miles to the south and east.
10084{_}3449{_}Here and there he found fairly good placer gold.
10084{_}3450{_}He might have panned six or eight dollars' worth a day.
10084{_}3451{_}With this much he was disgusted.
10084{_}3452{_}Week after week he continued to work his way up-stream, and the farther he went the poorer his pans became.
10084{_}3453{_}At last only occasionally did he find colors.
10084{_}3454{_}After such disgusting weeks as these Sandy was dangerous--when in the company of others.
10084{_}3455{_}Alone he was harmless.
10084{_}3456{_}One afternoon he ran his canoe ashore on a white strip of sand.
10084{_}3457{_}This was at a bend, where the stream had widened, and gave promise of at least a few colors.
10084{_}3458{_}He had bent down close to the edge of the water when something caught his attention on the wet sand.
10084{_}3459{_}What he saw were the footprints of animals.
10084{_}3460{_}Two had come down to drink.
10084{_}3461{_}They had stood side by side.
10084{_}3462{_}And the footprints were fresh--made not more than an hour or two before.
10084{_}3463{_}A gleam of interest shot into Sandy's eyes.
10084{_}3464{_}He looked behind him, and up and down the stream.
10084{_}3465{_}"Wolves," he grunted.
10084{_}3466{_}"Wish I could 'a' shot at 'em with that old minute-gun back there.
10084{_}3467{_}Gawd--listen to that!
10084{_}3468{_}And in broad daylight, too!"
10084{_}3469{_}He jumped to his feet, staring off into the bush.
10084{_}3470{_}A quarter of a mile away Gray Wolf had caught the dreaded scent of man in the wind, and was giving voice to her warning.
10084{_}3471{_}It was a long wailing howl, and not until its last echoes had died away did Sandy McTrigger move.
10084{_}3472{_}Then he returned to the canoe, took out his old gun, put a fresh cap on the nipple and disappeared quickly over the edge of the bank.
10084{_}3473{_}For a week Kazan and Gray Wolf had been wandering about the headwaters of the McFarlane and this was the first time since the preceding winter that Gray Wolf had caught the scent of man in the air.
10084{_}3474{_}When the wind brought the danger-signal to her she was alone.
10084{_}3475{_}Two or three minutes before the scent came to her Kazan had left her side in swift pursuit of a snow-shoe rabbit, and she lay flat on her belly under a bush, waiting for him.
10084{_}3476{_}In these moments when she was alone Gray Wolf was constantly sniffing the air.
10084{_}3477{_}Blindness had developed her scent and hearing until they were next to infallible.
10084{_}3478{_}First she had heard the rattle of Sandy McTrigger's paddle against the side of his canoe a quarter of a mile away.
10084{_}3479{_}Scent had followed swiftly.
10084{_}3480{_}Five minutes after her warning howl Kazan stood at her side, his head flung up, his jaws open and panting.
10084{_}3481{_}Sandy had hunted Arctic foxes, and he was using the Eskimo tactics now, swinging in a half-circle until he should come up in the face of the wind.
10084{_}3482{_}Kazan caught a single whiff of the man-tainted air and his spine grew stiff.
10084{_}3483{_}But blind Gray Wolf was keener than the little red-eyed fox of the North.
10084{_}3484{_}Her pointed nose slowly followed Sandy's progress.
10084{_}3485{_}She heard a dry stick crack under his feet three hundred yards away.
10084{_}3486{_}She caught the metallic click of his gun-barrel as it struck a birch sapling.
10084{_}3487{_}The moment she lost Sandy in the wind she whined and rubbed herself against Kazan and trotted a few steps to the southwest.
10084{_}3488{_}At times such as this Kazan seldom refused to take guidance from her.
10084{_}3489{_}They trotted away side by side and by the time Sandy was creeping up snake-like with the wind in his face, Kazan was peering from the fringe of river brush down upon the canoe on the white strip of sand.
10084{_}3490{_}When Sandy returned, after an hour of futile stalking, two fresh tracks led straight down to the canoe.
10084{_}3491{_}He looked at them in amazement and then a sinister grin wrinkled his ugly face.
10084{_}3492{_}He chuckled as he went to his kit and dug out a small rubber bag.
10084{_}3493{_}From this he drew a tightly corked bottle, filled with gelatine capsules.
10084{_}3494{_}In each little capsule were five grains of strychnine.
10084{_}3495{_}There were dark hints that once upon a time Sandy McTrigger had tried one of these capsules by dropping it in a cup of coffee and giving it to a man, but the police had never proved it.
10084{_}3496{_}He was expert in the use of poison.
10084{_}3497{_}Probably he had killed a thousand foxes in his time, and he chuckled again as he counted out a dozen of the capsules and thought how easy it would be to get this inquisitive pair of wolves.
10084{_}3498{_}Two or three days before he had killed a caribou, and each of the capsules he now rolled up in a little ball of deer fat, doing the work with short sticks in place of his fingers, so that there would be no man-smell clinging to the death-baits.
10084{_}3499{_}Before sundown Sandy set out at right-angles over the plain, planting the baits.
10084{_}3500{_}Most of them he hung to low bushes.
10084{_}3501{_}Others he dropped in worn rabbit and caribou trails.
10084{_}3502{_}Then he returned to the creek and cooked his supper.
10084{_}3503{_}Then next morning he was up early, and off to the poison baits.
10084{_}3504{_}The first bait was untouched.
10084{_}3505{_}The second was as he had planted it.
10084{_}3506{_}The third was gone.
10084{_}3507{_}A thrill shot through Sandy as he looked about him.
10084{_}3508{_}Somewhere within a radius of two or three hundred yards he would find his game.
10084{_}3509{_}Then his glance fell to the ground under the bush where he had hung the poison capsule and an oath broke from his lips.
10084{_}3510{_}The bait had not been eaten.
10084{_}3511{_}The caribou fat lay scattered under the bush and still imbedded in the largest portion of it was the little white capsule--unbroken.
10084{_}3512{_}It was Sandy's first experience with a wild creature whose instincts were sharpened by blindness, and he was puzzled.
10084{_}3513{_}He had never known this to happen before.
10084{_}3514{_}If a fox or a wolf could be lured to the point of touching a bait, it followed that the bait was eaten.
10084{_}3515{_}Sandy went on to the fourth and the fifth baits.
10084{_}3516{_}They were untouched.
10084{_}3517{_}The sixth was torn to pieces, like the third.
10084{_}3518{_}In this instance the capsule was broken and the white powder scattered.
10084{_}3519{_}Two more poison baits Sandy found pulled down in this manner.
10084{_}3520{_}He knew that Kazan and Gray Wolf had done the work, for he found the marks of their feet in a dozen different places.
10084{_}3521{_}The accumulated bad humor of weeks of futile labor found vent in his disappointment and anger.
10084{_}3522{_}At last he had found something tangible to curse.
10084{_}3523{_}The failure of his poison baits he accepted as a sort of climax to his general bad luck.
10084{_}3524{_}Everything was against him, he believed, and he made up his mind to return to Red Gold City.
10084{_}3525{_}Early in the afternoon he launched his canoe and drifted down-stream with the current.
10084{_}3526{_}He was content to let the current do all of the work to-day, and he used his paddle just enough to keep his slender craft head on.
10084{_}3527{_}He leaned back comfortably and smoked his pipe, with the old rifle between his knees.
10084{_}3528{_}The wind was in his face and he kept a sharp watch for game.
10084{_}3529{_}It was late in the afternoon when Kazan and Gray Wolf came out on a sand-bar five or six miles down-stream.
10084{_}3530{_}Kazan was lapping up the cool water when Sandy drifted quietly around a bend a hundred yards above them.
10084{_}3531{_}If the wind had been right, or if Sandy had been using his paddle, Gray Wolf would have detected danger.
10084{_}3532{_}It was the metallic click-click of the old-fashioned lock of Sandy's rifle that awakened her to a sense of peril.
10084{_}3533{_}Instantly she was thrilled by the nearness of it.
10084{_}3534{_}Kazan heard the sound and stopped drinking to face it.
10084{_}3535{_}In that moment Sandy pressed the trigger.
10084{_}3536{_}A belch of smoke, a roar of gunpowder, and Kazan felt a red-hot stream of fire pass with the swiftness of a lightning-flash through his brain.
10084{_}3537{_}He stumbled back, his legs gave way under him, and he crumpled down in a limp heap.
10084{_}3538{_}Gray Wolf darted like a streak off into the bush.
10084{_}3539{_}Blind, she had not seen Kazan wilt down upon the white sand.
10084{_}3540{_}Not until she was a quarter of a mile away from the terrifying thunder of the white man's rifle did she stop and wait for him.
10084{_}3541{_}Sandy McTrigger grounded his canoe on the sand-bar with an exultant yell.
10084{_}3542{_}"Got you, you old devil, didn't I?"
10084{_}3543{_}he cried.
10084{_}3544{_}"I'd 'a' got the other, too, if I'd 'a' had something besides this damned old relic!"
10084{_}3545{_}He turned Kazan's head over with the butt of his gun, and the leer of satisfaction in his face gave place to a sudden look of amazement.
10084{_}3546{_}For the first time he saw the collar about Kazan's neck.
10084{_}3547{_}"My Gawd, it ain't a wolf," he gasped.
10084{_}3548{_}"It's a dog, Sandy McTrigger--_a dog!
10084{_}3549{_}"_     CHAPTER XXII  SANDY'S METHOD   McTrigger dropped on his knees in the sand.
10084{_}3550{_}The look of exultation was gone from his face.
10084{_}3551{_}He twisted the collar about the dog's limp neck until he came to the worn plate, on which he could make out the faintly engraved letters _K-a-z-a-n_.
10084{_}3552{_}He spelled the letters out one by one, and the look in his face was of one who still disbelieved what he had seen and heard.
10084{_}3553{_}"A dog!"
10084{_}3554{_}he exclaimed again.
10084{_}3555{_}"A dog, Sandy McTrigger an' a--a beauty!"
10084{_}3556{_}He rose to his feet and looked down on his victim.
10084{_}3557{_}A pool of blood lay in the white sand at the end of Kazan's nose.
10084{_}3558{_}After a moment Sandy bent over to see where his bullet had struck.
10084{_}3559{_}His inspection filled him with a new and greater interest.
10084{_}3560{_}The heavy ball from the muzzle-loader had struck Kazan fairly on top of the head.
10084{_}3561{_}It was a glancing blow that had not even broken the skull, and like a flash Sandy understood the quivering and twitching of Kazan's shoulders and legs.
10084{_}3562{_}He had thought that they were the last muscular throes of death.
10084{_}3563{_}But Kazan was not dying.
10084{_}3564{_}He was only stunned, and would be on his feet again in a few minutes.
10084{_}3565{_}Sandy was a connoisseur of dogs--of dogs that had worn sledge traces.
10084{_}3566{_}He had lived among them two-thirds of his life.
10084{_}3567{_}He could tell their age, their value, and a part of their history at a glance.
10084{_}3568{_}In the snow he could tell the trail of a Mackenzie hound from that of a Malemute, and the track of an Eskimo dog from that of a Yukon husky.
10084{_}3569{_}He looked at Kazan's feet.
10084{_}3570{_}They were wolf feet, and he chuckled.
10084{_}3571{_}Kazan was part wild.
10084{_}3572{_}He was big and powerful, and Sandy thought of the coming winter, and of the high prices that dogs would bring at Red Gold City.
10084{_}3573{_}He went to the canoe and returned with a roll of stout moose-hide babiche.
10084{_}3574{_}Then he sat down cross-legged in front of Kazan and began making a muzzle.
10084{_}3575{_}He did this by plaiting babiche thongs in the same manner that one does in making the web of a snow-shoe.
10084{_}3576{_}In ten minutes he had the muzzle over Kazan's nose and fastened securely about his neck.
10084{_}3577{_}To the dog's collar he then fastened a ten-foot rope of babiche.
10084{_}3578{_}After that he sat back and waited for Kazan to come to life.
10084{_}3579{_}When Kazan first lifted his head he could not see.
10084{_}3580{_}There was a red film before his eyes.
10084{_}3581{_}But this passed away swiftly and he saw the man.
10084{_}3582{_}His first instinct was to rise to his feet.
10084{_}3583{_}Three times he fell back before he could stand up.
10084{_}3584{_}Sandy was squatted six feet from him, holding the end of the babiche, and grinning.
10084{_}3585{_}Kazan's fangs gleamed back.
10084{_}3586{_}He growled, and the crest along his spine rose menacingly.
10084{_}3587{_}Sandy jumped to his feet.
10084{_}3588{_}"Guess I know what you're figgering on," he said.
10084{_}3589{_}"I've had _your_ kind before.
10084{_}3590{_}The dam' wolves have turned you bad, an' you'll need a whole lot of club before you're right again.
10084{_}3591{_}Now, look here."
10084{_}3592{_}Sandy had taken the precaution of bringing a thick club along with the babiche.
10084{_}3593{_}He picked it up from where he had dropped it in the sand.
10084{_}3594{_}Kazan's strength had fairly returned to him now.
10084{_}3595{_}He was no longer dizzy.
10084{_}3596{_}The mist had cleared away from his eyes.
10084{_}3597{_}Before him he saw once more his old enemy, man--man and the club.
10084{_}3598{_}All of the wild ferocity of his nature was roused in an instant.
10084{_}3599{_}Without reasoning he knew that Gray Wolf was gone, and that this man was accountable for her going.
10084{_}3600{_}He knew that this man had also brought him his own hurt, and what he ascribed to the man he also attributed to the club.
10084{_}3601{_}In his newer undertaking of things, born of freedom and Gray Wolf, Man and Club were one and inseparable.
10084{_}3602{_}With a snarl he leaped at Sandy.
10084{_}3603{_}The man was not expecting a direct assault, and before he could raise his club or spring aside Kazan had landed full on his chest.
10084{_}3604{_}The muzzle about Kazan's jaws saved him.
10084{_}3605{_}Fangs that would have torn his throat open snapped harmlessly.
10084{_}3606{_}Under the weight of the dog's body he fell back, as if struck down by a catapult.
10084{_}3607{_}As quick as a cat he was on his feet again, with the end of the babiche twisted several times about his hand.
10084{_}3608{_}Kazan leaped again, and this time he was met by a furious swing of the club.
10084{_}3609{_}It smashed against his shoulder, and sent him down in the sand.
10084{_}3610{_}Before he could recover Sandy was upon him, with all the fury of a man gone mad.
10084{_}3611{_}He shortened the babiche by twisting it again and again about his hand, and the club rose and fell with the skill and strength of one long accustomed to its use.
10084{_}3612{_}The first blows served only to add to Kazan's hatred of man, and the ferocity and fearlessness of his attacks.
10084{_}3613{_}Again and again he leaped in, and each time the club fell upon him with a force that threatened to break his bones.
10084{_}3614{_}There was a tense hard look about Sandy's cruel mouth.
10084{_}3615{_}He had never known a dog like this before, and he was a bit nervous, even with Kazan muzzled.
10084{_}3616{_}Three times Kazan's fangs would have sunk deep in his flesh had it not been for the babiche.
10084{_}3617{_}And if the thongs about his jaws should slip, or break--.
10084{_}3618{_}Sandy followed up the thought with a smashing blow that landed on Kazan's head, and once more the old battler fell limp upon the sand.
10084{_}3619{_}McTrigger's breath was coming in quick gasps.
10084{_}3620{_}He was almost winded.
10084{_}3621{_}Not until the club slipped from his hand did he realize how desperate the fight had been.
10084{_}3622{_}Before Kazan recovered from the blow that had stunned him Sandy examined the muzzle and strengthened it by adding another babiche thong.
10084{_}3623{_}Then he dragged Kazan to a log that high water had thrown up on the shore a few yards away and made the end of the babiche rope fast to a dead snag.
10084{_}3624{_}After that he pulled his canoe higher up on the sand, and began to prepare camp for the night.
10084{_}3625{_}For some minutes after Kazan's stunned senses had become normal he lay motionless, watching Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}3626{_}Every bone in his body gave him pain.
10084{_}3627{_}His jaws were sore and bleeding.
10084{_}3628{_}His upper lip was smashed where the club had fallen.
10084{_}3629{_}One eye was almost closed.
10084{_}3630{_}Several times Sandy came near, much pleased at what he regarded as the good results of the beating.
10084{_}3631{_}Each time he brought the club.
10084{_}3632{_}The third time he prodded Kazan with it, and the dog snarled and snapped savagely at the end of it.
10084{_}3633{_}That was what Sandy wanted--it was an old trick of the dog-slaver.
10084{_}3634{_}Instantly he was using the club again, until with a whining cry Kazan slunk under the protection of the snag to which he was fastened.
10084{_}3635{_}He could scarcely drag himself.
10084{_}3636{_}His right forepaw was smashed.
10084{_}3637{_}His hindquarters sank under him.
10084{_}3638{_}For a time after this second beating he could not have escaped had he been free.
10084{_}3639{_}Sandy was in unusually good humor.
10084{_}3640{_}"I'll take the devil out of you all right," he told Kazan for the twentieth time.
10084{_}3641{_}"There's nothin' like beatin's to make dogs an' wimmin live up to the mark.
10084{_}3642{_}A month from now you'll be worth two hundred dollars or I'll skin you alive!"
10084{_}3643{_}Three or four times before dusk Sandy worked to rouse Kazan's animosity.
10084{_}3644{_}But there was no longer any desire left in Kazan to fight.
10084{_}3645{_}His two terrific beatings, and the crushing blow of the bullet against his skull, had made him sick.
10084{_}3646{_}He lay with his head between his forepaws, his eyes closed, and did not see McTrigger.
10084{_}3647{_}He paid no attention to the meat that was thrown under his nose.
10084{_}3648{_}He did not know when the last of the sun sank behind the western forests, or when the darkness came.
10084{_}3649{_}But at last something roused him from his stupor.
10084{_}3650{_}To his dazed and sickened brain it came like a call from out of the far past, and he raised his head and listened.
10084{_}3651{_}Out on the sand McTrigger had built a fire, and the man stood in the red glow of it now, facing the dark shadows beyond the shoreline.
10084{_}3652{_}He, too, was listening.
10084{_}3653{_}What had roused Kazan came again now--the lost mourning cry of Gray Wolf far out on the plain.
10084{_}3654{_}With a whine Kazan was on his feet, tugging at the babiche.
10084{_}3655{_}Sandy snatched up his club, and leaped toward him.
10084{_}3656{_}"Down, you brute!"
10084{_}3657{_}he commanded.
10084{_}3658{_}In the firelight the club rose and fell with ferocious quickness.
10084{_}3659{_}When McTrigger returned to the fire he was breathing hard again.
10084{_}3660{_}He tossed his club beside the blankets he had spread out for a bed.
10084{_}3661{_}It was a different looking club now.
10084{_}3662{_}It was covered with blood and hair.
10084{_}3663{_}"Guess that'll take the spirit out of him," he chuckled.
10084{_}3664{_}"It'll do that--or kill 'im!"
10084{_}3665{_}Several times that night Kazan heard Gray Wolf's call.
10084{_}3666{_}He whined softly in response, fearing the club.
10084{_}3667{_}He watched the fire until the last embers of it died out, and then cautiously dragged himself from under the snag.
10084{_}3668{_}Two or three times he tried to stand on his feet, but fell back each time.
10084{_}3669{_}His legs were not broken, but the pain of standing on them was excruciating.
10084{_}3670{_}He was hot and feverish.
10084{_}3671{_}All that night he had craved a drink of water.
10084{_}3672{_}When Sandy crawled out from between his blankets in the early dawn he gave him both meat and water.
10084{_}3673{_}Kazan drank the water, but would not touch the meat.
10084{_}3674{_}Sandy regarded the change in him with satisfaction.
10084{_}3675{_}By the time the sun was up he had finished his breakfast and was ready to leave.
10084{_}3676{_}He approached Kazan fearlessly now, without the club.
10084{_}3677{_}Untying the babiche he dragged the dog to the canoe.
10084{_}3678{_}Kazan slunk in the sand while his captor fastened the end of the hide rope to the stern of the canoe.
10084{_}3679{_}Sandy grinned.
10084{_}3680{_}What was about to happen would be fun for him.
10084{_}3681{_}In the Yukon he had learned how to take the spirit out of dogs.
10084{_}3682{_}He pushed off, bow foremost.
10084{_}3683{_}Bracing himself with his paddle he then began to pull Kazan toward the water.
10084{_}3684{_}In a few moments Kazan stood with his forefeet planted in the damp sand at the edge of the stream.
10084{_}3685{_}For a brief interval Sandy allowed the babiche to fall slack.
10084{_}3686{_}Then with a sudden powerful pull he jerked Kazan out into the water.
10084{_}3687{_}Instantly he sent the canoe into midstream, swung it quickly down with the current, and began to paddle enough to keep the babiche taut about his victim's neck.
10084{_}3688{_}In spite of his sickness and injuries Kazan was now compelled to swim to keep his head above water.
10084{_}3689{_}In the wash of the canoe, and with Sandy's strokes growing steadily stronger, his position became each moment one of increasing torture.
10084{_}3690{_}At times his shaggy head was pulled completely under water.
10084{_}3691{_}At others Sandy would wait until he had drifted alongside, and then thrust him under with the end of his paddle.
10084{_}3692{_}He grew weaker.
10084{_}3693{_}At the end of a half-mile he was drowning.
10084{_}3694{_}Not until then did Sandy pull him alongside and drag him into the canoe.
10084{_}3695{_}The dog fell limp and gasping in the bottom.
10084{_}3696{_}Brutal though Sandy's methods had been, they had worked his purpose.
10084{_}3697{_}In Kazan there was no longer a desire to fight.
10084{_}3698{_}He no longer struggled for freedom.
10084{_}3699{_}He knew that this man was his master, and for the time his spirit was gone.
10084{_}3700{_}All he desired now was to be allowed to lie in the bottom of the canoe, out of reach of the club, and safe from the water.
10084{_}3701{_}The club lay between him and the man.
10084{_}3702{_}The end of it was within a foot or two of his nose, and what he smelled was his own blood.
10084{_}3703{_}For five days and five nights the journey down-stream continued, and McTrigger's process of civilizing Kazan was continued in three more beatings with the club, and another resort to the water torture.
10084{_}3704{_}On the morning of the sixth day they reached Red Gold City, and McTrigger put up his tent close to the river.
10084{_}3705{_}Somewhere he obtained a chain for Kazan, and after fastening the dog securely back of the tent he cut off the babiche muzzle.
10084{_}3706{_}"You can't put on meat in a muzzle," he told his prisoner.
10084{_}3707{_}"An' I want you to git strong--an' fierce as hell.
10084{_}3708{_}I've got an idee.
10084{_}3709{_}It's an idee you can lick your weight in wildcats.
10084{_}3710{_}We'll pull off a stunt pretty soon that'll fill our pockets with dust.
10084{_}3711{_}I've done it afore, and we can do it _here_.
10084{_}3712{_}Wolf an' dog--s'elp me Gawd but it'll be a drawin' card!"
10084{_}3713{_}Twice a day after this he brought fresh raw meat to Kazan.
10084{_}3714{_}Quickly Kazan's spirit and courage returned to him.
10084{_}3715{_}The soreness left his limbs.
10084{_}3716{_}His battered jaws healed.
10084{_}3717{_}And after the fourth day each time that Sandy came with meat he greeted him with the challenge of his snarling fangs.
10084{_}3718{_}McTrigger did not beat him now.
10084{_}3719{_}He gave him no fish, no tallow and meal--nothing but raw meat.
10084{_}3720{_}He traveled five miles up the river to bring in the fresh entrail of a caribou that had been killed.
10084{_}3721{_}One day Sandy brought another man with him and when the stranger came a step too near Kazan made a sudden swift lunge at him.
10084{_}3722{_}The man jumped back with a startled oath.
10084{_}3723{_}"He'll do," he growled.
10084{_}3724{_}"He's lighter by ten or fifteen pounds than the Dane, but he's got the teeth, an' the quickness, an' he'll give a good show before he goes under."
10084{_}3725{_}"I'll make you a bet of twenty-five per cent.
10084{_}3726{_}of my share that he don't go under," offered Sandy.
10084{_}3727{_}"Done!"
10084{_}3728{_}said the other.
10084{_}3729{_}"How long before he'll be ready?"
10084{_}3730{_}Sandy thought a moment.
10084{_}3731{_}"Another week," he said.
10084{_}3732{_}"He won't have his weight before then.
10084{_}3733{_}A week from to-day, we'll say.
10084{_}3734{_}Next Tuesday night.
10084{_}3735{_}Does that suit you, Harker?"
10084{_}3736{_}Harker nodded.
10084{_}3737{_}"Next Tuesday night," he agreed.
10084{_}3738{_}Then he added, "I'll make it a _half_ of my share that the Dane kills your wolf-dog."
10084{_}3739{_}Sandy took a long look at Kazan.
10084{_}3740{_}"I'll just take you on that," he said.
10084{_}3741{_}Then, as he shook Harker's hand, "I don't believe there's a dog between here and the Yukon that can kill the wolf!"
10084{_}3742{_}CHAPTER XXIII  PROFESSOR McGILL   Red Gold City was ripe for a night of relaxation.
10084{_}3743{_}There had been some gambling, a few fights and enough liquor to create excitement now and then, but the presence of the mounted police had served to keep things unusually tame compared with events a few hundred miles farther north, in the Dawson country.
10084{_}3744{_}The entertainment proposed by Sandy McTrigger and Jan Harker met with excited favor.
10084{_}3745{_}The news spread for twenty miles about Red Gold City and there had never been greater excitement in the town than on the afternoon and night of the big fight.
10084{_}3746{_}This was largely because Kazan and the huge Dane had been placed on exhibition, each dog in a specially made cage of his own, and a fever of betting began.
10084{_}3747{_}Three hundred men, each of whom was paying five dollars to see the battle, viewed the gladiators through the bars of their cages.
10084{_}3748{_}Harker's dog was a combination of Great Dane and mastiff, born in the North, and bred to the traces.
10084{_}3749{_}Betting favored him by the odds of two to one.
10084{_}3750{_}Occasionally it ran three to one.
10084{_}3751{_}At these odds there was plenty of Kazan money.
10084{_}3752{_}Those who were risking their money on him were the older wilderness men--men who had spent their lives among dogs, and who knew what the red glint in Kazan's eyes meant.
10084{_}3753{_}An old Kootenay miner spoke low in another's ear:  "I'd bet on 'im even.
10084{_}3754{_}I'd give odds if I had to.
10084{_}3755{_}He'll fight all around the Dane.
10084{_}3756{_}The Dane won't have no method."
10084{_}3757{_}"But he's got the weight," said the other dubiously.
10084{_}3758{_}"Look at his jaws, an' his shoulders--"  "An' his big feet, an' his soft throat, an' the clumsy thickness of his belly," interrupted the Kootenay man.
10084{_}3759{_}"For Gawd's sake, man, take my word for it, an' don't put your money on the Dane!"
10084{_}3760{_}Others thrust themselves between them.
10084{_}3761{_}At first Kazan had snarled at all these faces about him.
10084{_}3762{_}But now he lay back against the boarded side of the cage and eyed them sullenly from between his forepaws.
10084{_}3763{_}The fight was to be pulled off in Barker's place, a combination of saloon and cafe.
10084{_}3764{_}The benches and tables had been cleared out and in the center of the one big room a cage ten feet square rested on a platform three and a half feet from the floor.
10084{_}3765{_}Seats for the three hundred spectators were drawn closely around this.
10084{_}3766{_}Suspended just above the open top of the cage were two big oil lamps with glass reflectors.
10084{_}3767{_}It was eight o'clock when Harker, McTrigger and two other men bore Kazan to the arena by means of the wooden bars that projected from the bottom of his cage.
10084{_}3768{_}The big Dane was already in the fighting cage.
10084{_}3769{_}He stood blinking his eyes in the brilliant light of the reflecting lamps.
10084{_}3770{_}He pricked up his ears when he saw Kazan.
10084{_}3771{_}Kazan did not show his fangs.
10084{_}3772{_}Neither revealed the expected animosity.
10084{_}3773{_}It was the first they had seen of each other, and a murmur of disappointment swept the ranks of the three hundred men.
10084{_}3774{_}The Dane remained as motionless as a rock when Kazan was prodded from his own cage into the fighting cage.
10084{_}3775{_}He did not leap or snarl.
10084{_}3776{_}He regarded Kazan with a dubious questioning poise to his splendid head, and then looked again to the expectant and excited faces of the waiting men.
10084{_}3777{_}For a few moments Kazan stood stiff-legged, facing the Dane.
10084{_}3778{_}Then his shoulders dropped, and he, too, coolly faced the crowd that had expected a fight to the death.
10084{_}3779{_}A laugh of derision swept through the closely seated rows.
10084{_}3780{_}Catcalls, jeering taunts flung at McTrigger and Harker, and angry voices demanding their money back mingled with a tumult of growing discontent.
10084{_}3781{_}Sandy's face was red with mortification and rage.
10084{_}3782{_}The blue veins in Barker's forehead had swollen twice their normal size.
10084{_}3783{_}He shook his fist in the face of the crowd, and shouted:  "Wait!
10084{_}3784{_}Give 'em a chance, you dam' fools!"
10084{_}3785{_}At his words every voice was stilled.
10084{_}3786{_}Kazan had turned.
10084{_}3787{_}He was facing the huge Dane.
10084{_}3788{_}And the Dane had turned his eyes to Kazan.
10084{_}3789{_}Cautiously, prepared for a lunge or a sidestep, Kazan advanced a little.
10084{_}3790{_}The Dane's shoulders bristled.
10084{_}3791{_}He, too, advanced upon Kazan.
10084{_}3792{_}Four feet apart they stood rigid.
10084{_}3793{_}One could have heard a whisper in the room now.
10084{_}3794{_}Sandy and Harker, standing close to the cage, scarcely breathed.
10084{_}3795{_}Splendid in every limb and muscle, warriors of a hundred fights, and fearless to the point of death, the two half-wolf victims of man stood facing each other.
10084{_}3796{_}None could see the questioning look in their brute eyes.
10084{_}3797{_}None knew that in this thrilling moment the unseen hand of the wonderful Spirit God of the wilderness hovered between them, and that one of its miracles was descending upon them.
10084{_}3798{_}It was _understanding_.
10084{_}3799{_}Meeting in the open--rivals in the traces--they would have been rolling in the throes of terrific battle.
10084{_}3800{_}But _here_ came that mute appeal of brotherhood.
10084{_}3801{_}In the final moment, when only a step separated them, and when men expected to see the first mad lunge, the splendid Dane slowly raised his head and looked over Kazan's back through the glare of the lights.
10084{_}3802{_}Harker trembled, and under his breath he cursed.
10084{_}3803{_}The Dane's throat was open to Kazan.
10084{_}3804{_}But between the beasts had passed the voiceless pledge of peace.
10084{_}3805{_}Kazan did not leap.
10084{_}3806{_}He turned.
10084{_}3807{_}And shoulder to shoulder--splendid in their contempt of man--they stood and looked through the bars of their prison into the one of human faces.
10084{_}3808{_}A roar burst from the crowd--a roar of anger, of demand, of threat.
10084{_}3809{_}In his rage Harker drew a revolver and leveled it at the Dane.
10084{_}3810{_}Above the tumult of the crowd a single voice stopped him.
10084{_}3811{_}"Hold!"
10084{_}3812{_}it demanded.
10084{_}3813{_}"Hold--in the name of the law!"
10084{_}3814{_}For a moment there was silence.
10084{_}3815{_}Every face turned in the direction of the voice.
10084{_}3816{_}Two men stood on chairs behind the last row.
10084{_}3817{_}One was Sergeant Brokaw, of the Royal Northwest Mounted.
10084{_}3818{_}It was he who had spoken.
10084{_}3819{_}He was holding up a hand, commanding silence and attention.
10084{_}3820{_}On the chair beside him stood another man.
10084{_}3821{_}He was thin, with drooping shoulders, and a pale smooth face--a little man, whose physique and hollow cheeks told nothing of the years he had spent close up along the raw edge of the Arctic.
10084{_}3822{_}It was he who spoke now, while the sergeant held up his hand.
10084{_}3823{_}His voice was low and quiet:  "I'll give the owners five hundred dollars for those dogs," he said.
10084{_}3824{_}Every man in the room heard the offer.
10084{_}3825{_}Harker looked at Sandy.
10084{_}3826{_}For an instant their heads were close together.
10084{_}3827{_}"They won't fight, and they'll make good team-mates," the little man went on.
10084{_}3828{_}"I'll give the owners five hundred dollars."
10084{_}3829{_}Harker raised a hand.
10084{_}3830{_}"Make it six," he said.
10084{_}3831{_}"Make it six and they're yours."
10084{_}3832{_}The little man hesitated.
10084{_}3833{_}Then he nodded.
10084{_}3834{_}"I'll give you six hundred," he agreed.
10084{_}3835{_}Murmurs of discontent rose throughout the crowd.
10084{_}3836{_}Harker climbed to the edge of the platform.
10084{_}3837{_}"We ain't to blame because they wouldn't fight," he shouted, "but if there's any of you small enough to want your money back you can git it as you go out.
10084{_}3838{_}The dogs laid down on us, that's all.
10084{_}3839{_}We ain't to blame."
10084{_}3840{_}The little man was edging his way between the chairs, accompanied by the sergeant of police.
10084{_}3841{_}With his pale face close to the sapling bars of the cage he looked at Kazan and the big Dane.
10084{_}3842{_}"I guess we'll be good friends," he said, and he spoke so low that only the dogs heard his voice.
10084{_}3843{_}"It's a big price, but we'll charge it to the Smithsonian, lads.
10084{_}3844{_}I'm going to need a couple of four-footed friends of your moral caliber."
10084{_}3845{_}And no one knew why Kazan and the Dane drew nearer to the little scientist's side of the cage as he pulled out a big roll of bills and counted out six hundred dollars for Harker and Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}3846{_}CHAPTER XXIV  ALONE IN DARKNESS   Never had the terror and loneliness of blindness fallen upon Gray Wolf as in the days that followed the shooting of Kazan and his capture by Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}3847{_}For hours after the shot she crouched in the bush back from the river, waiting for him to come to her.
10084{_}3848{_}She had faith that he would come, as he had come a thousand times before, and she lay close on her belly, sniffing the air, and whining when it brought no scent of her mate.
10084{_}3849{_}Day and night were alike an endless chaos of darkness to her now, but she knew when the sun went down.
10084{_}3850{_}She sensed the first deepening shadows of evening, and she knew that the stars were out, and that the river lay in moonlight.
10084{_}3851{_}It was a night to roam, and after a time she moved restlessly about in a small circle on the plain, and sent out her first inquiring call for Kazan.
10084{_}3852{_}Up from the river came the pungent odor of smoke, and instinctively she knew that it was this smoke, and the nearness of man, that was keeping Kazan from her.
10084{_}3853{_}But she went no nearer than that first circle made by her padded feet.
10084{_}3854{_}Blindness had taught her to wait.
10084{_}3855{_}Since the day of the battle on the Sun Rock, when the lynx had destroyed her eyes, Kazan had never failed her.
10084{_}3856{_}Three times she called for him in the early night.
10084{_}3857{_}Then she made herself a nest under a _banskian_ shrub, and waited until dawn.
10084{_}3858{_}Just how she knew when night blotted out the last glow of the sun, so without seeing she knew when day came.
10084{_}3859{_}Not until she felt the warmth of the sun on her back did her anxiety overcome her caution.
10084{_}3860{_}Slowly she moved toward the river, sniffing the air and whining.
10084{_}3861{_}There was no longer the smell of smoke in the air, and she could not catch the scent of man.
10084{_}3862{_}She followed her own trail back to the sand-bar, and in the fringe of thick bush overhanging the white shore of the stream she stopped and listened.
10084{_}3863{_}After a little she scrambled down and went straight to the spot where she and Kazan were drinking when the shot came.
10084{_}3864{_}And there her nose struck the sand still wet and thick with Kazan's blood.
10084{_}3865{_}She knew it was the blood of her mate, for the scent of him was all about her in the sand, mingled with the man-smell of Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}3866{_}She sniffed the trail of his body to the edge of the stream, where Sandy had dragged him to the canoe.
10084{_}3867{_}She found the fallen tree to which he had been tied.
10084{_}3868{_}And then she came upon one of the two clubs that Sandy had used to beat wounded Kazan into submissiveness.
10084{_}3869{_}It was covered with blood and hair, and all at once Gray Wolf lay back on her haunches and turned her blind face to the sky, and there rose from her throat a cry for Kazan that drifted for miles on the wings of the south wind.
10084{_}3870{_}Never had Gray Wolf given quite that cry before.
10084{_}3871{_}It was not the "call" that comes with the moonlit nights, and neither was it the hunt-cry, nor the she-wolf's yearning for matehood.
10084{_}3872{_}It carried with it the lament of death.
10084{_}3873{_}And after that one cry Gray Wolf slunk back to the fringe of bush over the river, and lay with her face turned to the stream.
10084{_}3874{_}A strange terror fell upon her.
10084{_}3875{_}She had grown accustomed to darkness, but never before had she been _alone_ in that darkness.
10084{_}3876{_}Always there had been the guardianship of Kazan's presence.
10084{_}3877{_}She heard the clucking sound of a spruce hen in the bush a few yards away, and now that sound came to her as if from out of another world.
10084{_}3878{_}A ground-mouse rustled through the grass close to her forepaws, and she snapped at it, and closed her teeth on a rock.
10084{_}3879{_}The muscles of her shoulders twitched tremulously and she shivered as if stricken by intense cold.
10084{_}3880{_}She was terrified by the darkness that shut out the world from her, and she pawed at her closed eyes, as if she might open them to light.
10084{_}3881{_}Early in the afternoon she wandered back on the plain.
10084{_}3882{_}It was different.
10084{_}3883{_}It frightened her, and soon she returned to the beach, and snuggled down under the tree where Kazan had lain.
10084{_}3884{_}She was not so frightened here.
10084{_}3885{_}The smell of Kazan was strong about her.
10084{_}3886{_}For an hour she lay motionless, with her head resting on the club clotted with his hair and blood.
10084{_}3887{_}Night found her still there.
10084{_}3888{_}And when the moon and the stars came out she crawled back into the pit in the white sand that Kazan's body had made under the tree.
10084{_}3889{_}With dawn she went down to the edge of the stream to drink.
10084{_}3890{_}She could not see that the day was almost as dark as night, and that the gray-black sky was a chaos of slumbering storm.
10084{_}3891{_}But she could smell the presence of it in the thick air, and could _feel_ the forked flashes of lightning that rolled up with the dense pall from the south and west.
10084{_}3892{_}The distant rumbling of thunder grew louder, and she huddled herself again under the tree.
10084{_}3893{_}For hours the storm crashed over her, and the rain fell in a deluge.
10084{_}3894{_}When it had finished she slunk out from her shelter like a thing beaten.
10084{_}3895{_}Vainly she sought for one last scent of Kazan.
10084{_}3896{_}The club was washed clean.
10084{_}3897{_}Again the sand was white where Kazan's blood had reddened it.
10084{_}3898{_}Even under the tree there was no sign of him left.
10084{_}3899{_}Until now only the terror of being alone in the pit of darkness that enveloped her had oppressed Gray Wolf.
10084{_}3900{_}With afternoon came hunger.
10084{_}3901{_}It was this hunger that drew her from the sand-bar, and she wandered back into the plain.
10084{_}3902{_}A dozen times she scented game, and each time it evaded her.
10084{_}3903{_}Even a ground-mouse that she cornered under a root, and dug out with her paws, escaped her fangs.
10084{_}3904{_}Thirty-six hours before this Kazan and Gray Wolf had left a half of their last kill a mile of two farther back on the plain.
10084{_}3905{_}The kill was one of the big barren rabbits, and Gray Wolf turned in its direction.
10084{_}3906{_}She did not require sight to find it.
10084{_}3907{_}In her was developed to its finest point that sixth sense of the animal kingdom, the sense of orientation, and as straight as a pigeon might have winged its flight she cut through the bush to the spot where they had cached the rabbit.
10084{_}3908{_}A white fox had been there ahead of her, and she found only scattered bits of hair and fur.
10084{_}3909{_}What the fox had left the moose-birds and bush-jays had carried away.
10084{_}3910{_}Hungrily Gray Wolf turned back to the river.
10084{_}3911{_}That night she slept again where Kazan had lain, and three times she called for him without answer.
10084{_}3912{_}A heavy dew fell, and it drenched the last vestige of her mate's scent out of the sand.
10084{_}3913{_}But still through the day that followed, and the day that followed that, blind Gray Wolf clung to the narrow rim of white sand.
10084{_}3914{_}On the fourth day her hunger reached a point where she gnawed the bark from willow bushes.
10084{_}3915{_}It was on this day that she made a discovery.
10084{_}3916{_}She was drinking, when her sensitive nose touched something in the water's edge that was smooth, and bore a faint odor of flesh.
10084{_}3917{_}It was one of the big northern river clams.
10084{_}3918{_}She pawed it ashore, sniffing at the hard shell.
10084{_}3919{_}Then she crunched it between her teeth.
10084{_}3920{_}She had never tasted sweeter meat than that which she found inside, and she began hunting for other clams.
10084{_}3921{_}She found many of them, and ate until she was no longer hungry.
10084{_}3922{_}For three days more she remained on the bar.
10084{_}3923{_}And then, one night, the call came to her.
10084{_}3924{_}It set her quivering with a strange new excitement--something that may have been a new hope, and in the moonlight she trotted nervously up and down the shining strip of sand, facing now the north, and now the south, and then the east and the west--her head flung up, listening, as if in the soft wind of the night she was trying to locate the whispering lure of a wonderful voice.
10084{_}3925{_}And whatever it was that came to her came from out of the south and east.
10084{_}3926{_}Off there--across the barren, far beyond the outer edge of the northern timber-line--was _home_.
10084{_}3927{_}And off there, in her brute way, she reasoned that she must find Kazan.
10084{_}3928{_}The call did not come from their old windfall home in the swamp.
10084{_}3929{_}It came from beyond that, and in a flashing vision there rose through her blindness a picture of the towering Sun Rock, of the winding trail that led to it, and the cabin on the plain.
10084{_}3930{_}It was there that blindness had come to her.
10084{_}3931{_}It was there that day had ended, and eternal night had begun.
10084{_}3932{_}And it was there that she had mothered her first-born.
10084{_}3933{_}Nature had registered these things so that they could never be wiped out of her memory, and when the call came it was from the sunlit world where she had last known light and life and had last seen the moon and the stars in the blue night of the skies.
10084{_}3934{_}And to that call she responded, leaving the river and its food behind her--straight out into the face of darkness and starvation, no longer fearing death or the emptiness of the world she could not see; for ahead of her, two hundred miles away, she could see the Sun Rock, the winding trail, the nest of her first-born between the two big rocks--_and Kazan_!
10084{_}3935{_}CHAPTER XXV  THE LAST OF McTRIGGER   Sixty miles farther north Kazan lay at the end of his fine steel chain, watching little Professor McGill mixing a pail of tallow and bran.
10084{_}3936{_}A dozen yards from him lay the big Dane, his huge jaws drooling in anticipation of the unusual feast which McGill was preparing.
10084{_}3937{_}He showed signs of pleasure when McGill approached him with a quart of the mixture, and he gulped it between his huge jaws.
10084{_}3938{_}The little man with the cold blue eyes and the gray-blond hair stroked his back without fear.
10084{_}3939{_}His attitude was different when he turned to Kazan.
10084{_}3940{_}His movements were filled with caution, and yet his eyes and his lips were smiling, and he gave the wolf-dog no evidence of his fear, if it could be called fear.
10084{_}3941{_}The little professor, who was up in the north country for the Smithsonian Institution, had spent a third of his life among dogs.
10084{_}3942{_}He loved them, and understood them.
10084{_}3943{_}He had written a number of magazine articles on dog intellect that had attracted wide attention among naturalists.
10084{_}3944{_}It was largely because he loved dogs, and understood them more than most men, that he had bought Kazan and the big Dane on the night when Sandy McTrigger and his partner had tried to get them to fight to the death in the Red Gold City saloon.
10084{_}3945{_}The refusal of the two splendid beasts to kill each other for the pleasure of the three hundred men who had assembled to witness the fight delighted him.
10084{_}3946{_}He had already planned a paper on the incident.
10084{_}3947{_}Sandy had told him the story of Kazan's capture, and of his wild mate, Gray Wolf, and the professor had asked him a thousand questions.
10084{_}3948{_}But each day Kazan puzzled him more.
10084{_}3949{_}No amount of kindness on his part could bring a responsive gleam in Kazan's eyes.
10084{_}3950{_}Not once did Kazan signify a willingness to become friends.
10084{_}3951{_}And yet he did not snarl at McGill, or snap at his hands when they came within reach.
10084{_}3952{_}Quite frequently Sandy McTrigger came over to the little cabin where McGill was staying, and three times Kazan leaped at the end of his chain to get at him, and his white fangs gleamed as long as Sandy was in sight.
10084{_}3953{_}Alone with McGill he became quiet.
10084{_}3954{_}Something told him that McGill had come as a friend that night when he and the big Dane stood shoulder to shoulder in the cage that had been built for a slaughter pen.
10084{_}3955{_}Away down in his brute heart he held McGill apart from other men.
10084{_}3956{_}He had no desire to harm him.
10084{_}3957{_}He tolerated him, but showed none of the growing affection of the huge Dane.
10084{_}3958{_}It was this fact that puzzled McGill.
10084{_}3959{_}He had never before known a dog that he could not make love him.
10084{_}3960{_}To-day he placed the tallow and bran before Kazan, and the smile in his face gave way to a look of perplexity.
10084{_}3961{_}Kazan's lips had drawn suddenly back.
10084{_}3962{_}A fierce snarl rolled deep in his throat.
10084{_}3963{_}The hair along his spine stood up.
10084{_}3964{_}His muscles twitched.
10084{_}3965{_}Instinctively the professor turned.
10084{_}3966{_}Sandy McTrigger had come up quietly behind him.
10084{_}3967{_}His brutal face wore a grin as he looked at Kazan.
10084{_}3968{_}"It's a fool job--tryin' to make friends with _him_" he said.
10084{_}3969{_}Then he added, with a sudden interested gleam in his eyes, "When you startin'?"
10084{_}3970{_}"With first frost," replied McGill.
10084{_}3971{_}"It ought to come soon.
10084{_}3972{_}I'm going to join Sergeant Conroy and his party at Fond du Lac by the first of October."
10084{_}3973{_}"And you're going up to Fond du Lac--alone?"
10084{_}3974{_}queried Sandy.
10084{_}3975{_}"Why don't you take a man?"
10084{_}3976{_}The little professor laughed softly.
10084{_}3977{_}"Why?"
10084{_}3978{_}he asked.
10084{_}3979{_}"I've been through the Athabasca waterways a dozen times, and know the trail as well as I know Broadway.
10084{_}3980{_}Besides, I like to be alone.
10084{_}3981{_}And the work isn't too hard, with the currents all flowing to the north and east."
10084{_}3982{_}Sandy was looking at the Dane, with his back to McGill.
10084{_}3983{_}An exultant gleam shot for an instant into his eyes.
10084{_}3984{_}"You're taking the dogs?"
10084{_}3985{_}"Yes."
10084{_}3986{_}Sandy lighted his pipe, and spoke like one strangely curious.
10084{_}3987{_}"Must cost a heap to take these trips o' yourn, don't it?"
10084{_}3988{_}"My last cost about seven thousand dollars.
10084{_}3989{_}This will cost five," said McGill.
10084{_}3990{_}"Gawd!"
10084{_}3991{_}breathed Sandy.
10084{_}3992{_}"An' you carry all that along with you!
10084{_}3993{_}Ain't you afraid--something might happen--?"
10084{_}3994{_}The little professor was looking the other way now.
10084{_}3995{_}The carelessness in his face and manner changed.
10084{_}3996{_}His blue eyes grew a shade darker.
10084{_}3997{_}A hard smile which Sandy did not see hovered about his lips for an instant.
10084{_}3998{_}Then he turned, laughing.
10084{_}3999{_}"I'm a very light sleeper," he said.
10084{_}4000{_}"A footstep at night rouses me.
10084{_}4001{_}Even a man's breathing awakes me, when I make up my mind that I must be on my guard.
10084{_}4002{_}And, besides"--he drew from his pocket a blue-steeled Savage automatic--"I know how to use _this_."
10084{_}4003{_}He pointed to a knot in the wall of the cabin.
10084{_}4004{_}"Observe," he said.
10084{_}4005{_}Five times he fired at twenty paces, and when Sandy went up to look at the knot he gave a gasp.
10084{_}4006{_}There was one jagged hole where the knot had been.
10084{_}4007{_}"Pretty good," he grinned.
10084{_}4008{_}"Most men couldn't do better'n that with a rifle."
10084{_}4009{_}When Sandy left, McGill followed him with a suspicious gleam in his eyes, and a curious smile on his lips.
10084{_}4010{_}Then he turned to Kazan.
10084{_}4011{_}"Guess you've got him figgered out about right, old man," he laughed softly.
10084{_}4012{_}"I don't blame you very much for wanting to get him by the throat.
10084{_}4013{_}Perhaps--"  He shoved his hands deep in his pockets, and went into the cabin.
10084{_}4014{_}Kazan dropped his head between his forepaws, and lay still, with wide-open eyes.
10084{_}4015{_}It was late afternoon, early in September, and each night brought now the first chill breaths of autumn.
10084{_}4016{_}Kazan watched the last glow of the sun as it faded out of the southern skies.
10084{_}4017{_}Darkness always followed swiftly after that, and with darkness came more fiercely his wild longing for freedom.
10084{_}4018{_}Night after night he had gnawed at his steel chain.
10084{_}4019{_}Night after night he had watched the stars, and the moon, and had listened for Gray Wolf's call, while the big Dane lay sleeping.
10084{_}4020{_}To-night it was colder than usual, and the keen tang of the wind that came fresh from the west stirred him strangely.
10084{_}4021{_}It set his blood afire with what the Indians call the Frost Hunger.
10084{_}4022{_}Lethargic summer was gone and the days and nights of hunting were at hand.
10084{_}4023{_}He wanted to leap out into freedom and run until he was exhausted, with Gray Wolf at his side.
10084{_}4024{_}He knew that Gray Wolf was off there--where the stars hung low in the clear sky, and that she was waiting.
10084{_}4025{_}He strained at the end of his chain, and whined.
10084{_}4026{_}All that night he was restless--more restless than he had been at any time before.
10084{_}4027{_}Once, in the far distance, he heard a cry that he thought was the cry of Gray Wolf, and his answer roused McGill from deep sleep.
10084{_}4028{_}It was dawn, and the little professor dressed himself and came out of the cabin.
10084{_}4029{_}With satisfaction he noted the exhilarating snap in the air.
10084{_}4030{_}He wet his fingers and held them above his head, chuckling when he found the wind had swung into the north.
10084{_}4031{_}He went to Kazan, and talked to him.
10084{_}4032{_}Among other things he said, "This'll put the black flies to sleep, Kazan.
10084{_}4033{_}A day or two more of it and we'll start."
10084{_}4034{_}Five days later McGill led first the Dane, and then Kazan, to a packed canoe.
10084{_}4035{_}Sandy McTrigger saw them off, and Kazan watched for a chance to leap at him.
10084{_}4036{_}Sandy kept his distance, and McGill watched the two with a thought that set the blood running swiftly behind the mask of his careless smile.
10084{_}4037{_}They had slipped a mile down-stream when he leaned over and laid a fearless hand on Kazan's head.
10084{_}4038{_}Something in the touch of that hand, and in the professor's voice, kept Kazan from a desire to snap at him.
10084{_}4039{_}He tolerated the friendship with expressionless eyes and a motionless body.
10084{_}4040{_}"I was beginning to fear I wouldn't have much sleep, old boy," chuckled McGill ambiguously, "but I guess I can take a nap now and then with _you_ along!"
10084{_}4041{_}He made camp that night fifteen miles up the lake shore.
10084{_}4042{_}The big Dane he fastened to a sapling twenty yards from his small silk tent, but Kazan's chain he made fast to the butt of a stunted birch that held down the tent-flap.
10084{_}4043{_}Before he went into the tent for the night McGill pulled out his automatic and examined it with care.
10084{_}4044{_}For three days the journey continued without a mishap along the shore of Lake Athabasca.
10084{_}4045{_}On the fourth night McGill pitched his tent in a clump of _banskian_ pine a hundred yards back from the water.
10084{_}4046{_}All that day the wind had come steadily from behind them, and for at least a half of the day the professor had been watching Kazan closely.
10084{_}4047{_}From the west there had now and then come a scent that stirred him uneasily.
10084{_}4048{_}Since noon he had sniffed that wind.
10084{_}4049{_}Twice McGill had heard him growling deep in his throat, and once, when the scent had come stronger than usual, he had bared his fangs, and the bristles stood up along his spine.
10084{_}4050{_}For an hour after striking camp the little professor did not build a fire, but sat looking up the shore of the lake through his hunting glass.
10084{_}4051{_}It was dusk when he returned to where he had put up his tent and chained the dogs.
10084{_}4052{_}For a few moments he stood unobserved, looking at the wolf-dog.
10084{_}4053{_}Kazan was still uneasy.
10084{_}4054{_}He lay _facing_ the west.
10084{_}4055{_}McGill made note of this, for the big Dane lay behind Kazan--to the east.
10084{_}4056{_}Under ordinary conditions Kazan would have faced him.
10084{_}4057{_}He was sure now that there was something in the west wind.
10084{_}4058{_}A little shiver ran up his back as he thought of what it might be.
10084{_}4059{_}Behind a rock he built a very small fire, and prepared supper.
10084{_}4060{_}After this he went into the tent, and when he came out he carried a blanket under his arm.
10084{_}4061{_}He chuckled as he stood for a moment over Kazan.
10084{_}4062{_}"We're not going to sleep in there to-night, old hoy," he said.
10084{_}4063{_}"I don't like what you've found in the west wind.
10084{_}4064{_}It may he a--_thunder-storm!_" He laughed at his joke, and buried himself in a clump of stunted _banskians_ thirty paces from the tent.
10084{_}4065{_}Here he rolled himself in his blanket, and went to sleep.
10084{_}4066{_}It was a quiet starlit night, and hours afterward Kazan dropped his nose between his forepaws and drowsed.
10084{_}4067{_}It was the snap of a twig that roused him.
10084{_}4068{_}The sound did not awaken the sluggish Dane but instantly Kazan's head was alert, his keen nostrils sniffing the air.
10084{_}4069{_}What he had smelled all day was heavy about him now.
10084{_}4070{_}He lay still and quivering.
10084{_}4071{_}Slowly, from out of the _banskians_ behind the tent, there came a figure.
10084{_}4072{_}It was not the little professor.
10084{_}4073{_}It approached cautiously, with lowered head and hunched shoulders, and the starlight revealed the murderous face of Sandy McTrigger.
10084{_}4074{_}Kazan crouched low.
10084{_}4075{_}He laid his head flat between his forepaws.
10084{_}4076{_}His long fangs gleamed.
10084{_}4077{_}But he made no sound that betrayed his concealment under a thick _banskian_ shrub.
10084{_}4078{_}Step by step Sandy approached, and at last he reached the flap of the tent.
10084{_}4079{_}He did not carry a club or a whip in his hand now.
10084{_}4080{_}In the place of either of those was the glitter of steel.
10084{_}4081{_}At the door to the tent he paused, and peered in, his back to Kazan.
10084{_}4082{_}Silently, swiftly--the wolf now in every movement, Kazan came to his feet.
10084{_}4083{_}He forgot the chain that held him.
10084{_}4084{_}Ten feet away stood the enemy he hated above all others he had ever known.
10084{_}4085{_}Every ounce of strength in his splendid body gathered itself for the spring.
10084{_}4086{_}And then he leaped.
10084{_}4087{_}This time the chain did not pull him back, almost neck-broken.
10084{_}4088{_}Age and the elements had weakened the leather collar he had worn since the days of his slavery in the traces, and it gave way with a snap.
10084{_}4089{_}Sandy turned, and in a second leap Kazan's fangs sank into the flesh of his arm.
10084{_}4090{_}With a startled cry the man fell, and as they rolled over on the ground the big Dane's deep voice rolled out in thunderous alarm as he tugged at his leash.
10084{_}4091{_}In the fall Kazan's hold was broken.
10084{_}4092{_}In an instant he was on his feet, ready for another attack.
10084{_}4093{_}And then the change came.
10084{_}4094{_}He was _free_.
10084{_}4095{_}The collar was gone from his neck.
10084{_}4096{_}The forest, the stars, the whispering wind were all about him.
10084{_}4097{_}_Here_ were men, and off there was--Gray Wolf!
10084{_}4098{_}His ears dropped, and he turned swiftly, and slipped like a shadow back into the glorious freedom of his world.
10084{_}4099{_}A hundred yards away something stopped him for an instant.
10084{_}4100{_}It was not the big Dane's voice, but the sharp _crack--crack--crack_, of the little professor's automatic.
10084{_}4101{_}And above that sound there rose the voice of Sandy McTrigger in a weird and terrible cry.
